Episode highlight
本期亮点

DHH
(00:00:00) No one anywhere who’s serious believes that cookie banners does anything good for anyone, yet we’ve been unable to get rid of it. This is the thing that really gets me about cookie banners too. It’s not just the EU, it’s the entire world. You can’t hide from cookie banners anywhere on this planet. If you go to goddamn Mars on one of Elon’s rockets and you try to access a web page, you’ll still see a cookie banner. No one in the universe is safe from this nonsense.
(00:00:00) 世界上任何一个认真的人，都不相信 Cookie 弹窗对任何人有任何好处，但我们却始终无法摆脱它。真正让我抓狂的是，这不仅仅是欧盟的问题，而是整个世界都如此。在这个星球上，你根本无处可逃。哪怕你坐着埃隆的火箭去了该死的火星，只要你尝试访问一个网页，还是会看到 Cookie 弹窗。宇宙中没人能躲开这种荒谬。

(00:00:26) It sometimes feels like we’re barely better off. Web pages aren’t that different from what they were in the late ’90s, early 2000s. They’re still just forms. They still just write to databases. A lot of people, I think, are very uncomfortable with the fact that they are essentially crud monkeys. They just make systems that create, read, update, or delete rows in a database and they have to compensate for that existential dread by over complicating things. That’s a huge part of the satisfaction of driving a race car is driving in at the edge of adhesion, as we call it, where you’re essentially just a tiny movement away from spinning out. Doesn’t take much. Then the car starts rotating. Once it starts rotating, you lose grip and you’re going for the wall. That balance of danger and skill is what’s so intoxicating.
(00:00:26) 有时候感觉我们几乎没什么进步。如今的网页和 90 年代末、21 世纪初相比并没有太大不同——它们仍然只是表单，仍然只是向数据库写入数据。我认为很多人其实非常不安于这样一个事实：他们本质上就是 CRUD 猴子（指只会做增删改查操作的程序员）。他们构建的系统不过是创建、读取、更新或删除数据库中的记录，为了缓解这种存在主义式的焦虑，他们只能通过把事情过度复杂化来补偿自己。驾驶赛车的一大满足感，就在于行驶在抓地力的极限边缘——我们称之为“附着极限”。在这种状态下，你离失控打滑只差一点点微小的动作。不需要太多，车子一旦开始旋转，就会失去抓地力，直冲墙壁而去。正是这种危险与技巧之间的平衡，才如此令人着迷。

Introduction

介绍

Lex Fridman
(00:01:21) The following is a conversation with David Heinemeyer Hansen, also known as DHH. He is a legend in the programming and tech world, brilliant and insightful, sometimes controversial, and always fun to talk to. He’s the creator of Ruby on Rails, which is an influential web development framework behind many websites used by millions of people, including Shopify, GitHub, and Airbnb. He is the co-owner and CTO of 37signals that created Basecamp, HEY, and ONCE.
(00:01:21) 接下来是对大卫·海涅迈尔·汉森（David Heinemeier Hansson），也就是大家熟知的 DHH 的一次对话。他是编程和技术领域的传奇人物，聪明而富有洞察力，有时颇具争议，但总是充满趣味。他是 Ruby on Rails 的创造者——这是一个极具影响力的 Web 开发框架，支撑着数以百万用户使用的众多网站，包括 Shopify、GitHub 和 Airbnb。他还是 37signals 公司的联合创始人兼首席技术官，该公司开发了 Basecamp、HEY 和 ONCE。

(00:01:57) He is a New York Times best-selling author together with his co-author, Jason Fried, of four books, Rework, Remote, Getting Real, and It Doesn’t Have To Be Crazy At Work. And on top of that, he’s also a race car driver, including being a class winner at the legendary twenty-four-hour Le Mans race. This is the Lex Fridman podcast. To support it, please check out our sponsors in the description and consider subscribing to this channel. And now, dear friends, here’s DHH.
(00:01:57) 他与合著者杰森·弗里德（Jason Fried）共同撰写了四本《纽约时报》畅销书：《重来》（Rework）、《远程》（Remote）、《务实》（Getting Real）以及《工作不必疯狂》（It Doesn’t Have To Be Crazy At Work）。除此之外，他还是一名赛车手，曾在传奇的勒芒 24 小时耐力赛中赢得组别冠军。这里是 Lex Fridman 播客。若想支持本节目，请查看简介中的赞助商信息，并考虑订阅本频道。现在，亲爱的朋友们，有请 DHH。

Programming

编程

Lex Fridman
(00:02:32) For someone who became a legendary programmer, you officially got into programming late in life, and I guess that’s because you tried to learn how to program a few times and you failed. So can you tell me the full story, the saga of your failures to learn programming? Was Commodore 64 involved?
(00:02:32) 对于一位后来成为传奇程序员的人来说，你正式接触编程其实很晚，我猜这是因为你曾多次尝试学习编程但都失败了。你能跟我讲讲完整的故事吗？那段你屡次学编程却屡屡失败的“史诗”？是不是还涉及 Commodore 64？
(00:02:53) Commodore 64 was the inspiration. I really wanted a Commodore 64. That was the first computer I ever sat down in front. And the way I sat down in front of it was I was five years old and there was this one kid on my street who had a Commodore 64. No one else had a computer, so we were all the kids just getting over there and we were all playing Yie Ar Kung-Fu. I don’t know if you’ve ever seen that game. It was one of the original fighting games. It’s really a great game and I was playing that for the first time at five years old, and we were like seven kids sitting up in this one kid’s bedroom all taking our turn to play the game. And I just found that unbelievably interesting. And I begged and I begged and I begged my dad, “Could I get a computer?” And he finally comes home. He’s like, “I got you a computer.” I was like, yes, my own Commodore 64. And he pulls out this black, green and blue keyboard that’s an Amstrad 464. I was like, “Dad, what’s this?”

(00:02:53) Commodore 64 是灵感来源。我真的很想要一台 Commodore 64。那是我人生中第一次坐在电脑前。当时我才五岁，街上有个小孩有一台 Commodore 64。其他人都没有电脑，所以我们一群孩子都跑到他家，一起玩《Yie Ar Kung-Fu》。不知道你有没有玩过那款游戏？它是最早的格斗游戏之一，真的非常棒。我五岁时第一次玩它，大概七个孩子挤在那个孩子的卧室里，轮流玩游戏。我觉得这简直不可思议地有趣。于是我一遍又一遍地恳求爸爸：“我能买一台电脑吗？”他终于有一天回家说：“我给你弄了台电脑。”我当时心想：太好了，我自己的 Commodore 64！结果他拿出一个黑、绿、蓝配色的键盘——是一台 Amstrad 464。我问：“爸，这是什么？”

Lex Fridman
(00:03:53) The disappointment.
Lex Fridman
(00:03:53) 太失望了。

DHH
(00:03:54) This is not a Commodore 64. But it was a computer. So I got my first computer at essentially six years old, that Amstrad 464. And of course, the first thing I wanted to do, I wanted to play video games. And I think the computer, which he by the way had traded for a TV and a stereo recorder or something like that, came with two games. One was this Frogger game where you had to escape from underground. It was actually kind of dark, like this frog, you’re trying to get it out from underground. I was pretty bad at it. And I only had those two games and then I wanted more games. And one way to get more games when you’re a kid who doesn’t have a lot of money and can’t just buy a bunch of games is to type them in yourself. Back in ’84, ’85, magazines would literally print source code at the back of their magazines and you could just sit and type it in.

DHH
(00:03:54) 这根本不是 Commodore 64。但它确实是一台电脑。所以我大约六岁时拥有了人生第一台电脑——那台 Amstrad 464。当然，我最想做的事就是玩电子游戏。顺便提一句，我爸是用一台电视和一台立体声录音机之类的东西换来的这台电脑，它附带了两个游戏。其中一个是一款《Frogger》（青蛙过河）类游戏，你需要让一只青蛙从地下逃出来。画面其实挺阴暗的，就是操控这只青蛙从地下逃出去。我玩得特别差。我只有这两个游戏，但我想玩更多。对于一个没钱、买不起一堆游戏的小孩来说，获得新游戏的一种方式就是自己手动输入。在 1984、1985 年那会儿，杂志真的会在最后几页直接刊登源代码，你只要坐下来一个字一个字敲进去就行。

(00:04:46) So I tried to do that and it would take like two hours to print this game into the Amstrad, and of course I’d make some spelling mistake along the way and something wouldn’t work and the whole thing… I wasn’t that good of English, I was born in Denmark. So I was really trying to get into it because I wanted all these games and I didn’t have the money to buy them. And I tried quite hard for quite a while to get into it, but it just never clicked. And then I discovered the magic of piracy, and after that I basically just took some time off from learning to program because well now suddenly I had access to all sorts of games. So that was the first attempt around six, seven years old. And what’s funny is I remember these fragments. I remember not understanding the purpose of a variable.

(00:04:46) 所以我尝试这么做，把一个游戏输入到 Amstrad 里大概要花两个小时。当然，过程中我总会打错几个拼写，导致程序出错，整个东西就……我的英语也不太好，毕竟我出生在丹麦。我真的非常努力地想学会，因为我想要那些游戏，又没钱买。我坚持尝试了相当长一段时间，但始终没能真正入门。后来，我发现了盗版的“魔力”——从此之后，我基本上就暂时放弃了学编程，因为突然之间我能玩到各种各样的游戏了。这是我大约六七岁时的第一次尝试。有趣的是，我还记得一些片段，比如当时完全不理解变量的作用。

(00:05:34) If there’s a thing and you assign something, why would you assign another thing to it? So for some reason, I understood constants. Constants made sense to me, but variables didn’t. Then maybe I’m 11 to 12, I’ve gotten into the Amiga at this point. The Amiga, by the way, still perhaps my favorite computer of all time. I mean, this is one of those things where people get older and they’re like, oh, the music from the ’80s was amazing. To me, even as someone who loves computers and love new computers, the Amiga was this magical machine that was made by the same company that produced the Commodore 64 and I got the Amiga 500 I think in ’87.

(00:05:34) 如果有一个东西，你给它赋了一个值，为什么还要再给它赋另一个值呢？不知为何，我那时能理解常量——常量对我来说很合理，但变量却不行。后来大概十一二岁时，我开始接触 Amiga 电脑。顺便说一句，Amiga 至今可能仍是我有史以来最喜欢的电脑。就像有些人年纪大了会说“80 年代的音乐真棒”一样，即使我热爱电脑、也喜欢新电脑，Amiga 对我而言仍是一台神奇的机器——它和 Commodore 64 是同一家公司生产的。我记得我是在 1987 年得到了一台 Amiga 500。

Lex Fridman
(00:06:16) Look at this sexy thing. That is a sexy machine right there.
Lex Fridman
(00:06:16) 看看这个性感的家伙。这可真是一台性感的机器。

DHH
(00:06:19) This is from an age by the way where computing wasn’t global in the same sense, that different territories had different computers that were popular. The Amiga was really popular in Europe, but it wasn’t very popular at all in the US as far as I understand. It wasn’t popular in Japan. There were just different machines. The Apple II was a big thing in the US. I’d never even heard of Apple in the ’80s in Copenhagen. But the Amiga 500 was the machine that brought me to want to try it again. And do you know what’s funny? The reason I wanted to try it again was I remembered the first time I tried to learn and then there was this programming language that was literally called EasyAMOS, like the easy version of AMOS. I’m like, if it’s easy AMOS, how hard can it be? I’ve got to be able to figure this out.

DHH
(00:06:19) 那个年代，计算机世界还没像今天这样全球化，不同地区流行的电脑各不相同。据我所知，Amiga 在欧洲非常流行，但在美国几乎没人用，在日本也不流行。各地用的都是不同的机器。Apple II 在美国很火，但我在 80 年代的哥本哈根甚至都没听说过苹果。而 Amiga 500 正是让我重新燃起学习编程欲望的那台机器。你知道有趣的是什么吗？我想再试一次的原因是，我记得自己第一次尝试学习编程的经历，后来我发现了一种编程语言，名字就叫 EasyAMOS——也就是 AMOS 的简易版。我当时就想：如果叫“简易 AMOS”，那能有多难？我肯定能搞明白。

(00:07:04) And this time I tried harder. I got into conditionals, I got into loops, I got into all these things and still, I couldn’t do it. And on the second attempt, I really got to the point of maybe I’m not smart enough. Maybe it’s too much math. I like math in this sort of superficial way. I don’t like it in the deep way that some of my perhaps slightly nerdier friends did, who I had tremendous respect for, but I’m not that person. I’m not the math geek who’s going to figure it all out. So after that attempt with EasyAMOS and failing to even get… I don’t even think I completed one even very basic game. I thought, programming’s just not for me. I’m going to have to do something else. I still love computers. I still love video games.

(00:07:04) 这次我更努力了。我学了条件语句、循环，接触了所有这些概念，但还是搞不定。第二次尝试时，我真的开始怀疑：也许我不够聪明，也许涉及太多数学了。我对数学的喜爱是比较表面的，不像我那些稍微更“极客”一点的朋友那样深入钻研——我对他们非常敬佩，但我不是那种人。我不是那种能靠数学天赋把一切都搞明白的书呆子。所以那次用 EasyAMOS 尝试后，我甚至连一个非常基础的游戏都没完成。我心想：编程可能真的不适合我，我得去做点别的。但我依然热爱电脑，依然热爱电子游戏。

(00:07:53) I actually at that time had already begun making friends with people who knew how to program, who weren’t even programming EasyAMOS, they were programming with freaking Assembly. And I would sit down and just go, the moves and the memories and the copies, how do you even do this? I don’t even understand how you go from this to Amiga demos for example. That was the big thing with the Amiga. It had this wonderful demo scene in Europe. It’s this really interesting period of time in the Amiga’s history where you had all these programmers spread out mostly all over Europe who would compete on graphic competitions where you could probably bring one of these different-

(00:07:53) 实际上，那时候我已经开始结交一些会编程的朋友，他们甚至都不用 EasyAMOS，而是直接用汇编语言（Assembly）编程。我会坐在旁边看着，心里想：这些移动操作、内存操作、复制指令，你们到底是怎么做到的？我完全无法理解，比如，你们是怎么从这些代码做出 Amiga 演示程序（demos）的？这正是 Amiga 最厉害的地方——它在欧洲拥有一个精彩的演示场景（demo scene）。这是 Amiga 历史上一段非常有趣的时期：大量程序员遍布欧洲各地，参加图形竞赛，你可能会带来其中某一种……

Lex Fridman
(00:08:34) On that thing?
Lex Fridman
(00:08:34) 用那台机器？

DHH
(00:08:36) On this thing. They would make these little almost like music videos, combining some MIDI music, combining some cool graphics, and they would do all of it in like 4K. Four kilobytes that is. Not four Ks of resolution. Four kilobytes of memory. And I just thought that was such a cool scene. This was obviously pre-internet. It was even pre-BBS, bulletin board systems, to some extent. It was you swap your demo software with someone else by sending them a disk in the mail, like the 3.5s. And I was enamored with that whole scene. I was enamored with what they were able to create and I just wanted to be a part of it even though I kind of didn’t have any skills to contribute. And that’s how I got into running BBSs.

DHH
(00:08:36) 就是用这台机器。他们会制作一些类似音乐视频的小作品，结合 MIDI 音乐和酷炫的图形，而且整个作品只用大约 4K——指的是 4 千字节，不是 4K 分辨率，而是 4KB 的内存。我觉得那场景太酷了。那显然是互联网出现之前的时代，甚至在某种程度上连 BBS（电子公告板系统）都还没普及。那时候你要和别人交换演示程序，就得把软盘（比如 3.5 英寸盘）寄过去。我深深迷上了那个圈子，迷上了他们能创造出的东西，尽管我自己没什么技能可以贡献，但我就是想成为其中一员。也正是这样，我开始运营 BBS。

(00:09:22) I didn’t learn programming then and I wouldn’t learn programming until much later, until I was almost 20 years old. The bulletin board systems existed in this funny space where they were partly a service to the demo scenes allowing all these demo groups to distribute their amazing demos. And then it was also a place to trade piracy software, pirated software. And I ended up starting one of those when I was 14 years old in my tiny little bedroom in Copenhagen. I had my, at that point, Amiga 4000. I had three telephone lines coming in to my tiny room.

(00:09:22) 那时候我并没有学会编程，直到很久以后——差不多快 20 岁时才真正开始学。电子公告板系统（BBS）存在于一个奇特的空间：一方面，它为演示场景提供服务，让各个演示小组能分发他们惊艳的作品；另一方面，它也是交换盗版软件的地方。我在 14 岁时就在哥本哈根自己狭小的卧室里搭建了一个这样的 BBS。那时我用的是 Amiga 4000，房间里接进了三条电话线。

Lex Fridman
(00:09:59) Nice.
Lex Fridman
(00:09:59) 真酷。

DHH
(00:10:00) Which is funny because again, I’m 14 years old. By the time I was installing my third line, you had to get someone from the telephone company to come do it. I get this guy and he’s just looking around, like what is this? Why the hell is a 14 year old having three phone lines into their tiny little bedroom? What’s going on here? Why are all these modems blinking red and black and making funny sounds?

DHH
(00:10:00) 这挺搞笑的，毕竟我才 14 岁。装第三条电话线时，必须请电话公司的人上门安装。来了个师傅，他环顾四周，一脸困惑：“这到底是什么情况？一个 14 岁的孩子，为什么要在这么小的卧室里装三条电话线？这里发生了什么？为什么这些调制解调器一直在闪红灯和黑灯，还发出奇怪的声音？”

Lex Fridman
(00:10:23) Did your parents know?
Lex Fridman
(00:10:23) 你父母知道吗？

DHH

(00:14:23) All the other experiences I had with programming was I’d spend hours typing something in, I click run and it wouldn’t work, and I’d get an error message that made no sense to me as a kid either at six or seven or at 12. (00:14:23) 我之前所有的编程经历都是这样的：花好几个小时敲代码，一点“运行”，结果却不行，然后弹出一条错误信息，不管是六七岁还是十二岁时的我，都完全看不懂。而现在，我坐在一台连着互联网的电脑前，让文字闪烁、变大，把它变成H1或H2标题。就在那里，我们做了大概一个半小时，突然我意识到：“哦，我可以做些东西放到网上，让德国的人也能访问看到，而且还不用征求任何人许可？” 这太酷了！我一定要多做点这样的事。于是我就一头扎进了互联网，开始使用HTML。我仍然和那些来自演示派对的朋友保持联系，并开始和他们一起创建游戏网站。

(00:15:11) I’d rather buy the video games, I’d review them. This was another good way of getting new video games was to walk down to some store and say like, hey, I’m a journalist. (00:15:11) 我更愿意去买游戏，然后写评测。另一种获取新游戏的好办法就是走到商店里，对他们说：“嘿，我是记者。” 我当时就是个十五岁的孩子，他们看着我：“你是记者？”“对啊，我能借几款游戏吗？” 因为那时候游戏已经转向PlayStation和其他平台了，盗版不像以前那么容易，至少一开始不是。所以我就这样去做了，这也开启了我在互联网上的旅程。我开始做这些游戏网站，和程序员合作，慢慢意识到自己也能做点事情——我可以负责HTML部分。

(00:15:44) It’s not really programming, but it kind of smells like it. You’re talking to a computer, you’re making it put text on the screen and you’re communicating with someone halfway around the world. So that became my pathway back into programming, and then slowly I picked up more and more of it. First website I did with someone, one of these programmers from the demo scene that was dynamic was asp.net. It wasn’t even actually called .net. That was what we started on, and then we moved on to PHP and PHP was when I finally got it, when it finally clicked, when conditionals and loops and variables and all of that stuff started to make sense enough to me that I thought, I can do this.
(00:15:44) 虽然严格来说那不算编程，但感觉很像。你在跟电脑对话，让它在屏幕上显示文字，同时与地球另一端的人交流。这成了我重返编程之路的入口，之后我慢慢地掌握了越来越多。我和一位来自演示场景的程序员合作做的第一个动态网站是用ASP.NET做的——其实那时候还不叫“.NET”。那是我们的起点，后来我们转向了PHP。正是在PHP这里，我才真正开窍了：条件语句、循环、变量这些东西终于对我有了意义，让我觉得：“我可以做到。”

Lex Fridman
(00:16:26) So would it be fair to say that we wouldn’t have DHH without PHP and therefore you owe all of your success to PHP?
Lex Fridman
(00:16:26) 那么这么说是否公平：如果没有PHP，就不会有今天的DHH，因此你所有的成功都要归功于PHP？

DHH
(00:16:33) A hundred percent, that’s true. And it’s even better than that because PHP to me didn’t just give me a start in terms of making my own web applications. It actually gave me a bar. In many ways I think the pinnacle of web developer ergonomics is late ’90s PHP. You write this script, you FTP it to a server and instantly it’s deployed. Instantly it’s available. You change anything in that file and you reload, boom, it’s right there. There’s no web servers, there’s no setup. There’s just an Apache that runs mod PHP, and it was essentially the easiest way to get a dynamic web page up and going, and this is one of the things I’ve been chasing that high for basically the rest of my career. It was so easy to make things for the internet in the mid to late ’90s.
DHH
(00:16:33) 百分之百正确。而且还不止如此，因为PHP给我的不仅仅是开发自己Web应用的起点，它还给我树立了一个标杆。在很多方面，我认为Web开发者人机工程学的巅峰就是90年代末的PHP。你写个脚本，用FTP传到服务器上，立刻就部署好了，马上就能访问。你改文件里的任何内容，刷新一下，boom，立刻生效。不需要配置Web服务器，不需要复杂设置，只需要一个运行mod_php模块的Apache就行。这基本上是搭建动态网页最简单的方式。而这种“爽快感”，几乎成了我整个职业生涯都在追寻的目标。在90年代中期到晚期，为互联网做东西就是这么简单。

(00:17:26) How did we lose the sensibilities that allowed us to not just work this way but get new people into the industry to give them those success experiences that I had adding a freaking blink tag to an HTML page, FTPing a PHP page to an Apache web server without knowing really anything about anything? Without knowing anything about frameworks, without knowing anything about setup. All of that stuff have really taken us to a place where it sometimes feels like we’re barely better off. Web pages aren’t that different from what they were in the late ’90s, early 2000s. They’re still just forms. They still just write to databases.
(00:17:26) 我们是怎么失去那种直觉的？那种不仅让我们能这样工作，还能吸引新人入行、让他们获得像我当年那样成功体验的直觉——比如往HTML页面加个该死的闪烁标签，或者把一个PHP页面FTP上传到Apache服务器上，而根本不需要懂任何框架、任何配置。如今所有这些复杂的工具反而把我们带到了一个有时让人觉得几乎毫无进步的地步。今天的网页跟90年代末、2000年代初相比并没有本质不同——它们仍然是表单，仍然只是向数据库写入数据。

(00:18:06) A lot of people, I think are very uncomfortable with the fact that they are essentially crud monkeys. They just make systems that create, read, update or delete rows in a database, and they have to compensate for that existential dread by over-complicating things. Now, that’s a bit of a character. There’s more to it and there’s things you can learn for more sophisticated ways of thinking about this, but there’s still an ideal here, which is why I was so happy you had Pieter Levels on because he still basically works like this. And I look at that and go, man, that’s amazing.
(00:18:06) 我认为很多人其实很不舒服地意识到，自己本质上就是“CRUD猴子”（指只会做增删改查操作的程序员）。他们做的系统无非就是在数据库里增、查、改、删记录，于是只好通过过度复杂化来缓解这种存在主义式的焦虑。当然，这种说法有点刻薄了。实际情况更复杂，也确实有更高级的思维方式可以学习。但这里仍有一个理想状态存在——这也是为什么我很高兴你邀请了Pieter Levels，因为他至今基本上还是这样工作的。我看他这么做，心里就想：“伙计，这太棒了。”

Lex Fridman
(00:18:39) Yeah, you’re chasing that high. He’s been high all along.
Lex Fridman
(00:18:39) 是啊，你在追逐那种“高潮感”，而他一直就处在那种状态中。

DHH
(00:18:42) Yes.
DHH
(00:18:42) 没错。

Lex Fridman

Lex Fridman
(00:18:43) 用的是PHP、jQuery和SQLite。
(00:18:47) I think it’s amazing because he’s proving that this isn’t just a nostalgic dream. He’s actually doing it. He’s running all these businesses. Now, some of that is, as he would admit up first upfront, is that he’s just one guy. And you could do different things when you’re just one guy. When you’re working in a team, when I started working on a team, when I started working with Jason Fried on Basecamp, we at first didn’t use version control together.
(00:18:47) 我觉得这很了不起，因为他正在证明这不仅仅是一种怀旧的幻想。他真的在做这件事——运营着所有这些业务。当然，正如他自己会首先坦承的那样，其中一部分原因在于他只是一个人。当你只是一个人的时候，你可以做很多不同的事情。但当你开始团队协作时——比如我刚开始和 Jason Fried 一起开发 Basecamp 的时候——我们最初并没有一起使用版本控制系统。

(00:19:16) I used version control for myself, and then I thought, do you know what? Designers, they’re probably not smart enough to figure out CBS and therefore I was just like, no, no, no, you just FTP it up. You just FTP it. They knew how to do FTP. And then after the third time I had overwritten their changes I was like, goddamn it, I guess I’ve got to teach Jason CBS to not do that again. But I think there’s still way more truth to the fact that we can work the way we did in the ’90s, work the way Pieter works today even in the team context, and that we’ve been far too willing to hand over far too much of our developer ergonomics to the merchants of complexity.
(00:19:16) 我自己用版本控制，然后我想：你知道吗？设计师们可能不够聪明，搞不懂 CVS（注：原文为CBS，应为CVS之误），所以我当时就想：“不不不，你们直接用 FTP 上传就行，就用 FTP。”因为他们知道怎么用 FTP。结果在我第三次不小心覆盖了他们的修改之后，我才意识到：“该死，看来我得教 Jason 用 CVS，不能再这样了。”但我认为更关键的一点是：即使在团队协作的环境下，我们依然完全可以像 90 年代那样工作，或者像 Pieter 现在这样工作；而我们却过于轻易地把太多开发者体验（developer ergonomics）拱手让给了那些“复杂性商人”。

JavaScript

Lex Fridman
Lex Fridman

(00:19:57) And you’ve been chasing that with Rails 8. So how do you bring all the cool features of a modern framework and make it no build, make it as easy to create something and to ship it as it was in the ’90s with just PHP? It’s very difficult for me to beat the Pieter Levels approach of just… It’s so easy to just ship some PHP.
(00:19:57) 而你一直在通过 Rails 8 追求这个目标。那么，你如何把现代框架的所有酷炫功能整合进来，同时做到“无需构建”（no build），让创建和部署应用变得像 90 年代只用 PHP 那样简单？对我来说，Pieter Levels 的那种方式真的很难超越……用 PHP 直接部署实在太简单了。

DHH
DHH

(00:20:21) And it should be. Why should it be harder than that? Our computers today are almost infinitely faster than what they were in the ’90s. So shouldn’t we be able to work in even easier ways? We should be looking back on the ’90s and go, oh, that was way too complicated. Now we have more sophisticated technology that’s way faster and it allows us to work in these easier to use ways. But that’s not true. But now you can see the line I draw in my work with Ruby on Rails, and especially with Rails 8. No build to me is reaching back to that ’90s feeling and going, now we can do some of those things without giving up on all the progress. Because I do think you can get too nostalgic. I do think you can start just fantasizing that everything was better in the ’90s. I wasn’t.
(00:20:21) 而且本就应该如此。为什么现在反而更难了呢？今天的计算机速度几乎比 90 年代快了无数倍，那我们难道不应该能以更简单的方式工作吗？我们应该回望 90 年代，说：“哦，那时候其实太复杂了。”如今我们拥有更先进、更快的技术，理应让我们以更易用的方式工作。但现实并非如此。而你现在可以看到我在 Ruby on Rails——尤其是 Rails 8——工作中划出的这条线。“无需构建”对我而言，就是重新找回那种 90 年代的感觉，并且告诉我们：现在我们可以做到那些事，而无需放弃过去所有的进步。因为我也认为，怀旧过头是有问题的。人很容易陷入一种幻想，觉得 90 年代什么都更好。但事实并非如此。

(00:21:10) I mean, I was there, there was a lot of things that sucked. And if we can somehow find a way to combine the advantages and advances we’ve had over the past 20 years with that ease of developer ergonomics, we can win. No build is a rejection of the part of web development I’ve hated the most in the past 10, 15 years, which is the JavaScript scene. And I don’t say that as someone who hates JavaScript. I mean, I often joke that JavaScript is my second favorite program language. It’s a very distant second. Ruby is by far and away number one, but I actually like JavaScript. I don’t think it’s a bad language. It gets a lot of flak. People add a string of two plus a one and it gives something nonsense, and I just go, yeah, but why would you do that? Just don’t do that. The language is actually quite lovely, especially the modern version.
(00:21:10) 毕竟我当时就在那里，有很多东西其实糟透了。如果我们能找到一种方式，把过去 20 年所取得的优势和进步，与那种简单的开发者体验结合起来，我们就赢了。“无需构建”正是我对过去 10 到 15 年中最讨厌的 Web 开发部分——也就是 JavaScript 生态——的一种拒绝。但我这么说，并不是因为我讨厌 JavaScript。我经常开玩笑说，JavaScript 是我第二喜欢的编程语言——不过这个“第二”差得非常远。Ruby 毫无疑问是第一，但我其实挺喜欢 JavaScript 的。我不觉得它是一门糟糕的语言。它确实挨了不少批评，比如有人把字符串 “2” 加上数字 1，结果得到一个荒谬的答案，我会说：“好吧，但你为什么要这么干？别这么写就行了。”实际上，这门语言相当优雅，尤其是现代版本。

(00:22:02) ES6, that really introduced a proper class syntax to it, so I could work with JavaScript in many of the same ways that I love working with Ruby. It made things so much better. But in the early 2010s until quite recently, all of that advancement happened in pre-processing, happened in build pipelines. The browsers couldn’t speak a dialect of JavaScript that was pleasant to work with so everyone started pre-compiling their JavaScript to be able to use more modern ways of programming with a browser that was seen as stuck with an ancient version of JavaScript that no one actually wanted to work with. And that made sense to me, but it was also deeply unpleasant. And I remember thinking during that time, the dark ages as I refer to them with JavaScript, that this cannot be the final destination. There’s no way that we have managed to turn the internet into such an unpleasant place to work where I would start working on a project in JavaScript using Webpack and all of these dependencies, and I would put it down for literally five minutes and the thing wouldn’t compile anymore.
(00:22:02) ES6 真正为 JavaScript 引入了规范的类语法，让我能以许多和使用 Ruby 时同样喜爱的方式编写 JavaScript。这让一切变得好多了。但在 2010 年代初直到最近，所有这些进步都发生在预处理阶段，发生在构建流水线中。因为浏览器无法直接运行那些令人愉悦的 JavaScript 方言，所以大家都开始对 JavaScript 进行预编译，以便能在浏览器中使用更现代的编程方式——而当时的浏览器被认为仍被卡在一个没人真正愿意使用的古老 JavaScript 版本上。这种做法我能理解，但也极其令人不快。我记得在那段我称之为 JavaScript “黑暗时代”的时期，我曾想：这绝不可能是终点。我们怎么可能把互联网变成了一个如此令人痛苦的工作场所？我刚用 Webpack 和一大堆依赖启动一个 JavaScript 项目，放下五分钟再回来，项目就再也编译不了了。

(00:23:14) The amount of churn that the JavaScript community, especially with its frameworks and its tooling, went through in the decade from 2010 to 2020 was absurd. And you had to be trapped inside of that asylum to not realize what an utterly perverse situation we had landed ourselves in. Why does everything break all the time? I mean, the joke wouldn’t be just that the software would break, that would annoy me personally. But then I’d go on Hacker News and I’d see some thread on the latest JavaScript release of some framework, and the thread would be like, someone would ask, well, aren’t we using the thing we just used three months ago? And people would be like, that thing is so outdated. That’s so three months ago. You’ve got to get with the new program, we’re completely rewriting everything for the [inaudible 00:24:07] time and anything you’ve learned in the framework you’ve been spending the last amount of time on, it’s all useless. You’ve got to throw everything out and you’ve got to start over. Why aren’t you doing it stupid idiot?
(00:23:14) 从 2010 到 2020 这十年间，JavaScript 社区——尤其是其框架和工具链——经历的剧烈变动简直荒谬至极。只有深陷那个“疯人院”里的人，才会意识不到我们把自己置于一种多么扭曲的境地。为什么所有东西总是在崩溃？我的困扰还不只是软件本身会坏掉——这已经够烦人了。更夸张的是，我去 Hacker News 上看到关于某个框架最新 JavaScript 版本的讨论帖，里面有人问：“我们不是三个月前才刚用过这个东西吗？”其他人就会回答：“那玩意儿早就过时了！那是三个月前的东西了！你得跟上新潮流啊，我们正在第 [听不清 00:24:07] 次彻底重写一切。你花那么多时间学的那个框架，现在全都没用了。你得把一切都扔掉，从头开始。你怎么还不这么做，你这个愚蠢的白痴？”

Lex Fridman
Lex Fridman

(00:24:18) Is that a kind of mass hysteria that took over the developer community you think? Like where you have to keep creating new frameworks and new frameworks and are we past that dark age?
(00:24:18) 你觉得这是不是一种席卷整个开发者社区的集体癔症？就像大家非得不断创造新的框架、再创造新的框架？我们现在是否已经走出了那个“黑暗时代”？

DHH
DHH

(00:24:29) I think we’re getting out of it and we’re getting out of it because browsers have gotten so much better. There was a stagnation in browser technology. Some of it was an overhang all the way back from IE5. So IE5 essentially put the whole internet development experience into a deep freeze because Microsoft won the browser wars in the mid-2000s, and then they basically disbanded their browser development team because they’re like all right, job done, we don’t need any more innovation on the internet. Can we just go back to writing Windows forms or something now that we control everything? And it really wasn’t until obviously Firefox kind of kindled a little bit of something. Then Chrome got into the scene and Google got serious about moving to web forward, that you had a kindling of maybe the browser could be better. Maybe the browser wasn’t frozen in time in 2005. Maybe the browser could actually evolve like the development platform that it is. But then what happened was you had a lot of smart people who poured in to the web because the web turned out to be the greatest application development platform of all time. This was where all the money was being made. This was where all the billionaires were being minted. This was where the Facebook’s and whatever of the world came to be. So you had all of this brain power applied to the problem of how to work with the web, and there were some very smart people with some I’m sure very good ideas who did not have programmer happiness as their motivation number one. They had other priorities and those priorities allowed them to discount and even rationalize the complexity they were injecting everywhere. Some of that complexity came from organizational structure. When you have a company like Facebook for example that does depend on the web and want to push it forward, but have sliced the development role job into these tiny little niches… I’m a front-end glob pipeline configurator.
(00:24:29) 我觉得我们正在走出那个时代，而之所以能走出来，是因为浏览器已经变得好太多了。此前浏览器技术长期停滞，部分原因甚至可以追溯到 IE5。IE5 实际上让整个互联网开发体验陷入了深度冻结——因为微软在 2000 年代中期赢得了浏览器大战，随后基本上解散了浏览器开发团队，心想：“好了，任务完成，互联网不需要更多创新了。既然我们已经掌控了一切，能不能回去继续写 Windows Forms 之类的东西？”直到后来 Firefox 点燃了一点星火，接着 Chrome 登场，Google 开始认真推动 Web 向前发展，人们才逐渐意识到：也许浏览器可以变得更好；也许浏览器并没有在 2005 年就被时间冻结；也许浏览器真的可以像它本应成为的开发平台那样持续演进。但随之而来的是，大量聪明人涌入 Web 领域，因为 Web 最终被证明是有史以来最伟大的应用开发平台——这里赚到了最多的钱，诞生了最多的亿万富翁，Facebook 之类的公司也在这里崛起。于是，大量顶尖人才开始集中解决“如何与 Web 协作”这个问题。其中不乏一些非常聪明、想法也很棒的人，但他们并非以“程序员幸福感”为首要动机。他们有其他优先事项，而这些优先事项让他们可以忽视、甚至合理化自己到处注入的复杂性。有些复杂性源于组织架构。比如像 Facebook 这样的公司，虽然依赖 Web 并希望推动其发展，却把开发岗位切割成无数微小的细分角色……“我是前端 Glob 流水线配置员。”

(00:26:41) Oh yeah, well, I’m a front-end whatever engineer. And suddenly the web developer was no longer one person. It was 15 different roles. That in itself injected a ton of complexity. But I also want to give it the bold case here, which was that some of that complexity was necessary to get to where we are today, that the complexity was a bridge. It wasn’t the destination, but we had to cross that bridge to get to where we are today where browsers are frankly incredible. The JavaScript you can write in a text file and then serve on a web server for a browser to ingest is amazing. It’s actually a really good experience. You don’t need any pre-processing. You could just write text files, send them to a browser, and you have an incredible development-
(00:26:41) “哦对了，那我就是个前端某某工程师。”突然之间，“Web 开发者”不再是一个人，而是变成了 15 个不同的角色。这本身就引入了大量复杂性。但我也想强调一点：其中某些复杂性其实是通往今天所必需的——那种复杂性是一座桥梁，而非终点。我们必须走过这座桥，才能到达今天这个浏览器已经无比强大的时代。如今，你只需在一个文本文件里写 JavaScript，然后通过 Web 服务器发送给浏览器，就能获得惊人的体验。这实际上是一种非常棒的开发体验。你不再需要任何预处理。你只需编写文本文件，发送给浏览器，就能拥有一个不可思议的开发——
(00:27:25) And we should also say that it can kind of be broken, at least the HTML, but even the JavaScript could be a little bit broken and it kind of still works. Like maybe it half-ass works, but just the amount of mess of smelly code that a browser has to deal with is insane.
（00:27:25）我们还应该指出，网页代码即使有点“坏掉”——至少 HTML 是这样，甚至 JavaScript 也可能有点问题——它在某种程度上仍然能运行。也许只是勉强能用，但浏览器必须处理的那些混乱、糟糕的代码量简直疯狂。

DHH
(00:27:44) This is one of the hardest problems in computing today is to parse the entire internet. Because thankfully for us as web developers, but perhaps not so much for the browser developers, every webpage that has ever been created minus the brief period with Flash still runs today. The webpage I did in ninth grade would render on a modern browser today, 30 years later.
DHH
（00:27:44）如今计算机领域最难的问题之一，就是解析整个互联网。对我们这些网页开发者来说这是件幸事，但对浏览器开发者而言可能就没那么幸运了——除了短暂的 Flash 时代之外，历史上创建过的每一个网页今天依然可以运行。我九年级时做的网页，30 年后的今天依然能在现代浏览器中正常显示。

Lex Fridman
(00:28:10) That’s crazy.
Lex Fridman
（00:28:10）这太疯狂了。

DHH
(00:28:11) That is completely crazy when you think about the amount of evolution we’ve had with the web, how much better we’ve made it, how many more standards browsers have adopted. It’s essentially an Apollo project today to create a new browser, which is why it doesn’t happen very often, which is why even companies like Microsoft had to throw in the towel and say, we can’t do it. Now, I actually don’t think that’s good for the web. There is the danger of the monoculture if we just get a single browser engine that runs everything, and we are in danger of that. I love the fact that the Ladybird project, for example, is trying to make a new browser engine from scratch. I’ve supported that project. I would encourage people to look into that. It’s really a wonderful thing. It’s staffed by a bunch of people who worked on other browser projects in the past.
DHH
（00:28:11）当你想到网络经历了如此巨大的演进，我们把它变得更好了，浏览器也采纳了更多标准，这种向后兼容性就显得格外不可思议。如今要从头开发一个新浏览器，几乎相当于阿波罗登月工程，所以这种事情很少发生，甚至连微软这样的公司最终都不得不放弃，承认“我们做不到”。实际上，我认为这对网络生态并非好事。如果我们最终只剩下一种浏览器引擎来运行一切，就会陷入单一文化的危险，而我们现在正面临这种风险。我很欣赏像 Ladybird 项目这样尝试从零开始构建全新浏览器引擎的努力。我一直支持这个项目，也鼓励大家去关注它。这真是一件了不起的事——它的团队由一群曾参与过其他浏览器项目的人组成。

(00:28:59) We really need that. If it had not been for Google and their entire business depending on a thriving open web, Apple, Microsoft I think would’ve been just as fine to see the web go away to disappear into being something that’s just served native mobile applications and native desktop applications that they could completely control. So I have all sorts of problems with Google, but it’s not Chrome. Chrome is a complete gift to web developers everywhere, to the web as a development platform, and they deserve an enormous amount of credit I think for that. Even if it’s entangled with their business model and half of Chrome is code that spies on you or informs targeted ads and a bunch of things I’m not a big fan of, I can divorce that from the fact that we need champions in the corner of the web who have trillions of dollars of market cap value riding on the open web.
DHH
（00:28:59）我们确实需要这样的独立浏览器。但与此同时，我也清楚地意识到：Google Chrome 对于网络作为首要 Web 开发平台的存续起到了关键作用。如果不是因为 Google 的整个商业模式都依赖于一个繁荣的开放网络，我认为苹果和微软很可能乐见网络逐渐消失，变成仅由它们完全掌控的原生移动应用和桌面应用所主导的世界。我对 Google 有很多不满，但 Chrome 不在其中。Chrome 对全球 Web 开发者、对 Web 作为一个开发平台而言，是一份完完全全的礼物，Google 在这方面理应获得巨大的赞誉。即便 Chrome 与其商业模式紧密捆绑，其中一半代码可能在监视你、推送定向广告——这些我并不喜欢——但我仍能将这些与另一个事实区分开来：我们需要有重量级的支持者站在 Web 这一边，而这些支持者的数万亿美元市值都建立在开放网络之上。

(00:31:44) Maybe it doesn’t need it, but it certainly benefits from it. And of all the things that are wrong with monopoly formation in technology, Chrome is the last thing, and this is why I get so frustrated sometimes about the monopoly fight, that there are real problems and we should be focusing on the premier problems first like the toll booths on our mobile phones. There are far bigger problems. It’s not the open web, it’s not the tools that we use to access the open web. If I don’t want to use Chrome, if my customers of my businesses that run on the internet don’t want to use Chrome, they don’t have to. We’re never forced to go through it. The open internet is still open. So I think it’s a real shame that the DOJ has chosen to pursue Google in this way. I do think there are other things you can nail Google for, their ad monopoly maybe, or the shenanigans they’ve done in controlling both sides of the ad ledger, that they both control the supply and the demand.
（00:31:44）也许开放网络并不“需要”它，但肯定从中获益。在科技领域所有与垄断相关的问题中，Chrome 是最不该被拿来开刀的。这正是我有时对反垄断斗争感到沮丧的原因：明明存在真正严重的问题，我们却不去优先解决那些首要问题，比如手机上的“收费站”（指应用商店抽成等）。还有大得多的问题亟待处理。问题不在开放网络本身，也不在我们访问开放网络所用的工具上。如果我不想用 Chrome，或者我那些基于互联网开展业务的客户不想用 Chrome，他们完全可以不用。我们从未被强迫使用它。开放互联网依然是开放的。因此，我认为司法部选择以这种方式针对 Google 实在令人遗憾。当然，Google 确实有其他可被追责之处，比如其广告垄断，或者他们在广告账本两端同时操控供需的种种操作。

(00:32:45) There are problems. Chrome, isn’t it. And you end up making the web much worse. And this is the thing we’ve always got to remember when we think about legislation, when we think about monopoly fights is you may not like how things look today and you may want to do something about it, but you may also make it worse. The good intentions behind the GDPR in Europe currently has amounted to what? Cookie banners that everyone on the internet hates, that helps no one do anything better, anything more efficient, that saves no privacy in any way, shape or form, has been a complete boondoggle that has only enriched lawyers and accountants and bureaucrats.
（00:32:45）问题确实存在，但 Chrome 并不是问题所在。强行拆分反而会让 Web 变得更糟。我们在思考立法、思考反垄断斗争时，必须始终牢记一点：你可能不喜欢现状，想采取行动，但也可能让情况变得更糟。欧洲 GDPR 背后的良好初衷，如今带来了什么？就是全网用户都痛恨的 Cookie 弹窗——它既没有帮助任何人做得更好或更高效，也没有以任何方式真正保护隐私，完全是一场闹剧，唯一受益的是律师、会计师和官僚。

DHH

(00:34:50) There’s this one piece of legislation that’s now I think 10 or 12 years old. It’s complete failure on every conceivable metric. (00:38:28) PHP was what converted me from just being able to fondle HTML and turn out some web pages to actually being able to produce web applications myself. DHH
(00:38:28) 是 PHP 让我从只会摆弄 HTML、制作一些网页，转变为能够真正独立开发 Web 应用程序。在这方面，我对 PHP 怀有极大的感激之情。但我从未把 PHP 视为自己的使命。我并不是那种“我是一名写 PHP 的专业程序员，这就是我的身份和事业”的人。我把 PHP 看作一种工具——一种我需要用它敲打电脑，直到它产出我想要的 Web 应用的工具。它纯粹是一种达成目的的手段。我并没有爱上 PHP。我很感激它教会了我编程的基础知识，也很感激它为我设定了经济层面的门槛。但真正让我开始将自己视为一名程序员的，是在接触 Ruby 之后。事情的契机是：我第一次被聘为专业程序员写代码，其实是受雇于我至今仍是商业伙伴的 Jason Fried。

(00:39:31) All the way back in 2001, I had been working on these gaming websites in PHP for essentially 18 months at that point. But we started working together and we started working together on some client projects.
(00:39:31) 那要追溯到 2001 年，当时我已经用 PHP 开发游戏网站大约 18 个月了，但没人因此付钱给我写代码。我通过一封从丹麦哥本哈根发往伊利诺伊州芝加哥的电子邮件，联系上了当时完全不认识我的 Jason Fried。我只是在回应他公开征求的建议。Jason 在网上提了个问题，我给他发了答案——他问的是 PHP 相关的问题，我就回复了解决方案。于是我们开始交谈，继而开始合作。顺便说一句，这正是互联网所能创造的奇迹：一个哥本哈根的年轻人，从未见过芝加哥的这个人，仅凭一封邮件就能开始一起工作？而且，24 年后的今天，我们仍在合作。这简直不可思议。但我们确实就这样开始了合作，并一起承接了一些客户项目。

(00:40:25) Jason would do the design, 37signals would do the design. I would bring the programming PHP. And after we work on I think two or three client projects together in PHP, we kept hitting the same problem that whenever you work with a client, you start that project off an email, “Oh, yeah, let’s work together. Here’s what we’re building.” And you start trading more and more emails and before a few weeks have passed, you got to add someone to the project. They don’t have the emails, they don’t have the context. You send them, “Where’s the latest file?” “Oh, I’ve uploaded it on the FTP. It’s like final, final V06 2.0.” Right? That’s the one to get. It’s just a mess, a beautiful mess in some ways. It’s a mess that still runs the vast majority of projects to this day. Email is the lowest common denominator. That’s wonderful.
(00:40:25) Jason 负责设计，37signals 负责设计，而我则负责用 PHP 编程。在我们一起用 PHP 完成了两三个客户项目之后，我们反复遇到同一个问题：每当与客户合作时，项目总是从一封邮件开始：“哦，好的，我们一起干吧。我们要做的是这个。”然后你们开始来回发越来越多的邮件；几周之内，你就得往项目里加新人。但他们没有之前的邮件，也没有上下文。你问他们：“最新文件在哪儿？”他们会回答：“哦，我传到 FTP 上了，文件名大概是 ‘final_final_V06_2.0’。”对吧？就那个版本。这简直一团糟——某种意义上还挺“美妙”的混乱。而这种混乱至今仍支撑着绝大多数项目的运转。电子邮件是最通用的最低标准，这很棒。

(00:41:13) But we had dropped the ball a couple of times in serious ways with customers and we thought we can do better. We know how to make web applications. Can’t we just make a system that’s better than email for managing projects? It can’t be that hard. We’ve been doing blogs, we’ve been doing to-do lists. Let’s put some of these things together and just make a system where everything that anyone involved in the project needs is on one page. And it has to be simple enough that I’m not going to run a seminar teaching you how to use the system. I’m just going to give you the login code. You’re going to jump into it. So that’s Basecamp. When we started working on Basecamp, I, for the first time in the experience I had with Jason had the freedom of technology choice. There was no client telling me, “Yeah, PHP, that sounds good. We know PHP. Can you build it in PHP?”
(00:41:13) 但我们曾几次严重地搞砸了客户的事情，于是我们想：我们明明能做得更好。我们知道如何开发 Web 应用，难道就不能做一个比电子邮件更适合管理项目的系统吗？这应该不难。我们做过博客，也做过待办清单。不如把这些功能整合起来，打造一个系统，让所有项目相关人员需要的一切信息都集中在一个页面上。而且这个系统必须足够简单，我不需要开培训课教你如何使用——我只要给你登录凭证，你就能立刻上手。这就是 Basecamp 的由来。当我们开始开发 Basecamp 时，这是我与 Jason 合作以来第一次拥有技术选型的自由。没有客户对我说：“PHP 听起来不错，我们就懂 PHP，你能用 PHP 做吗？”

(00:42:06) I had free reins. At that time I’d been reading IEEE magazine and a couple of other magazines back from the early 2000s where Dave Thomas and Martin Fowler had been writing about programming patterns and how to write better code. These two guys in particular were both using Ruby to explain their concepts because Ruby looked like pseudocode. Whether you were programming in C or Java or PHP, all three constituencies could understand Ruby because it basically just reads like English. So these guys were using Ruby to describe the concepts, and first of all, I would read these articles for just the concepts they were explaining and I’d be like, “What is this program language?” I mean, I like the concept you’re explaining, but I also want to see the programming language. Why haven’t I heard of this?
(00:42:06) 我拥有了完全的自主权。那时我正在读《IEEE 杂志》以及其他几本 2000 年代初的刊物，其中 Dave Thomas 和 Martin Fowler 正在撰写关于编程模式和如何写出更好代码的文章。这两位作者特别喜欢用 Ruby 来阐释他们的概念，因为 Ruby 看起来就像伪代码。无论你用的是 C、Java 还是 PHP，这三类程序员都能理解 Ruby，因为它基本上读起来就像英语。所以他们用 Ruby 来描述编程思想。起初，我只是为了理解这些概念而读这些文章，但很快我就忍不住想：“这是什么编程语言？”我的意思是，我喜欢你解释的概念，但我也想看看这门语言本身——为什么我之前从来没听说过它？

(00:43:02) So I started looking into Ruby and I realized at that time, Ruby might not be known by anyone, but it’s actually been around for a long time. Matz, the Japanese creator of Ruby, had started working on Ruby back in ’93 before the internet was even a thing. And here I am in 2003, 10 years later, picking up what seems like this hidden gem that’s just laying in obscurity and plain sight. But Dave Thomas and Martin Fowler, I think successfully put me and a handful of other people on the trail of a programming language that hadn’t been used much in the west, but could be. So I picked up Ruby and I thought, this is very different. First of all, where are all the semicolons? I’d been programming in PHP, in ASP, I’d even done some Pascal. I’d looked at some C. There were semicolons everywhere.
(00:43:02) 于是我开始研究 Ruby，很快意识到：虽然当时几乎没人知道 Ruby，但它其实已经存在很久了。Ruby 的日本创造者 Matz（松本行弘）早在 1993 年——互联网都还没普及的时候——就开始开发 Ruby 了。而我在 2003 年，也就是十年后，偶然发现了这颗仿佛被遗忘在光天化日之下的隐藏宝石。我认为，正是 Dave Thomas 和 Martin Fowler 成功地引导我和少数其他人，踏上了一门在西方尚未被广泛使用、却潜力巨大的编程语言之路。于是我开始使用 Ruby，立刻觉得它非常不同。首先，分号去哪儿了？我之前用过 PHP、ASP，甚至还写过一些 Pascal，也看过一些 C 语言代码——到处都是分号。
(00:44:05) That was the first thing that struck me is where are the damn semicolons? And I started thinking, actually, why do we have semicolons in programming? They’re to tell the interpreter that there’s a new line of instructions, but I don’t need them as a human. Oh, someone is looking out for the human here, not for the machine. So that really got me interested. And then I thought to myself, do you know what? I know PHP quite well. I’m not an amazing programmer. I haven’t been working in programming for all that long, but maybe I can figure it out. I’m going to give myself two weeks. I’m going to write a proof of concept where I talked to a database, I pulled some records, I format them a bit, and I display them on an HTML page. Can I figure that out in a couple of weeks? It took about one weekend and I was completely mesmerized. I was completely mind blown because Ruby was made for my brain like a perfect tailored glove by someone I’d never met. How is this even possible?

（00:44:05）最先让我震惊的是：该死的分号都去哪儿了？我开始思考，编程中为什么要有分号？它们的作用是告诉解释器这里有一条新的指令，但作为人类，我其实并不需要它们。哦，原来有人是在为人类着想，而不是为机器。这真的引起了我的兴趣。然后我对自己说：“你知道吗？我对 PHP 相当熟悉。我不是什么了不起的程序员，也没干编程多久，但也许我能搞明白。我要给自己两周时间，写一个概念验证程序：连接数据库、拉取一些记录、稍作格式化，然后在 HTML 页面上显示出来。我能在几周内搞定吗？”结果大概只用了一个周末，我就彻底着迷了。我完全被震撼了，因为 Ruby 就像是某个我从未谋面的人为我的大脑量身打造的一副完美贴合的手套。这怎么可能？

（00:46:41）Ruby 到处都是这样的设计。让我再深入举几个例子，因为我觉得这有助于描绘出整体图景。不过我要先说明一点：我其实很喜欢 Python 的精神气质。我认为 Ruby 和 Python 社区有很多相似之处。它们都是动态解释型语言，都注重即时性、生产力和易用性。但在很多其他方面，它们又截然不同。其中一个显著差异就在于美学。

(00:47:12) Python to me, I hope I don’t offend people too much. （00:47:12）对我来说，Python——希望我不会冒犯太多人——它就是丑，而且从底层就丑，因为它充斥着大量冗余的语法，这些只是因为 Guido 在 1987 年创建 Python 时的历史原因而保留下来，到了 2025 年依然存在，而我的大脑实在无法接受。举个基本例子：当你在 Python 中定义一个类时，初始化方法（即构造函数）以 def 开头，好吧，这点还算合理，实际上 Ruby 也一样，都是用 def 来定义方法。但接下来却是双下划线、init、再双下划线：__init__，然后括号开始，self，逗号，接着才是第一个参数。

Ruby itself is implemented in C. It’s very difficult to parse Ruby code because Ruby is written for humans and humans are messy creatures. They like things in just the right way. DHH
（00:48:41）它让我意识到程序可以有多美。我以前根本不知道。我之前一直在用 ASP、PHP 编程，从未想过“美学”或“美丽的代码”是可以被优化的目标。这不仅是可以追求的，甚至可以凌驾于其他目标之上。Ruby 之所以如此优美，并非偶然，也绝非易事。Ruby 本身是用 C 实现的，而解析 Ruby 代码非常困难，因为 Ruby 是为人类写的，而人类本身就是混乱的生物——我们喜欢恰到好处的东西。我无法完全解释为什么 __init__ 让我如此反感，但它确实让我反感。而当我看到 Ruby 的替代方案时，就特别有启发性：同样是 def，也就是 D-E-F 加空格，然后直接写 initialize，连括号都不需要——如果你的方法不需要参数，甚至连括号都可以省略。

(00:49:44) That in itself is actually also a major part. If the human doesn’t need the additional characters, we’re not just going to put them in because it’d be nicer to parse for the computer. We’re going to get rid of the semicolons, we’re going to get rid of the parentheses, we’re going to get rid of the underscores, we’re going to get rid of all that ugliness, all the line noise and boil it down to its pure essentials and at the same time, we’re not going to abbreviate. This is a key difference in the aesthetics between Ruby and Python as well. Init is shorter to type, it’s only five characters. Initialize is a lot longer, but it looks a lot better and you don’t type it very often, so you should look at something pretty. If you don’t have to do it all the time, it’s okay that it’s long.

（00:49:44）这一点本身就非常重要：如果人类不需要额外的字符，我们就不会仅仅因为对计算机解析更友好而强行加上它们。我们要去掉分号、去掉括号、去掉下划线，去掉所有那些丑陋的“行噪声”，将其提炼为最纯粹的本质。同时，我们也不做缩写。这也是 Ruby 与 Python 在美学上的一个关键区别。“Init”打起来更短，只有五个字符；而“Initialize”要长得多，但它看起来好看得多，而且你并不经常需要输入它，所以你应该看到的是赏心悦目的东西。既然不是天天都要敲，长一点也无妨。

(00:50:29) Those kinds of aesthetic evaluations are rife all over the Ruby language. But let me give you an even better example. The if conditional, that’s the bedrock of all programming languages. They have the if conditional, if you take most programming languages, they’ll have if, that’s basically the same in almost every language, space, start parentheses, we all do that. And then you have perhaps, let’s say you’re calling a object called user. is admin, close parentheses, close parentheses, start brackets, and here’s what we’re going to do if the user’s an admin, right? That would be a normal programming language. Ruby doesn’t do it like that. Ruby boils almost all of it away. We start with the if. Okay, that’s the same, no parentheses necessary because there’s no ambiguity for the human to distinguish that the next part is just a single statement. So you do if, space, user dot admin, question mark, no open brackets, no parentheses, no nothing. Next open line, here’s your conditional.

（00:50:29）这类美学考量遍布整个 Ruby 语言。但我再给你一个更好的例子：if 条件语句，这是所有编程语言的基石。大多数语言中，if 语句基本都一样：写 if，加空格，再加左括号——我们都这么干。比如你调用一个叫 user 的对象，判断 is_admin，然后右括号、左大括号，接着写如果用户是管理员要执行的操作，对吧？这是普通编程语言的做法。但 Ruby 不是这样。Ruby 几乎把这些全都去掉了。我们从 if 开始，这部分一样；但不需要括号，因为对人类来说，下一部分显然是一个单一语句，毫无歧义。所以你只需写 if user.admin?，不需要大括号，不需要括号，什么都不需要。下一行直接写你的条件体即可。

(00:51:45) That question mark means nothing to the computer, but it means something to the human. Ruby put in the predicate method style purely as a communication tool between humans. It’s actually more work for the interpreter to be able to see that this question mark is there. Why is this question mark in here? Because it just reads so nicely. If user admin question mark, that’s a very human phrase, but it gets better. You can turn this around. You can have your statement, you want to execute before the conditional. You can do user.upgrade, say you’re calling an upgrade method on a user, space, if, space, user.admin question mark. We do the thing, if the thing is true, instead of saying if the thing is true, do the thing. But it gets even better. This is why I love this example with the conditional because you can keep diving into it. So let’s flip it around. user.downgrade if exclamation point, not user.admin, that’d be a typical way of writing it. Ruby goes that exclamation point is light noise. Why do we have if and then an exclamation point that’s ugly? We could do user.downgrade unless user.admin question mark.

（00:51:45）这个问号对计算机毫无意义，但对人类却有意义。Ruby 引入谓词方法（predicate method）风格，纯粹是为了人与人之间的沟通。实际上，解释器要识别这个问号反而更费劲。那为什么要加这个问号？因为它读起来太顺了。“如果用户是管理员？”这是一个非常人性化的表达。而且还能更进一步：你可以把要执行的语句放在条件之前。比如你可以写 user.upgrade if user.admin? ——假设你在调用用户的升级方法。意思是“如果用户是管理员，就升级”，而不是“如果条件成立，就执行操作”。但这还不是全部。这正是我喜欢这个条件语句例子的原因——你可以不断深入挖掘。比如反过来：user.downgrade if !user.admin，这是一种常见的写法。但 Ruby 认为那个感叹号就是“视觉噪声”。为什么我们要写 if 再加一个丑陋的感叹号？我们可以直接写成 user.downgrade unless user.admin?。

Lex Fridman
(00:53:16) That is awesome.

Lex Fridman
（00:53:16）这太棒了。
(00:53:17) That to me is an encapsulation of the incredible beauty that Ruby affords the programmer through ambiguity that is only to serve the human reader and writer. All of these statements we’ve just discussed, they’re the same for the computer. It’ll compile down to the same C code. They’ll compile down to the same assembly code. It makes no difference whatsoever. In fact, it just makes it harder to write an interpreter. But for the human who gets to choose whether the statement comes before the conditional or the predicate method has, it’s just incredible. It reads like poetry at some point.

（00:53:17）对我来说，这正是 Ruby 通过模糊性赋予程序员的惊人之美——这种模糊性唯一的目的就是服务于人类读者和写作者。我们刚才讨论的所有这些语句，对计算机而言都是一样的。它们会被编译成相同的 C 代码，也会被编译成相同的汇编代码。这完全没有任何区别。事实上，它只会让编写解释器变得更困难。但对于可以自由选择“语句是否放在条件之前”或“谓词方法如何使用”的人来说，这种体验简直不可思议。在某些时刻，它读起来就像诗歌一样。

DHH
(00:54:24) That’s what Matz thought about because he started his entire mission off a different premise than almost every programming language designer that I’d heard at least articulate their vision, that his number one goal was programmer happiness. That his number one goal was the affordances that would allow programmers to articulate code in ways that not just executed correctly, but were a joy to write and were a joy to read. That vision is based on a fundamentally different view of humanity. There’s no greater contrast between Matz and James Gosling, the designer of Java. I wanted to listen to James talk about the design of Java. Why was it the way it was? Why was it so rigid? He was very blunt about it, which by the way, I really appreciate and I think Gosling has done a tremendous job with Java, but his view of humanity is rather dark.

DHH
（00:54:24）这正是 Matz 所思考的问题，因为他的出发点与我所听过的几乎所有编程语言设计者都不同——他最首要的目标是程序员的幸福感。他最看重的是那些能让程序员以不仅正确执行、而且写起来愉悦、读起来愉快的方式表达代码的能力。这一愿景基于一种根本不同的人性观。Matz 与 Java 的设计者 James Gosling 之间的对比再鲜明不过了。我曾很想听听 James 谈论 Java 的设计：为什么它会是那个样子？为什么如此僵化？他对此非常直率——顺便说一句，我真的很欣赏这一点，而且我认为 Gosling 在 Java 上做得非常出色——但他对人性的看法相当悲观。

(00:55:24) His view of humanity was programmers at the average are stupid creatures. They cannot be trusted with sophisticated programming languages because they’re going to shoot their foot off or their hand off. And that would be kind of inconvenient to the regional development office of a mid-tier insurance company writing code that has to last for 20 years. Now it’s actually a very Thomas Sowell view of constrained capacity in humans that I’ve come to appreciate much later in life. But it’s also a very depressing view of programmers that there are just certain programmers who are too dumb to appreciate code poetry. They’re too ignorant to learn how to write it well. We need to give them a sandbox where they just won’t hurt themselves too much.

（00:55:24）他认为普通程序员是愚蠢的生物，不能被信任去使用复杂的编程语言，因为他们很可能会“搬起石头砸自己的脚”——这对一家中等规模保险公司的地方开发部门来说会很不方便，毕竟他们写的代码要维持二十年之久。这实际上是一种托马斯·索维尔（Thomas Sowell）式的人类能力受限的观点，我在人生后期才逐渐理解并欣赏这种看法。但这也是一种令人沮丧的程序员观：认为有些程序员太愚钝，无法欣赏代码中的诗意；太无知，学不会如何写出优美的代码。所以我们需要给他们一个沙盒，让他们不至于伤到自己太多。

(00:56:20) Matz went the complete opposite direction. He believes in humanity. He believes in the unlimited capacity of programmers to learn and become better so much so that he’s willing to put the stranger at his own level. This is the second part I truly appreciate about Ruby. Ruby allows you to extend base classes. You know how we just talked about five dot times is a way to iterate over a statement five times? That five is obviously a base class, it’s a number. Do you know what? You can add your own methods to that? I did extensively. In Rails, we have something called active support, which is essentially my dialect of Ruby for programming web applications. I’ll give you one example. I’ve added a method called Days to the Number. So if you do five .days, you get five days in seconds because seconds is the way we set cache expiration times and other things like that. So you can say cache expires in five .days and you’re going to get whatever-

（00:56:20）Matz 则完全走向了相反的方向。他相信人性，相信程序员拥有无限的学习与进步潜力，以至于他愿意将陌生人置于与自己同等的位置。这是我真正欣赏 Ruby 的第二个方面：Ruby 允许你扩展基础类。你还记得我们刚刚提到的 5.times 吗？这是一种将某条语句重复执行五次的方式。这里的 5 显然是一个基础类，即一个数字。但你知道吗？你可以给它添加自己的方法！我大量地这样做过。在 Rails 中，我们有一个叫 ActiveSupport 的模块，本质上是我为 Web 应用开发而定制的 Ruby 方言。举个例子：我给 Number 类添加了一个叫 days 的方法。所以当你写 5.days 时，你会得到五天对应的秒数——因为我们通常用秒来设置缓存过期时间之类的东西。因此你可以说“缓存将在 5.days 后过期”，然后就会得到相应的数值——

Lex Fridman
(00:57:34) That’s nice.

Lex Fridman
（00:57:34）这真不错。

DHH
(00:57:35) … five times, 24 times 60 times 60 is or whatever the math is, right? Very humanly readable. In a normal programming language, you would type out the seconds and then you would have a little comment above it saying this represent five days. In Ruby, you get to write five days. But even better than that, Matz didn’t come up with it. Matz didn’t need the five days. I needed that because I needed to expire caches. I was allowed by Matz to extend his story with my own chapters on equal footing such that a reader of Ruby could not tell the difference between the code Matz wrote and the code that I wrote.

DHH
（00:57:35）……也就是 5 × 24 × 60 × 60 秒，或者不管具体怎么算，对吧？这种方式极具可读性。在普通编程语言中，你得直接写出秒数，然后在上面加一行注释说明“这代表五天”。而在 Ruby 中，你只需写 5.days。更妙的是，这个功能并非 Matz 自己想出来的——他并不需要“五天”这个概念，是我需要它，因为我得设置缓存过期时间。而 Matz 允许我在平等的基础上，用自己的章节去延展他创造的故事，以至于 Ruby 的读者根本分不清哪些代码是 Matz 写的，哪些是我写的。

(00:58:16) He trusted me as a complete stranger from Denmark who he’d never met to mess with his beautiful story. That level of trust is essentially unheard of. I know there are other program languages that allow things with macros and so forth, but none do it in a way like Ruby does it. None does it with an articulated vision of humanity, a trust in humanity like Matz does. That is the opposite end of the spectrum of Java.

（00:58:16）他信任我这个来自丹麦、素未谋面的陌生人，允许我去“篡改”他那美丽的故事。这种程度的信任几乎是闻所未闻的。我知道还有其他编程语言也通过宏等方式提供类似能力，但没有一种语言像 Ruby 这样去做——没有一种语言像 Matz 那样，带着清晰的人性观和对人类的信任来实现这一点。这与 Java 完全处于光谱的两端。

Lex Fridman
(00:58:46) Yeah, I mean for my aesthetic sensibilities, just the way you described five .days, that’s really pleasant to me. I could see myself sitting alone sleep-deprived and just writing that. DHH
(00:59:51) No, you can compress it too far. Perl golf is a thing where you can turn programs into something that’s unreadable for humans. DHH
（00:59:51）没错，压缩过度也不行。“Perl Golf”就是一种把程序写得对人类完全不可读的竞赛。Perl 的伟大之处在于它早于 Ruby 出现。Matz 是 Larry Wall 的优秀学生，也是 Perl、Python、Smalltalk 和 Lisp 的优秀学生。他从所有这些早期创建优秀编程语言的尝试中汲取灵感，并真正提炼出了其中最精华的部分。因此，他能够从前人的经验中学习。但我觉得 Ruby 最不可思议的是：如今已是 2025 年，Ruby 已经发展了三十多年，而我们今天使用的代码中，仍有 90% 基本上就是最初那个初稿。

(01:00:38) There was almost a sense of divine inspiration possible in wherever Matz was writing that initial version of Ruby that transcended time to such a degree that no one has still even begun to reach it. This is the other thing I always find fascinating. I generally believe in the efficient market theory that if someone comes up with a better mousetrap or better idea, others, they’ll eventually copy them to such an extent that perhaps the original mousetrap is no longer even remembered. No one has been able to copy that essence of Ruby. They borrowed elements and that’s totally fine, but Ruby still stands taller than everyone else on these metrics, on this trust in humanity and programmers.

（01:00:38）Matz 当初编写 Ruby 初始版本时，几乎有种神启般的灵感，其超越时代之深，至今无人能及。这也是我一直觉得迷人的另一点。我通常相信有效市场理论：如果有人发明了更好的捕鼠器或更好的主意，其他人最终会模仿到连原始发明者都被遗忘的地步。但没有人能复制 Ruby 的这种精髓。他们可以借鉴一些元素——这完全没问题——但在“对人性和程序员的信任”这一维度上，Ruby 依然比其他所有语言都更高一筹。

Lex Fridman
(01:01:21) And we should also say maybe the perfect programming language is that metric, and then there’s the successful language and those are often different. There’s something wonderful about the Brendan Eich story of creating JavaScript. There’s something truly beautiful about the way JavaScript took over the world. I’ve recently got to visit the Amazon jungle and just one of my favorite things to do is just to watch the ants take over anything, everything. And it’s just like it’s a nice distributed system. It’s a messy thing that doesn’t seem to be ordered, but it just works and the machinery of it.

Lex Fridman
（01:01:21）我们还应该指出：也许“完美的编程语言”可以用这种标准来衡量，但“成功的语言”往往是另一回事。Brendan Eich 创造 JavaScript 的故事本身就有一种奇妙之处。JavaScript 席卷世界的方式也确实有一种独特的美。我最近去了亚马逊雨林，我最喜欢做的事情之一就是观察蚂蚁如何占领一切——它们就像一个精妙的分布式系统：看似混乱无序，却能高效运转，其内在机制令人着迷。
(01:01:58) Worse is Better. I mean that’s actually the name of a pattern in software development and other ways of how is the pattern of Linux. Linux was quantifiably worse than I think it was Minix at the time, other ways of it that were more cathedral, less bizarre, and it’s still want. That there’s something to it that the imperfections can help something go forward. It’s actually a trick I’ve studied to the degree that I now incorporated in almost all open source that I do. I make sure that when I release the first version of any new thing I work on, it’s a little broken. It’s a little busted in ways that invite people to come in and help me. Because there’s no easier way to get the collaboration of other programmers than to put something out that they know how to fix and improve.

（01:01:58）“更糟就是更好”（Worse is Better）。这实际上是一种软件开发中的模式名称，也是 Linux 所遵循的模式。当时 Linux 在可量化指标上确实比 Minix 更差，而 Minix 则属于那种更“大教堂式”、不那么怪异的系统，但 Linux 却胜出了。这其中自有道理：某些不完美反而能推动事物向前发展。我深入研究过这一策略，以至于如今我做的几乎所有开源项目都融入了这一点。我会确保在我发布任何新项目的第一个版本时，它多少有点问题——有些小缺陷，恰好能吸引人们参与进来帮我改进。因为要获得其他程序员协作，最简单的方式莫过于发布一个他们知道如何修复和优化的东西。

Lex Fridman
(01:02:49) Yeah, that’s awesome.

Lex Fridman
（01:02:49）是啊，这太棒了。

DHH
(01:02:49) But Ruby is somehow or was at least a little bit different in that regard. Not in all regards. Matz got the ethos of the language, the design of language just right. But the first versions of Ruby were terribly slow. It’s taken, I mean hundreds of man-years to get Ruby to be both this beautiful yet also highly efficient and really fast.

DHH
（01:02:49）但 Ruby 在这方面多少有些不同，至少曾经如此——并非在所有方面都如此。松本行弘（Matz）对语言的精神内核和设计把握得恰到好处。但早期的 Ruby 版本运行速度非常慢。可以说，花了数百人年的努力，才让 Ruby 既保持了这种美感，又变得高效且真正快速。

Metaprogramming

元编程

Lex Fridman
(01:03:15) We should say that the thing that made you fall in love with this particular programming language is Metaprogramming.

Lex Fridman
（01:03:15）我们应该指出，让你爱上这门特定编程语言的关键正是元编程（Metaprogramming）。

DHH
(01:03:21) Yes. So that takes all of these elements we’ve just talked about and turned them up to 11. I’ll explain Metaprogramming real simple.

DHH
（01:03:21）没错。这把我们刚才谈到的所有要素都推向了极致。我来用非常简单的方式解释一下元编程。

Lex Fridman
(01:03:28) Yeah, please.

Lex Fridman
（01:03:28）好啊，请讲。

DHH
(01:03:29) Metaprogramming is essentially a version of the five .days. You get to add keywords to the language. Active record is the part of Rails that communicates with the database. This is a system where every table in the database is represented by a class. So if we take the user example, again, you do class, user descends from active record base, and then the first line you can write is this, I want my users to have many posts or have many comments. Let’s do that. We’re making some system where users can make comments. The very next line is, has underscore many space colon comments.

DHH
（01:03:29）元编程本质上就像是“five.days”那样的扩展机制——你可以向语言中添加新的关键字。Active Record 是 Rails 中负责与数据库通信的部分。在这个系统中，数据库里的每张表都由一个类来表示。比如我们再次以用户（user）为例：你定义一个类，User 继承自 ActiveRecord::Base，然后你写下的第一行代码可以是：“我希望我的用户拥有多个帖子或多个评论。”我们就以评论为例吧——我们要构建一个允许用户发表评论的系统。紧接着下一行代码就是：has_many :comments。

(01:04:15) Now you’ve set up a dependency between users and comments that will give you a whole host of access and factory methods for users to be able to own comments, to create comments, to update comments. In that line alone ” has many” looks like a keyword. It looks like it’s part of the Ruby language. That’s metaprogramming. When Rails is able to add these elements to how you define a class, and then that runs code that adds a bunch of methods to the use of class, that’s Metaprogramming.

（01:04:15）现在你就在用户和评论之间建立了一种关联，从而自动获得大量访问器方法和工厂方法，使用户能够拥有、创建和更新评论。仅这一行代码中的 “has many” 看起来就像一个关键字，仿佛它是 Ruby 语言本身的一部分。这就是元编程。当 Rails 能够在你定义类时注入这些元素，并在运行时动态地为该类添加一堆方法，这就是元编程。

(01:04:49) And when Metaprogramming is used in this way, we call it domain-specific languages. You take a generic language like Ruby and you tailor it to a certain domain like describing relationships in a database at a object level. This is one of those early examples where you can do, user has many comments, belongs underscore two space colon account. Now you’ve set up a one-to-one relationship before we had a one-to-many relationship. Rails is rife with all these kinds of domain-specific languages where at sometimes it doesn’t even look like Ruby. You can’t identify Ruby keywords. You can just identify what looks like keywords in its own programming language. Now again, I know that Lisp and others also do this stuff. They just do it with the maximum amount of line noise that can ever be crammed into a programming language and Ruby does it at a level where you cannot tell my metaprogramming from Matz’s keywords and with zero line noise.

（01:04:49）当元编程以这种方式使用时，我们就称之为领域特定语言（DSL）。你拿一门通用语言如 Ruby，将其定制化，专门用于某个特定领域——比如在对象层面描述数据库中的关系。这是早期的一个典型例子：你可以写 user has_many :comments，或者 belongs_to :account。前者建立一对多关系，后者则建立一对一关系。Rails 充斥着这类 DSL，有时甚至看起来都不像 Ruby 了——你无法分辨出哪些是 Ruby 的原生关键字，只能识别出那些看起来像是它自己语言中的关键字。当然，我知道 Lisp 等语言也能做到这一点，但它们是以编程语言所能容纳的最大“行噪声”（line noise）方式实现的；而 Ruby 做到的程度，是你根本分不清哪些是我通过元编程添加的，哪些是 Matz 本人设计的关键字，而且完全没有行噪声。

Lex Fridman
(01:05:56) Yeah, I should say that my first love was Lisp. So there’s a slow tear that you can’t see.

Lex Fridman
（01:05:56）是啊，我得说我的初恋是 Lisp。所以此刻我正默默流下一滴你看不见的眼泪。

DHH
(01:06:01) I’ve actually never written any real Lisp myself.

DHH
（01:06:01）其实我自己从未真正写过 Lisp。

Lex Fridman
(01:06:04) Well, how can you judge it so harshly then?

Lex Fridman
（01:06:04）那你凭什么这么严厉地评判它呢？

DHH
(01:06:07) Because I have two eyes and I can look at code and my aesthetic sensibilities forbid me to even go much further, which is the limitation, I know. I should actually dive into Lisp because I’ve found that I’ve learned a lot just diving into, maybe I’m insulting Lisp again here, but the past of programming languages. With Smalltalk, for example, I think Smalltalk is a incredible experiment that also worked but isn’t suitable for today’s programming environments.

DHH
（01:06:07）因为我有双眼，能看代码，而我的审美直觉不允许我再深入下去——我知道这是我的局限。我其实应该去深入学习 Lisp，因为我发现，只要深入探索编程语言的历史（也许我又在冒犯 Lisp 了），就能学到很多东西。比如 Smalltalk，我认为它是一次非凡且成功的实验，但并不适合当今的编程环境。

Dynamic typing

动态类型

Lex Fridman
(01:06:36) I love that we’re talking about Ruby so much and what a beautiful code is and what a beautiful programming language is. So one of the things that is I think implied maybe you made explicit in your descriptions there is that Ruby is dynamic typing versus strict typing. And you have been not just saying that it’s a nice thing, but that you will defend dynamic typing to the death. That freedom is a powerful freedom to preserve.

Lex Fridman
（01:06:36）我很喜欢我们如此深入地讨论 Ruby，讨论什么是优美的代码，什么是优美的编程语言。我想你刚才的描述中隐含（或许也明确表达了）的一点是：Ruby 采用的是动态类型，而非严格（静态）类型。你不仅认为这是件好事，甚至宣称要誓死捍卫动态类型——因为这种自由是一种值得守护的强大自由。

DHH
(01:07:04) It’s the essence of what makes Ruby Ruby. This is why I don’t fully understand when people call for Ruby to add static typing because to me it’s the bedrock of what this is. Why would you want to turn one of the most beautiful languages into something far uglier? This is one of my primary objections to static typing. It’s not just that it limits you in certain ways. It makes metaprogramming harder. I write a bunch of metaprogramming. I’ve seen what it takes to do metaprogramming in TypeScript. That was actually one of the things that just really sent me on a tear of getting meta or getting TypeScript out of some of the projects that I’m involved with.

DHH
（01:07:04）这正是 Ruby 之所以为 Ruby 的核心所在。因此，当有人呼吁给 Ruby 加入静态类型时，我完全无法理解——因为对我而言，动态类型正是这门语言的基石。你为什么要将一门最美的语言变成某种远为丑陋的东西？这是我反对静态类型的主要原因之一。它不仅在某些方面限制了你，还让元编程变得更困难。我写了很多元编程代码，也亲身体验过在 TypeScript 中做元编程有多痛苦。事实上，正是这一点让我下定决心，要把 TypeScript 从我参与的一些项目中彻底移除。

(01:07:42) We pulled TypeScript out of Turbo, one of the front-end frameworks that we have because I tried to write to Metaprogramming in TypeScript and I was just infuriated. I don’t want that experience, but I also don’t want it from an aesthetic point of view. I hate repetition. We’ve just talked about how much I love that Ruby boils all of these expressions.

（01:07:42）我们从 Turbo（我们开发的一个前端框架）中移除了 TypeScript，原因就是我尝试在 TypeScript 中写元编程，结果气得不行。我不想要那种体验，从审美角度来说也完全无法接受。我讨厌重复。我们刚刚谈到，我多么热爱 Ruby 能将所有表达浓缩到极致。

DHH
(01:08:00) … about how much I love that Ruby boils all of these expressions down to its essence. You can’t remove one dot. You can’t remove one character without losing something. This moment you go for static typing, that you declare at least … I know there are ways to do implied typing and so forth, but let’s just take the stereotypical case of an example, for example. Capital U user, I’m declaring the type of the variable. Lowercase user. I’m now naming my variable, equals uppercase user or new uppercase user. I’ve repeated user three times. I don’t have time for this. I don’t have sensibilities for this. I don’t want my Ruby polluted with this. Now, I understand all the arguments for why people like static typing. One of the primary arguments is that it makes tooling easier. It makes it easier to do auto-complete in editors, for example. It makes it easier to find certain kinds of bugs, because maybe you’re calling methods that don’t exist on an object and the editor can actually catch that bug before you even run it. I don’t care.

DHH
（01:08:00）……我多么热爱 Ruby 能将所有表达精炼到其本质——你无法再删掉任何一个点号，无法再删掉任何一个字符而不损失含义。一旦你转向静态类型，比如声明变量类型时——我知道有类型推断等方法，但我们就拿最典型的例子来说：User user = new User();。这里，“User” 出现了三次：第一次声明变量类型，第二次是变量名（小写 user），第三次又是类型（new User）。我可没时间搞这种重复，我的审美也受不了。我不想让我的 Ruby 被这种东西污染。当然，我理解人们喜欢静态类型的各种理由。其中一个主要论点是它让工具链更强大——比如编辑器能更容易实现自动补全，也更容易发现某些类型的错误，比如你调用了对象上不存在的方法，编辑器甚至能在你运行前就捕捉到这个 bug。但我不在乎。

(01:09:11) First of all, I don’t write code with tools, I write them with text editors. I chisel them out of the screen with my bare hands. I don’t auto-complete. This is why I love Ruby so much, and this is why I continue to be in love with the text editor rather than the IDE. I don’t want an IDE. I want my fingers to have to individually type out every element of it, because it will force me to stay in the world where Ruby is beautiful. Because as soon as it gets easy to type a lot of boilerplate, well, guess what? You can have a lot of boilerplate. Every single language basically that has great tooling support has a much higher tolerance for boilerplate because the thinking is, well, you’re not typing it anyway, you’re just auto- completing it. I don’t want that at all. I want something where the fabric I’m working in is just a text file, there’s nothing else to it. So these things play together. There’s the aesthetic part, there’s the tooling part, there’s the meta-programming part.

（01:09:11）首先，我不是用“工具”写代码，而是用纯文本编辑器写的。我是亲手在屏幕上一点点“凿”出代码的，从不依赖自动补全。这正是我如此深爱 Ruby 的原因，也是我始终钟情于文本编辑器而非 IDE 的原因。我不想要 IDE。我希望我的手指必须逐字敲出每一个元素，因为这会迫使我停留在 Ruby 美丽的世界里。因为一旦编写大量样板代码变得轻而易举，那会发生什么？你就会真的写出大量样板代码。几乎所有拥有强大工具支持的语言，对样板代码的容忍度都高得多——因为人们觉得：“反正你也不用手敲，都是自动补全的嘛。”但我完全不要这样。我想要的是一种工作媒介，它就是一份纯文本文件，别无他物。因此，这些因素是相互关联的：有审美层面，有工具层面，也有元编程层面。
(01:10:16) There’s the fact that Ruby’s ethos of duck typing … I don’t know if you’ve heard that term before. It’s essentially not about, can I call this method if an object is of a certain class? Can I call this method if the method responds? It’s very out of small talk in that regard. You don’t actually check whether that class has the method, which allows you to dynamically add methods at runtime and do all sorts of really interesting things that underpin all the beautiful meta-programming that we do in Ruby. I don’t want to lose any of that and I don’t care for the benefits. One of the benefits I’ve seen touted over and over again is that it’s much easier to write correct software. You’re going to have fewer bugs. You’re going to have less Null Pointer Exceptions, you’re going to have less of all of this stuff. Yeah, I don’t have any of that. It’s just not something that occurs in my standard mode of operation. I’m not saying I don’t have bugs, of course I do, but I catch those bugs with unit testing, with integration testing.

(01:10:16) 有一个事实是，Ruby 的“鸭子类型”（duck typing）理念……我不知道你以前是否听说过这个术语。它的核心并不是“如果一个对象属于某个特定类，我能不能调用这个方法？”，而是“只要对象能响应这个方法，我就能调用它”。在这方面，它完全跳脱了琐碎的类型检查。你实际上并不需要检查那个类是否拥有该方法，这让你可以在运行时动态地添加方法，并实现各种非常有趣的功能——这些正是 Ruby 中那些优美元编程的基础。我不想失去这些特性，也并不看重（静态类型带来的）那些所谓好处。我反复听到的一个好处是：使用静态类型能更容易写出正确的软件，你会遇到更少的 bug，更少的空指针异常（Null Pointer Exceptions），以及其他类似问题。但说实话，我根本没遇到过这些问题。在我的常规开发模式中，这些根本不会发生。我不是说我没有 bug——当然有，但我通过单元测试和集成测试来捕获它们。

(01:11:19) Those are the kinds of precautions that will catch logical bugs, things that compile but are wrong, along with the uncompilable stuff. So I’ve never been drawn into this world, and part of it is because I work on a certain class of systems. I fully accept that. If you’re writing systems that have five, 10, 50 million lines of code with hundreds, thousands or tens of thousands of programmers, I fully accept that you need different methods. What I object to is the idea that what’s right for a code base of 10 million lines of code, with 100,000 programmers working on it, is also the same thing I should be using in my bedroom to create Basecamp, because I’m just a single individual. That’s complete nonsense. In the real world, we would know that that makes no sense at all. That you don’t, I don’t know, use your Pagani to go pick up groceries at Costco. It’s a bad vehicle for that. It just doesn’t have the space, you don’t want to muddy the beautiful seats. You don’t want to do any of those things.

(01:11:19) 这些预防措施能捕捉到逻辑错误——那些能编译通过但行为错误的代码，以及无法编译的代码。因此，我从未被吸引进入（静态类型）那个世界，部分原因是我所开发的系统属于某一特定类别。我完全承认这一点：如果你在编写拥有五百万、一千万甚至五千万行代码的系统，并且有数百、数千甚至数万名程序员参与，那么你确实需要不同的方法。但我反对的是这样一种观念：适用于一千万行代码、十万人协作的代码库的方法，也应该是我在卧室里独自开发 Basecamp 时应该采用的方法。这完全是胡说八道。在现实世界中，我们都知道这毫无道理——就像你不会开着你的帕加尼（Pagani）去好市多（Costco）买菜一样。那辆车根本不适合干这种事：它没有足够的空间，你也不想弄脏那些精美的座椅，更不想做任何会损害它的事。

(01:12:21) We know that certain things that are very good in certain domains don’t apply to all. In programming languages, it seems like we forget that. Now, to be fair, I also had a little bit perhaps of a reputation of forgetting that. When I first learned Ruby, I was so head over heels in love with this programming language that I almost found it unconceivable that anyone would choose any other programming language at all to write web applications. I kind of engaged the evangelism of Ruby on Rails in that spirit as a crusade, as, I just need to teach you the gospel. I just need to show you this conditional code that we just talked about, and you will convert at the point of a sharp argument. Now, I learned that’s not the way, and part of the reason it’s not the way is that programmers think differently. Our brains are configured differently. My brain is configured perfectly for Ruby, perfectly for a dynamically duck-typed language that I can chisel code out of a text editor with.

(01:12:21) 我们知道，某些在特定领域表现极佳的东西，并不适用于所有场景。但在编程语言领域，我们似乎忘了这一点。公平地说，我自己过去可能也有点这样的名声——当我第一次接触 Ruby 时，我对这门语言爱得如此深沉，以至于几乎无法想象有人会选择其他任何语言来开发 Web 应用。我当时以一种近乎传教士般的热情推广 Ruby on Rails，觉得我只需要向你传授“福音”，只需向你展示刚才提到的那种条件代码，你就会在我犀利的论证下立刻皈依。但后来我意识到，事情并非如此。其中一个原因就是程序员的思维方式各不相同——我们的大脑构造不同。我的大脑天生就非常适合 Ruby，非常适合这种动态鸭子类型的语言，让我能直接从文本编辑器中“雕刻”出代码。

Scaling

扩展性

(01:13:22) Other people need the security of an IDE. They want the security of classes that won’t compile unless you call the methods on it. I have come to accept that, but most programmers don’t. They’re still stuck in essentially, I like static typing. Therefore, static typing is the only way to create reliable, correct systems. Which is just such a mind-blowing, to be blunt, idiotic thing to say in the face of mountains of evidence to the contrary. This is one of the reasons I’m so in love with Shopify as the flagship application for Ruby on Rails. Shopify exists at a scale that most programmers will never touch. On Black Friday, I think Shopify did one million requests per second. That’s not one million requests of images, that’s of dynamic requests that are funneling through the pipeline of commerce. I mean, Shopify runs something like 30% of all E-commerce stores on the damn Internet. A huge portion of all commerce in total runs through Shopify and that runs on Ruby on Rails. So Ruby on Rails is able to scale up to that level without using static typing in all of what it does.

(01:13:22) 其他人则需要 IDE 提供的安全感，他们希望类在方法调用不正确时就无法编译。我已经接受了这一点，但大多数程序员还没有。他们仍然固守着一种观念：“我喜欢静态类型，因此静态类型是构建可靠、正确系统的唯一方式。”坦率地说，在大量反例面前还坚持这种说法，简直令人难以置信地愚蠢。这也是我如此钟爱 Shopify 作为 Ruby on Rails 旗舰应用的原因之一。Shopify 所达到的规模，是绝大多数程序员一辈子都接触不到的。我记得在黑色星期五，Shopify 每秒处理了约一百万次请求。这可不是一百万次图片请求，而是通过整个商业流水线处理的一百万次动态请求。事实上，Shopify 支撑着互联网上大约 30% 的电商店铺，大量的全球商业交易都流经 Shopify——而这一切都是建立在 Ruby on Rails 之上的。这说明 Ruby on Rails 完全可以在不依赖静态类型的情况下，支撑起如此庞大的系统。

(01:14:45) Now, I know they’ve done certain experiments in certain ways, because they are hitting some of the limits that you will hit with dynamic typing. Some of those limits you hit with dynamic typing are actually, by the way, just limits you hit when you write 5 million lines of code. I think the Shopify monolith is about 5 million lines of code. At that scale, everything breaks because you’re at the frontier of what humans are capable of doing with programming languages. The difference in part is that Ruby is such a succinct language that those 5 million, if they had been written in, let’s just say Go or Java, would have been 50 or 25. Now, that might have alleviated some of the problems that you have when you work on huge systems with many programmers, but it certainly would also have compounded them; try to understand 25 million lines of code.

(01:14:45) 当然，我知道他们也在某些方面做过一些实验，因为他们确实碰到了动态类型所带来的某些限制。但顺便说一句，其中一些限制其实并不是动态类型独有的——而是当你写了五百万行代码时自然会遇到的瓶颈。我认为 Shopify 的单体应用大约有五百万行代码。在这个规模下，一切都会崩溃，因为你已经站在了人类使用编程语言所能处理的极限边缘。部分区别在于，Ruby 是一门极其简洁的语言——如果这五百万行代码是用 Go 或 Java 写的，可能会膨胀到两千五百万甚至五千万行。虽然那样或许能缓解大型团队协作中的一些问题，但也肯定会加剧另一些问题；试想一下，你要理解两千五百万行代码！

Lex Fridman

Lex Fridman

(01:15:33) So the thing does scale. That’s a persistent myth, that it doesn’t scale, Shopify, and others, but Shopify I think is a great example. By the way, I love Shopify and I love Toby.

(01:15:33) 所以它确实是可扩展的。“Ruby on Rails 无法扩展”是一个长期存在的误解，而 Shopify 等案例恰恰反驳了这一点，我认为 Shopify 就是个绝佳的例子。顺便说一句，我很喜欢 Shopify，也很欣赏 Toby。
