(01:16:18) 的确如此，而且非常深入。关于 Toby 有个有趣的故事：他在 2000 年代中期曾是 Rails 核心团队的成员。Toby 本人——

Lex Fridman

Lex Fridman

(01:16:28) Really?

(01:16:28) 真的吗？

DHH

DHH

(01:16:28) … wrote Active Merchant, which is one of the frameworks for creating shops. He wrote the Liquid templating language that Shopify still uses to this day. He has a huge list of contributions to the Rails ecosystem and he’s the CEO of the company. I think it’s very inspiring to me, because it’s such at the opposite end of what I like to do. I like to chisel code with my own hands most of the day, he runs a company of almost 10,000 people. That is literally, world commerce depends on it, a level of criticality I can’t even begin to understand. Yet, we can see eye to eye on so many of these fundamental questions in computer science and program development. That is a dynamic range, to be able to encompass Rails, being a great tool for the one developer who’s just starting out with an idea … who don’t even fully know everything, who is right at the level where PHP would have been a good fit in those late ’90s. Because yeah, I can probably upload something to an FTP server and so on.

(01:16:28) ……他开发了 Active Merchant，这是一个用于构建商店的框架。他还创造了 Liquid 模板语言，Shopify 至今仍在使用。他对 Rails 生态系统有着大量贡献，而如今他却是这家公司的 CEO。这对我来说非常鼓舞人心，因为他的工作方式与我截然相反：我整天喜欢亲手“雕刻”代码，而他却管理着近一万人的公司——全世界的商业运转在某种程度上都依赖于它，那种关键程度是我根本无法想象的。然而，我们在计算机科学和程序开发的许多根本问题上却看法一致。这正体现了 Rails 的强大动态范围：它既是一位刚刚萌生想法、甚至还不完全了解所有技术的新手开发者的好工具——那种在 90 年代末可能选择 PHP 的开发者（因为那时你只要能上传文件到 FTP 服务器就行）……

(01:17:33) Rails does have more complexity than that, but it also has so much longer runway. The runway goes all the way to goddamn Shopify. That is about the most convincing argument I can make for dynamic range, that we can do a lot of it. And even having said that, Shopify is the outlier of course. I don’t think about Shopify as the primary target when I write Rails, I think of the single developer. Actually, I do think about Shopify, but I don’t think about Shopify now. I think of Shopify when Toby was writing Snow Devil, which was the first E-commerce store to sell snowboards that he created. That was the pre-Shopify Shopify he created all by himself. And that was possible because Ruby on Rails isn’t just about beautiful code, it’s just as much about productivity. It’s just as much about the impact that an individual programmer is able to have.

(01:17:33) Rails 确实比那种场景更复杂一些，但它也拥有远为广阔的延展空间——这条跑道一路延伸到了该死的 Shopify。这大概是我能给出的关于“动态范围”最有力的论据：Rails 能做到非常多的事情。当然，即便如此，Shopify 依然是个特例。我在设计 Rails 时，并不把 Shopify 这样的巨头当作主要目标，我考虑的是单个开发者。其实我也确实会想到 Shopify，但不是现在的 Shopify，而是当年 Toby 一个人写 Snow Devil 的时候——那是他创建的第一个售卖滑雪板的电商网站，也就是 Shopify 的前身。他完全是靠自己完成了这一切。而这之所以可能，正是因为 Ruby on Rails 不仅仅关乎优美的代码，同样重要的是生产力，以及单个程序员所能产生的巨大影响力。
(01:18:24) That they can build system where they can keep the whole thing in their head and be able to move it forward, such that you can go from one developer sitting and working on something … and that something is Shopify, and it turns into what it is today. When we talk about programming languages and we compare them, we often compare them at a very late stage. Like, what is the better programming language for, let’s say Twitter in 2009 when it’s already a huge success? Twitter was started on Ruby on Rails. They then hit some scaling problems, it was a big debacle at the time. They end up then I think writing it in some other language, which by the way I think is the best advertisement ever for Ruby on Rails, because nothing fucking happened for 10 years after they switched over, essentially zero innovation. Some of that was because they were doing a long conversion, and all of the early success in part came because they had the agility to quickly change and adopt and so forth. That’s what startups need. That’s what Shopify needed, that’s what Twitter needed.
(01:18:24) 他们能够构建一个系统，把整个东西都装在脑子里，并推动它向前发展——比如最初只有一位开发者坐在那里开发某个东西……而那个东西就是 Shopify，最终成长为今天的样子。当我们谈论编程语言并进行比较时，常常是在非常后期的阶段才做对比。比如说，2009 年的 Twitter 已经取得了巨大成功，那么当时哪种编程语言更好？Twitter 最初是用 Ruby on Rails 构建的，后来遇到了一些扩展性问题，当时闹得沸沸扬扬。最终，我记得他们改用了另一种语言。顺便说一句，我认为这恰恰是对 Ruby on Rails 最好的广告，因为在他们切换之后的十年里几乎什么都没发生，基本上毫无创新。部分原因在于他们花了很长时间进行迁移，而早期的成功很大程度上正是因为他们具备快速变更、快速适应等敏捷能力。这正是初创公司所需要的，也是 Shopify 和 Twitter 所需要的。

(01:19:24) That’s what everyone needs, and that’s the number one priority for Ruby on Rails, to make sure that we don’t lose that. Because what happens so often when development tools and programming language are driven by huge companies, is that they mirror their org chart, React and everything else needed to use that, is in some ways a reflection of how Meta builds Facebook. Because of course it is, because of course it’s an distraction of that. I’m not saying React isn’t a great tool and that can’t used by smaller teams, of course it can, but it’s born in a very different context than something like Ruby on Rails.
(01:19:24) 这是所有团队都需要的，也是 Ruby on Rails 的首要任务——确保我们不会失去这种敏捷性。因为当开发工具和编程语言由大型公司主导时，往往会出现一种情况：它们会反映公司的组织架构。React 以及使用它所需的一切，在某种程度上就反映了 Meta 是如何构建 Facebook 的。当然会这样，因为它本质上就是这种环境下的产物。我并不是说 React 不是一个优秀的工具，也不是说小团队不能用它——当然可以用——但它诞生的背景与 Ruby on Rails 这类框架截然不同。

Lex Fridman
(01:20:00) Let me say as a small aside … because I think we might return to Shopify and celebrate it often, just a personal note. This particular podcast has way more sponsors, and sponsors that want to be sponsors, than I could possibly ever have. It’s really, really important for me to not give a shit and to be able to celebrate people. I celebrate people, I celebrate companies, and I don’t care that they’re sponsoring. I really don’t care. I just want to make that very explicit, because we’re going to continue saying positive things about Shopify. I don’t care, stop sponsoring, it doesn’t really matter to me. Yeah, I just want to make that explicit. But to linger on the scaling thing with the Twitter and the Shopify, can you just explain to me what Shopify is doing with the JIT? What did they have to try to do to scale this thing, because that’s kind of an incredible story, right?
Lex Fridman
(01:20:00) 容我插一句题外话……因为我们可能会反复回到 Shopify 并经常赞美它，这里先做个个人说明。这个播客收到的赞助商数量，以及想要成为赞助商的人数，远远超过我能接受的极限。对我来说，最重要的是毫不在意这些，而能自由地赞美他人。我会赞美人，也会赞美公司，我不在乎他们是否是赞助商。我真的不在乎。我只是想明确说明这一点，因为我们接下来还会继续对 Shopify 说好话。我不在乎，就算你们停止赞助，对我来说也无所谓。是的，我只是想把这点说清楚。不过回到 Twitter 和 Shopify 的扩展性话题，你能给我解释一下 Shopify 在 JIT（即时编译）方面做了什么吗？他们为了扩展系统到底尝试了哪些措施？因为这确实是个相当了不起的故事，对吧？

DHH
(01:20:59) Yeah. One of the great contributions that Shopify has made to the entire Ruby ecosystem … not just Rails, but in particular Rails, is YJIT. YJIT is their compiler for Ruby that just makes everything a lot more efficient. At Shopify scale, eking out even a five, 10% improvement in Ruby’s overhead and execution time is a huge deal. Now, Shopify didn’t need YJIT. Shopify was already running on the initial version of Ruby that was I think 10 times slower than what we have today, if you look back upon the Ruby 186 that Toby probably started on, just as I started on. That was enough to propel Shopify to the scale that it has today. A lot of the scaling conversation is lost in a failure to distinguish two things. Scale is one package we talk about when there are really multiple packages inside of it. One is runtime performance, latency, how fast can you execute a single request? Can it happen fast enough that the user will not notice? If your Rails request takes a second and a half to execute, the user’s going to notice. Your app is going to feel slow and sluggish.
DHH
(01:20:59) 是的。Shopify 对整个 Ruby 生态系统——不仅限于 Rails，尤其是 Rails——做出的一大贡献就是 YJIT。YJIT 是他们为 Ruby 开发的编译器，能让一切运行得更高效。在 Shopify 这样的规模下，哪怕只是将 Ruby 的开销和执行时间优化 5% 到 10%，都是意义重大的事情。但其实 Shopify 并不需要 YJIT。回溯到 Toby（可能）最初使用的 Ruby 1.8.6 版本（我自己也是从那个版本开始的），那版 Ruby 比今天的版本慢了大概十倍，但 Shopify 依然靠它发展到了如今的规模。许多关于“扩展性”的讨论之所以混乱，是因为人们没能区分两个不同的概念。“扩展性”听起来像是一个整体，但实际上它包含多个维度。其一是运行时性能和延迟：单个请求执行得多快？快到用户察觉不到吗？如果你的 Rails 请求需要 1.5 秒才能完成，用户一定会注意到，你的应用就会显得缓慢而迟钝。

(01:22:16) You have to get that response time down below, let’s say at least 300 milliseconds. I like to target a 100 milliseconds as my latency. That kind of performance, how much performance of that kind of latency can you squeeze out of a single CPU core? That tells you something about what the price of a single request will be. But then whether you can deal with one million requests a second, like Shopify is doing right now, if you have one box that can do 1,000 requests a second, you just need X boxes to get up to a million. What you’ll actually find is that when it comes to programming languages, they’re all the same in this way. They all scale, largely, beautifully horizontally, you just add more boxes. The hard parts of scaling a Shopify is typically not the programming language, it’s the database. That’s actually one of the challenges that Shopify has now is, how do you deal with MySQL at the scale that they’re operating at? When do you need to move to other databases to get worldwide performance? All of these things. The questions about scaling Ruby are economic questions.
(01:22:16) 你必须把响应时间压到至少 300 毫秒以下。我个人喜欢以 100 毫秒作为延迟目标。这种性能水平——即单个 CPU 核心能榨取出多少这样的低延迟性能——决定了单次请求的成本。但另一方面，像 Shopify 现在每秒处理一百万次请求，如果你有一台服务器每秒能处理 1000 次请求，那你只需要 X 台服务器就能达到百万级。事实上你会发现，在这方面，所有编程语言都差不多：它们基本上都能通过横向扩展（增加更多服务器）实现良好的扩展性。Shopify 扩展性的真正难点通常不在编程语言本身，而在数据库。这实际上是 Shopify 当前面临的挑战之一：在如此巨大的规模下，该如何处理 MySQL？什么时候需要迁移到其他数据库才能实现全球范围的高性能？诸如此类的问题。因此，关于 Ruby 扩展性的讨论，本质上是经济层面的问题。

(01:23:28) If we’re spending so-and- so much on application servers, if we can get just 5% more performance out of Ruby, well, we could save 5% of those servers and that could filter down into the budget. Now, that analysis concludes into basically one thing, Ruby is a luxury language. It’s a luxury, the highest luxury, in my opinion. It is the Coco Chanel of programming languages, something that not everyone can afford, and I mean this in the best possible way. There are some applications on the Internet where each request has so little value, you can’t afford to use a luxurious language like Ruby to program in it. You simply have to slum it with a C or a Go or some other low-level language, or a Rust, talk about line noise there.
(01:23:28) 如果我们在应用服务器上投入了大量资金，而 Ruby 能再提升 5% 的性能，那我们就能节省 5% 的服务器，进而影响整体预算。这种分析最终指向一个结论：Ruby 是一门奢侈的语言。在我看来，它是最高级别的奢侈——是编程语言中的香奈儿（Coco Chanel），并非人人都用得起，而我是怀着最大的敬意这么说的。互联网上确实存在一些应用场景，每次请求的价值极低，根本负担不起使用 Ruby 这样“奢侈”的语言来开发。你只能退而求其次，用 C、Go 或其他底层语言，或者 Rust——说到 Rust，那代码简直像噪声一样难读。

Lex Fridman
(01:24:17) That’s like the thrift store of languages.
Lex Fridman
(01:24:17) 那就像是语言界的二手商店。

DHH
(01:24:19) Exactly. What you need, you need a very low level to do it. You can’t afford to use a luxury language to build it with. That’s not true of Shopify. It wasn’t true of Basecamp even back in 2004. It’s not been true of 99% of all web applications ever created because the main cost component of 99% of web applications, it’s not CPU cores. It’s web cores, it’s human cores. It’s human capacity to understand and involve systems. It’s their personal productivity. I did a calculation once when someone had for the 400th time said, “Oh, if you switch from Ruby to some faster language, you could save a bunch of money.” I calculated it out that at the time … and I think the last time I did this calculation was almost a decade ago, we were spending about 15% of our operating budget on Ruby application servers. So for me, to improve my cost profile of the business by seven percentage points, I’d have to pick something twice as fast. That’s quite hard.
DHH
(01:24:19) 没错。有些场景确实需要非常底层的语言来实现，你负担不起用奢侈语言去构建。但这对 Shopify 来说并不成立，甚至早在 2004 年对 Basecamp 也不成立。事实上，99% 的 Web 应用程序都不属于这种情况，因为它们的主要成本并非来自 CPU 核心，而是“Web 核心”——也就是“人类核心”：人类理解和参与系统的能力，以及他们的个人生产力。我曾经做过一次计算，当时有人第 400 次对我说：“如果你从 Ruby 切换到某种更快的语言，就能省下一大笔钱。” 我算了一下——我记得上次做这个计算差不多是十年前——当时我们在 Ruby 应用服务器上的支出约占运营预算的 15%。也就是说，如果我想把业务成本结构改善 7 个百分点，就得选择一个速度翻倍的语言。这非常困难。

(01:25:27) Versus, if Ruby and Ruby on Rails was even 10% more productive than something else, I would move the needle far more, because making individual programmers more productive actually matters a lot more. This is why people are so excited about AI. This is why they’re freaking out over the fact that a single programmer in Silicon Valley, who makes $300,000 a year, can now do the work of three or five, at least in theory. I haven’t actually seen that fully in practice. But let’s just assume the theory is correct, if not now, then in six months, that’s a huge deal. That matters so much more than whether you can squeeze a few more cycles out of the CPU when it comes to these kinds of business applications. If you’re making Unreal Engine rendering stuff, like Tim Sweeney you had on, yeah, he needs to really sweat all those details. The Nanite engine can’t run on Ruby. It’s never going to, it was not meant for that, fine. These kinds of business applications absolutely can.
(01:25:27) 相比之下，如果 Ruby 和 Ruby on Rails 能比其他技术栈提升哪怕 10% 的开发效率，对我的影响要大得多，因为提升单个程序员的生产力才是真正关键。这也是为什么人们对 AI 如此兴奋——他们之所以焦虑，是因为理论上，一位年薪 30 万美元的硅谷程序员现在可以完成三到五个人的工作量。虽然我在实践中还没完全看到这种效果，但假设这个理论是正确的（即使现在不成立，六个月后也可能成立），那将是巨大的变革。对于这类商业应用来说，这远比从 CPU 中榨取几个额外的时钟周期重要得多。当然，如果你在做像 Tim Sweeney（你之前采访过的那位）那样的 Unreal Engine 渲染引擎，那他确实需要斤斤计较每一个细节。Nanite 引擎不可能用 Ruby 实现，也永远不会，因为它本来就不是为这种场景设计的——这没问题。但这类商业应用完全可以使用 Ruby。

(01:26:25) And everything people are excited about AI for right now, that extra capacity to just do more, that was why we were excited about Ruby back in the early 2000s. It was because I saw that if we could even squeeze out a 10% improvement of the human programmer, we’d be able to do so much more for so much less.
(01:26:25) 如今人们对 AI 的所有期待——那种“能做更多事”的额外能力——正是我们在 2000 年代初对 Ruby 感到兴奋的原因。因为我意识到，只要能让程序员的效率提升 10%，我们就能用少得多的资源完成多得多的事情。

Future of programming

未来编程

Lex Fridman

Lex Fridman
(01:26:47) We probably argue about this, but I really like working together with AI, collaborating with AI. I would argue that the kind of code you want AI to generate is human-readable, human interpretable. If it’s generating pro golf code, it’s not a collaboration. So it has to be speaking the human … it’s not just, you’re writing the prompts in English, you also want to read the responses in the human-interpretable language at Ruby, right? Because you’ve said that for you the sculptor, the elitist Coco Chanel sculptor, you want on your fancy keyboard to type every single letter yourself with your own fingers. （01:26:47）我们可能会就此争论，但我真的很喜欢与AI一起工作、与AI协作。我认为，你希望AI生成的代码应该是人类可读、人类可理解的。如果它生成的是“职业高尔夫球手级别”的晦涩代码，那就不是协作了。因此，它必须使用人类的语言……不仅仅是你用英文写提示词，你也希望用像Ruby这样人类可理解的语言来阅读AI的回应，对吧？这实际上对AI也有好处。因为你曾说过，对你这位雕塑家——那位精英主义的Coco Chanel式雕塑家而言——你希望在自己那把精美的键盘上，亲手敲下每一个字母。但Ruby的好处也体现在：即使代码是由AI写出来的，当你用自己的手指去编辑它时，你依然能与之互动，因为它具备人类可读性。

DHH
DHH

(01:27:47) The paradigm I really love with this was something Elon actually said on one of your shows when you guys were talking about Neuralink, that Neuralink allows the bandwidth between you and the machine to increase. That language, either spoken or written, is very low bandwidth. If you are to calculate just how many bits we can exchange as we’re sitting here, it’s very slow. Ruby has a much higher bandwidth of communication, revealed, conveys so much more concept per character than most other programming languages do. So when you are collaborating with AI, you want really high bandwidth. You want it to be able to produce programs with you, whether you’re letting it write the code or not, that both of you can actually understand really quickly. And that you could compress a grand concept, a grand system into far fewer parts that both of you can understand. Now, I actually love collaborating with AI too. I love chiseling my code, and the way I use AI is in a separate window. I don’t let it drive my code. I’ve tried that. I’ve tried the Cursors and the Windsurfs and I don’t enjoy that way of writing.
（01:27:47）我特别喜欢的一个范式，其实是Elon在你们某期节目聊Neuralink时提到的：Neuralink能提升你与机器之间的带宽。无论是口头还是书面语言，其带宽都非常低。如果我们坐在这里计算一下彼此能交换多少比特的信息，就会发现速度非常慢。而Ruby则具有高得多的通信带宽——它每个字符所传达的概念远比大多数其他编程语言丰富得多。因此，当你与AI协作时，你需要极高的带宽。你希望AI能和你一起产出程序——无论你是否让它写代码——这些程序必须能让你们双方都能迅速理解。你甚至能把一个宏大的概念、一个庞大的系统，压缩成更少的部分，而你们双方都能理解。事实上，我也非常喜欢与AI协作。我喜欢雕琢自己的代码，而我使用AI的方式是在另一个窗口里。我不会让它主导我的代码。我试过那样做，试过Cursor和Windsurf这类工具，但我不享受那种写作方式。

(01:29:03) One of the reasons I don’t enjoy that way of writing is, I can literally feel competence draining out of my fingers. That level of immediacy with the material disappears. Because what I was doing was setting up a Linux machine, that’s basically what Bash was designed for. It’s a great constraint. But what I found myself doing was asking AI for the same way of expressing a conditional, for example, in Bash over and over again. That by not typing it, I wasn’t learning it. I was using it, I was getting the expression I wanted, but I wasn’t learning it. I got a little scared.
（01:29:03）我不喜欢那种写作方式的原因之一是，我能真切地感受到能力正从我的指尖流失。那种与材料直接接触的即时感消失了。我感受最深的一次，是我刚转到Linux时做了一个叫Omakub的Ubuntu改版。它完全是用Bash写的。我之前从未认真写过Bash代码，所以当时就用AI来协作，让它帮我写大量Bash脚本，因为我确实需要这些功能。我知道自己想要什么，也能用Ruby表达出来，但我觉得通过Bash来实现是个有趣的挑战——毕竟我做的事情就是配置一台Linux机器，而这正是Bash的设计初衷，是个很棒的约束条件。但我发现自己反复向AI询问如何在Bash中表达同一个条件语句。因为没有亲手敲这些代码，我就没能真正学会它。我虽然用上了想要的表达方式，却没有掌握它。这让我有点害怕。

(01:30:08) I got a little scared, is this the end of learning? Am I no longer learning if I’m not typing? The way I, for me, recast that was, I don’t want to give up on the AI. It’s such a better experience as a programmer to look up APIs, to get a second opinion on something, to do a draft, but I have to do the typing myself because you learn with your fingers. If you’re learning how to play the guitar, you can watch as many YouTube videos as you want, you’re not going to learn the guitar. You have to put your fingers on the strings to actually learn the motions. I think there is a parallel here to programming, where programming has to be learned in part by the actual typing.
（01:30:08）我有点害怕：这是学习的终结吗？如果我不再亲手打字，我就不再学习了吗？我后来重新思考这个问题：我不想放弃AI。作为程序员，用AI查API、获取第二意见、起草初稿，体验确实好得多。但我必须自己动手打字，因为你是用手指学习的。学吉他时，你看再多YouTube视频也学不会弹吉他，你必须把手指放在琴弦上，才能真正掌握那些动作。我认为编程也是如此——编程的一部分必须通过实际打字来习得。

Even though we have great renditions of all the best songs, as I said, there are millions of people who love to play the guitar. It may no longer have as much economic value as it once did. I think that I’m quite convinced is true, that we perhaps have seen the peak.
（01:39:05）当然，最妙的是，我们只需要等五分钟。Anthropic 的 CEO 似乎认为，到今年年底，90% 的代码都将由 AI 编写。我对这种说法持相当程度的怀疑，但我愿意接受这样一种可能性：手动编程未来可能会变成骑马——变成一种休闲活动，而不是像过去那样作为在洛杉矶出行的交通工具。你不会真的骑着马去超市，然后把从 Whole Foods 买的东西塞进马鞍袋里。这种事情早已不复存在了。手动编程的未来或许就是这样，完全有可能。但我其实并不在意。正如我之前所说，即便我们已经有了所有经典歌曲的完美演绎版本，世界上仍有数以百万计的人热爱弹吉他。弹吉他或许不再像过去那样具有经济价值了。我相当确信这一点：我们可能已经见证了它的巅峰时期。

(01:40:01) Now, I understand the paradox, when the price of something goes down, actually the overall usage goes up, and total spend on that activity goes up. That could also happen maybe. But what we’re seeing right now is that a lot of the big shops, a lot of the big companies, are not hiring like they were five years ago. They’re not anticipating they’re going to need tons more programmers. Controversially, Toby actually put out a memo inside of Shopify asking everyone who’s considering hiring someone to ask the question, could this be done by AI? Now, he’s further ahead on this question than I am. I look at some of the code and [trenches 01:40:37] and I go like, I’d love to use AI more, and I see how it’s making us more productive. But it’s not yet at the level where I just go like, oh, we have this project, let me just give it to the AI agent and it’s going to go off and do it.
（01:40:01）我理解其中的悖论：当某样东西的价格下降时，整体使用量反而会上升，人们在该活动上的总支出也可能增加。这种情况或许也会发生。但我们现在看到的是，许多大型公司、大厂的招聘力度已远不如五年前。它们不再预期自己会需要大量新增程序员。颇具争议的是，Toby 甚至在 Shopify 内部发了一份备忘录，要求所有考虑招聘新员工的人都先问一句：“这件事能不能交给 AI 来做？”在这方面，他比我走得更远。当我审视一些代码和[实际开发场景]时，我会想：“我真希望能更多地使用 AI”，我也确实看到了它如何提升我们的生产力。但它目前还没达到那种程度——让我可以轻松地说：“哦，我们有这个项目，直接交给 AI 代理去做吧，它自己就能搞定。”

Lex Fridman
(01:40:47) But let’s just be honest, you’re like a Clint Eastwood type character cowboy on a horse seeing cars going around. You’re like, well-
Lex Fridman
（01:40:47）但咱们坦白说吧，你就像克林特·伊斯特伍德演的那种骑在马上的牛仔，看着汽车在周围飞驰。你会想：“嗯……”

DHH
(01:40:56) That’s part of it. I think it is important to have that humility, that what you are good at may no longer be what society values. This has happened a million times in history … that you could have been exceptionally good at saddle making, for example. That’s something that a lot of people used to care about because everyone rode a horse. And then suddenly riding a horse became this niche hobby, that there’s some people care about it, but not nearly as many. That’s okay. Now, the other thing of this is, I’ve had the good fortune to have been a programmer for nearly 30 years. That’s a great run. I try to look at life in this way, that I’ve already been blessed with decades of economically viable, highly valuable ways of translating what I like best in the working world, to write Ruby code. That that was so valuable that I could make millions and millions of dollars doing it, and if that’s over tomorrow, I shouldn’t look at that with regret. I should look at it with gratitude.
DHH
（01:40:56）这确实是其中一部分。我认为保持谦逊很重要——你擅长的东西，社会可能不再重视了。历史上这种事情发生过无数次……比如，你可能曾是一位极其出色的马鞍匠。在过去，很多人都在乎这个，因为人人都骑马。但突然之间，骑马变成了一种小众爱好，只有少数人还在关心，远不如从前。这没关系。另外一点是，我很幸运，做了将近 30 年的程序员。这是一段非常精彩的旅程。我试着这样看待人生：我已经拥有了几十年在经济上可行、极具价值的职业生涯，能够将我最热爱的事情——编写 Ruby 代码——转化为工作。这份技能曾如此宝贵，让我赚取了数以百万计的美元。如果明天这一切就结束了，我不该感到遗憾，而应心怀感激。

Lex Fridman
(01:41:57) But you’re also a highly experienced, brilliant and opinionated human …
Lex Fridman
（01:41:57）但你同时也是一位经验极其丰富、才华横溢且观点鲜明的人……

Lex Fridman
(01:42:00) Brilliant and opinionated human being. So it’s really interesting to get your opinion on the future of the horse because there’s a lot of young people listening to this who love programming or who are excited by the possibility of building stuff with software, with Ruby on Rails, that kind of language and now the possibility.
Lex Fridman
（01:42:00）一位才华横溢且观点鲜明的人。因此，听听你对“马的未来”——也就是手动编程未来的看法，真的很有意思。因为有很多年轻人正在收听这段对话，他们热爱编程，或者对用软件、用 Ruby on Rails 这类语言来构建东西的可能性感到兴奋。

DHH
(01:42:24) But is it a career?
DHH
（01:42:24）但它还能是一份职业吗？

Lex Fridman
(01:42:25) Is it a career and how if indeed a single person can build more and more and more with the help of AI, how do they learn that skill? Is this a good skill to learn? I mean, that to me is the real mystery here because I think it’s still absolutely true that you have to learn how to program from scratch currently, but how do you balance those two skills? Because I too, as I’m thinking now, there is a scary slipping away of skill that happens in a matter of really minutes on a particular piece of code. It’s scary the way driving when you have a car drive for you doesn’t quite slip away that fast. So that really scares me. When somebody comes up to me and asks me how do I learn to program? I don’t know what the advice is because I think it’s not enough to just use Cursor or Copilot to generate code.
Lex Fridman
（01:42:25）它还能是一份职业吗？如果一个人确实能在 AI 的帮助下独自构建越来越多的东西，那他们该如何习得这项技能？这还是值得学习的技能吗？对我来说，这才是真正的谜题。因为我认为目前依然绝对正确的一点是：你必须从零开始学习编程。但你该如何平衡这两种能力呢？因为就连我自己现在也在想：在某段特定代码上，技能的退化速度之快令人恐惧——几分钟之内就可能生疏了。相比之下，当你让汽车自动驾驶时，驾驶技能的退化并没有那么快。这真的让我感到害怕。当有人跑来问我“我该怎么学编程？”时，我甚至不知道该怎么回答，因为我觉得仅仅依靠 Cursor 或 Copilot 来生成代码是远远不够的。

DHH
(01:43:28) It’s absolutely not enough. Not if you want to learn, none of you want to become better at it. If you just become a tap monkey, maybe you’re productive in a second, but then you have to realize, well, can anyone just tap if that’s all we’re doing is just sitting around all day long tapping? Yes, yes, yes, yes, yes. That’s not a marketable skill. Now, I always preface this both to myself and when I speak to others about it, is rule number note one, nobody fucking knows anything. No one can predict even six months ahead.
DHH
（01:43:28）那绝对是不够的——如果你真心想学习、想变得更好。如果你只是变成一个“点按钮的猴子”，也许你能立刻产出成果，但你必须意识到：如果我们的工作整天就是坐在那里不停地点按钮，那任何人都能做到，对吧？是的，是的，是的，是的，是的。但这并不是一项有市场价值的技能。我一直对自己、也对别人强调一点，这也是最重要的原则：根本没人真正知道未来会怎样。没人能准确预测哪怕六个月后的事情。
(01:43:58) Right now, we’re probably at peak AI future hype because we see all the promise, because so much of it is real and so many people have experienced it themselves. This mind-boggling thing that the silicon is thinking in some way that feels eerily reminiscent of humans. I’d actually say the big thing for me wasn’t even ChatGPT, it wasn’t even Claude. It was DeepSeek. Running DeepSeek locally and seeing the think box where it converses with itself about how to formulate the response. I almost wanted to think, is this a gimmick? Is it doing this as a performance for my benefit? But that’s not actually how it thinks. If this is how it actually thinks. Okay, I’m a little scared. This is incredibly human how it thinks in this way, but where does that go?

Future of AI

AI的未来

(01:43:58) 现在，我们可能正处于对AI未来最狂热的炒作阶段，因为我们看到了所有潜力——其中很多是真实的，而且很多人已经亲身体验过。这种令人难以置信的现象：硅基芯片以某种方式“思考”，其感觉诡异得像人类一样。我甚至要说，对我而言，真正震撼我的不是ChatGPT，也不是Claude，而是DeepSeek。我在本地运行DeepSeek时，看到那个“思考框”里它自己和自己对话，讨论该如何组织回答。我几乎忍不住想：这是不是一种噱头？是不是为了让我觉得它聪明而特意表演给我看的？但事实并非如此——如果这真的是它思考的方式，那我就有点害怕了。它这样思考的方式实在太像人类了，但接下来会走向何方呢？

So in ’95, one of my favorite movies, one of my favorite B movies came out, The Lawnmower Man.

所以1995年，我最喜欢的一部电影、也是我最爱的B级片之一《割草人》上映了。

Lex Fridman
(01:44:56) Great movie,

Lex Fridman
(01:44:56) 好电影，

DHH
(01:44:57) Incredible movie about virtual reality. Being an avatar and living in VR, the story was a mess, but the aesthetics, the world that build up was incredible and I thought, we’re five years away. I’m going to be living in VR now. I’m just going to be floating around. I’m going to be an avatar. This is where most humans can spend most of the day. That didn’t happen. We’re 30 years later, VR is still not here. It’s here for gaming. It’s here for some specialized applications. My oldest loves playing Gorilla Tag. I don’t know if you’ve tried that. That’s basically the hottest VR game. Wonderful. It’s great. It’s really hard to predict the future because we just don’t know.

DHH
(01:44:57) 这是一部关于虚拟现实的惊人电影，讲的是成为虚拟化身并在VR中生活。故事情节一团糟，但它的美学和构建的世界却令人惊叹。我当时心想：再过五年，我们就能生活在VR里了。我现在就要进入VR世界，四处漂浮，成为一个虚拟化身。大多数人每天大部分时间都会待在那里。但这件事并没有发生。30年过去了，VR仍未真正到来。它只存在于游戏领域，或某些特定应用场景中。我最大的孩子特别喜欢玩《Gorilla Tag》，不知道你有没有试过——这基本上是最火的VR游戏，非常棒，真的很好玩。但预测未来真的很难，因为我们根本不知道会发生什么。

And then when you factor into AI and you have even the smartest people go like, “I don’t think we fully understand how this works.”

而当你把AI考虑进来时，就连最聪明的人都会说：“我觉得我们并不完全理解它是怎么运作的。”

Lex Fridman
(01:45:49) But then on the flip side, you have Moore’s law that seems to work for many, many, many years in decreasing the size of transistor, for example. Flash didn’t take over the internet, but Moore’s law worked, so we don’t know which one AI is.

Lex Fridman
(01:45:49) 但另一方面，你也有所谓的摩尔定律——比如晶体管尺寸持续缩小这一趋势，似乎已经有效运行了很多很多年。虽然Flash没有统治互联网，但摩尔定律确实奏效了。所以我们并不知道AI到底属于哪一种情况。

DHH
(01:46:07) It is what it is. And this is what I find so fascinating to, I forget who did this presentation, but someone in the web community, this great presentation on the history of the airplane. So you go from the Wright brothers flying in, what was 1903 or something like that, and 40 years later you have a jet flight, just an unbelievable amount of progress in four decades. Then in ’56, I think it was, the whole design for the Boeing 747 century precursor was designed and basically nothing has happened since. Just minor tweaks and improvements on the flying experience since the ’50s.

DHH
(01:46:07) 它就是它本来的样子。我觉得特别有意思的是（我不记得是谁做的这个演讲了），但网络社区有个人做过一个关于飞机历史的精彩演讲。从莱特兄弟1903年左右首次飞行，到40年后喷气式飞机就出现了——短短四十年间取得了难以置信的进步。但到了1956年左右，波音747这类“世纪之机”的整体设计就已经定型了，此后基本上再无重大突破，只是在50年代以来对飞行体验做了一些微小的调整和改进。

Somehow, if you were to predict where flying was going to go and you were sitting in ’42 and you’d seen, you’d remember the Wright brothers flying in oh three and you were seeing that jet engines coming, you’re like, “We’re going to fly to the stars in another two decades.”

如果你当时坐在1942年，试图预测航空业的未来，你会回想起1903年莱特兄弟的飞行，又看到喷气发动机正在出现，你可能会想：“再过二十年，我们就要飞向星辰大海了。”

(01:47:04) We’re going to invent super mega hypersonic flights that’s going to traverse the earth in two hours, and then that didn’t happen. It tapped out. This is what’s so hard about predicting the future. We can be so excited in the moment because we’re drawing a line through early dots on a chart, and it looks like those early dots is just going up into the right and sometimes it’s just flattened out.

(01:47:04) 我们将发明超级超音速飞行器，两小时内就能绕地球一圈——但这一切并没有发生。技术发展到某个点就停滞了。这正是预测未来的难点所在。我们之所以在当下如此兴奋，是因为我们在图表上用早期的几个数据点画出了一条上升曲线，看起来这些点正一路向右上方飙升，但有时候这条线最终却趋于平缓。

This is also one of those things where we have so much critical infrastructure, for example, that still runs on COBOL, that about five humans around the world really understand truly, deeply that it’s possible for society to lose a competence it still needs because it’s chasing the future.

这也引出了另一个问题：我们如今仍有大量关键基础设施运行在COBOL语言上，而全世界大概只有五个人真正深入理解它。社会有可能因为一味追逐未来，而丧失掉自身仍然需要的核心能力。

(01:47:44) COBOL is still with us. This is one of the things I think about with programming. Ruby on Rails is at such a level now that in 50 years from now, it’s exceedingly likely that there’s still a ton of Ruby on Rails systems running around now, very hard to predict what that exact world is going to be like, but yesterday’s weather tells us that if there’s still COBOL code from the ’70s operating social security today, and we haven’t figured out a clean way to convert that, let alone understand it, we should certainly be humble about predicting the future.

(01:47:44) COBOL至今仍在使用。这也是我在思考编程时常常想到的一点。如今Ruby on Rails已经达到了这样的成熟度，极有可能在50年后，仍会有大量Ruby on Rails系统在运行。虽然我们很难准确预测那时的世界会是什么样子，但历史经验告诉我们：如果今天社会保障系统还在运行70年代写的COBOL代码，而我们甚至还没找到干净利落地转换（更别说理解）这些代码的方法，那么我们在预测未来时就应当保持谦逊。

(01:48:16) I don’t think any of the programmers who wrote that COBOL code back in the ’70s had any idea that in 2025 checks were still being cut off the business logic that they had encoded back then. But that just brings me to the conclusion on the question for what should a young programmer do? You’re not going to be able to predict the future. No one’s going to be able to predict the future. If you like programming, you should learn programming. Now, is that going to be a career forever? I don’t know, but what’s going to be a career forever? Who knows?

(01:48:16) 我认为那些在70年代编写这些COBOL代码的程序员，根本没想到2025年人们还在依靠他们当年编码的业务逻辑来发放支票。但这正好引导我得出对“年轻程序员该怎么做”这个问题的结论：你无法预测未来，没人能预测未来。如果你喜欢编程，就应该去学编程。这会是一份终身职业吗？我不知道。但又有哪种职业能保证永远存在呢？谁知道呢？

A second ago we thought that it was the blue-collar labor that was going to be abstracted. First, it was the robots that were going to take over. Then Gen AI comes out, and then all the artists suddenly look like, “Holy shit, is this going to do all animation now? Is going to do all music now?”

不久前，我们还以为被自动化取代的是蓝领劳动力。一开始，大家以为机器人会接管一切。后来通用人工智能（Gen AI）出现了，艺术家们突然慌了：“天啊，它现在是不是要把所有动画都做了？是不是要把所有音乐都创作了？”

(01:48:59) They get real scared, and now I see the latest Tesla robot going like, “Oh, maybe we’re back now to blue-collar being in trouble because if it can dance like that, it can probably fix a toilet.” So no one knows anything, and you have to then position yourself for the future in such a way that it doesn’t matter that you pick a profession or path where if it turns out that you have to retool and re-skill, you’re not going to regret the path you took. That’s a general life principle.

(01:48:59) 他们真的感到害怕了。而现在我看到最新的特斯拉机器人，又想：“哦，也许现在轮到蓝领工作陷入危机了——如果它能跳那样的舞，大概也能修马桶吧。” 所以没人真正知道未来会怎样。你必须以这样一种方式为未来做准备：无论你选择什么职业或道路，即使将来不得不重新学习技能、转换方向，你也不会后悔当初的选择。这是一种普适的人生原则。

For me, how I look at all endeavors I involved myself in is I want to be content with all outcomes.

对我来说，我看待自己参与的所有事业的方式是：我希望无论结果如何，都能感到满足。

(01:49:39) When we start working on a new product at 37 Signals, I set up my mental model for success and I go, “Do you know what? If no one wants this, I will have had another opportunity to write beautiful Ruby code to explore greenfield domain, to learn something new, to build a system I want, even if no one else wants it.” What a blessing, what a privilege. If a bunch of people want it, that’s great. We can pay some salaries, we can keep the business running, and if it’s a blowaway success, wonderful. I get to impact a bunch of people.

(01:49:39) 每当我们在37signals开始开发一个新产品时，我会先设定好自己对“成功”的心理预期，然后告诉自己：“你知道吗？即使没人想要这个产品，我也又一次获得了编写优美Ruby代码的机会，可以探索全新的领域，学习新东西，构建我自己想要的系统——哪怕别人不需要它。” 这是多么大的福分，多么难得的特权啊！如果有很多人喜欢它，那当然很棒，我们可以支付工资，维持公司运转；如果它大获成功，那就更棒了——我能影响很多人。

Vibe coding

氛围编程（Vibe Coding）

Lex Fridman
(01:50:13) I think one of the big open questions to me is how far you can get with vibe coding, whether an approach for a young developer to invest most of the time into vibe coding or into writing code from scratch. So vibe coding, meaning I’m leaning into the meme a little bit, but the vibe coding, meaning you generate code, you have this idea of a thing you want to create, you generate the code and then you fix it with both natural language to the prompts and manually. You learn enough to manually fix it. So that’s the learning process. How you fix code that’s generated or you write code from scratch and have the LMS kind of tab, tab, tab, tab, add extra code, like which part do you lean on? I think to be safe, you should find the beauty and the artistry and skill in both, right? From scratch, so there should be some percent of your time just writing from scratch and some percent vibe coding.

Lex Fridman
(01:50:13) 对我来说，一个重要的开放性问题是：通过“氛围编程”（vibe coding）究竟能走多远？年轻开发者应该把大部分时间投入在氛围编程上，还是从零开始写代码？这里说的“氛围编程”——我稍微借用了一下网络迷因的说法——指的是你有一个想创造的东西的想法，然后让AI生成代码，再通过自然语言提示和手动修改来修正它。你需要学到足够知识，才能手动修复生成的代码。这就是学习过程：你是修复AI生成的代码，还是从头手写代码，并借助大语言模型（LLM）不断按Tab键自动补全、添加额外代码？你更依赖哪一部分？我认为稳妥的做法是：你应该在两者中都发现美、艺术性和技巧。也就是说，你应该拿出一部分时间纯粹从零开始写代码，另一部分时间用于氛围编程。

DHH
(01:51:16) There should be more of the time writing from scratch if you are interested in learning how to program. Unfortunately, you’re not going to get fit by watching fitness videos. You’re not going to learn how to play the guitar by watching YouTube guitar videos. You have to actually play yourself. You have to do the sit-ups. Programming, understanding, learning almost anything requires you to do. Humans are not built to absorb information in a way that transforms into skills by just watching others from afar. Now, ironically, it seems AI is actually quite good at that, but humans are not. If you want to learn how to become a competent programmer, you have to program. It’s really not that difficult to understand. Now, I understand the temptation and the temptation is there because vibe coding can produce things perhaps in this moment, especially in new domain, you’re not familiar with tools you don’t know perfectly well that’s better than what you could do or that you would take much longer to get at, but you’re not going to learn anything.

DHH
(01:51:16) 如果你想真正学会编程，就应该花更多时间从零开始写代码。不幸的是，你看健身视频并不会让你变健康，看YouTube上的吉他教学视频也不会让你学会弹吉他。你必须亲自上手弹奏，必须自己做仰卧起坐。编程、理解、学习几乎所有技能，都需要你亲身实践。人类的大脑并不是靠远观他人就能把信息转化为技能的。讽刺的是，AI似乎在这方面做得很好，但人类不行。如果你想成为一名合格的程序员，你就必须动手写代码——这其实并不难理解。我当然明白那种诱惑：氛围编程眼下确实能快速产出成果，尤其是在你不熟悉的全新领域，面对你不完全掌握的工具时，它可能比你自己写得更好，或者至少快得多。但问题是：你不会从中真正学到任何东西。
(01:52:15) You’re going to learn in this superficial way that feels like learning but is completely empty calories, and secondly, if you can just vibe code it, you’re not a programmer. Then anyone could do it, which may be wonderful. That’s essentially what happened with the Access database. That’s what happened with Excel. It took the capacity of accountants to become software developers because the tools became so accessible to them that they could build a model for how the business was going to do next week that required a programmer prior to Excel. Now, it didn’t because they could do it themselves by coding enables non-programmers to explore their ideas in a way that I find absolutely wonderful, but it doesn’t make you a programmer.

（01:52:15）你会以一种表面化的方式学习，这种方式感觉像是在学习，但实际上只是“空热量”——毫无实质内容。其次，如果你仅靠“氛围编码”（vibe coding）就能完成任务，那你并不是一名程序员。那样的话，任何人都能做到，这或许很棒。Access 数据库和 Excel 就是如此。这些工具变得如此易用，以至于会计师也能成为软件开发者：他们可以自己构建一个预测公司下周业务表现的模型，而在此之前，这种模型必须由程序员来开发。现在他们自己就能做到。这种通过编码让非程序员探索自己想法的能力，我认为非常美妙，但这并不意味着你就是一名程序员。

Lex Fridman
(01:53:02) I agree with you, but I want to allow for room for both of us be wrong. For example, there could be vibe coding could actually be a skill that if you train it and by vibe coding, let’s include the step of correction, the iterative correction, it’s possible if you get really good at that, that you’re outperforming the people that write from scratch that you can come up with truly innovative things, especially at this moment in history while the LLMs are a little bit too dumb to create super novel things and a complete product, but they’re starting to creep close to that, so if you are investing time now into becoming a really good vibe coder, maybe this is the right thing to do. If it’s indeed a skill, we kind of meme about vibe coding, like sitting back and it’s in the name, but if you treat it seriously, a competitive vibe coder and get good at riding the wave of AI and get good at the skill of editing code versus writing code from scratch, it’s possible that you can actually get farther in the long term.

Lex Fridman
（01:53:02）我同意你的观点，但我也希望我们双方都有可能犯错。比如，“氛围编码”实际上可能是一种技能——如果你加以训练，并且我们所说的“氛围编码”包括修正步骤，即迭代式修正，那么当你在这方面变得非常熟练时，你甚至可能超越那些从零开始写代码的人，能够提出真正创新的东西。尤其是在当前这个历史阶段，大语言模型（LLMs）还略显愚钝，尚不能创造出极具新意的完整产品，但它们正逐渐接近这一能力。因此，如果你现在投入时间去成为一名出色的“氛围编码者”，这或许正是正确的选择。如果它确实是一种技能，我们目前对它的调侃——比如“靠感觉”、“放松坐着”等字面意思——可能掩盖了其潜力；但如果你认真对待它，努力成为一名有竞争力的“氛围编码者”，擅长驾驭 AI 的浪潮，并精于编辑代码而非从头编写，那么长远来看，你或许能走得更远。

(01:54:12) Maybe editing is a fundamentally different task than writing from scratch if you take that seriously as a skill that you develop. I see. To me, that’s an open question. I just think I personally, now you’re on another level, but just personally, I’m not as good at editing the code that I didn’t write. That’s a different—

（01:54:12）也许编辑本质上是一项与从零开始编写完全不同的任务——如果你认真将其视为一项可培养的技能。我明白了。对我来说，这是一个开放性问题。就我个人而言——当然你现在处于另一个层次——但我个人并不擅长编辑别人写的代码。那是另一回事——

DHH
(01:54:36) No one is.

DHH
（01:54:36）没人擅长。

Lex Fridman
(01:54:38) No one is of this generation, but maybe that’s a skill. Maybe if you get on the same page as the AI, because there’s a consistency to the AI. It’s like it really is a pair of programmers with a consistent style and structure and so on. Plus, with your own prompting, you can control the kind of code you write. I mean, it could legitimately be a skill.

Lex Fridman
（01:54:38）我们这一代人确实没人擅长，但也许这本身就是一种技能。也许当你与 AI 达成一致时——因为 AI 具有一致性，就像一对拥有统一风格和结构的程序员搭档。再加上你可以通过自己的提示词（prompting）控制所生成代码的类型，这确实可能成为一项真正的技能。

DHH
(01:54:59) That’s the dream of the prompt engineer. I think it’s complete pipe dream. I don’t think editors exist that aren’t good at writing. I’ve written a number of books. I’ve had a number of professional editors. Not all of them wrote their own great books, but all of them were great writers in some regard. You cannot give someone pointers if you don’t know how to do it. It’s very difficult for an editor to be able to spot what’s wrong with a problem if the data couldn’t make the solution themselves. The capacity to be a good editor is the reward you get from being a good doer. You have to be a doer first. Now, that’s not the same as saying that vibe coding, prompt engineering won’t be able to produce fully formed amazing systems even shortly. I think that’s entirely possible, but then there’s no skill left, which maybe is the greatest payoff at all.

DHH
（01:54:59）这就是“提示工程师”的梦想。但我认为这完全是空中楼阁。我不相信存在不擅长写作却能胜任编辑工作的人。我写过好几本书，也合作过许多专业编辑。他们并非都写出了自己的杰作，但无一例外都在某种程度上是优秀的写作者。如果你自己不会做某件事，就无法给别人提供有效建议。如果一个编辑自己都无法构思出解决方案，那他很难准确指出问题所在。成为一个优秀编辑的能力，是你作为优秀实践者所获得的回报。你必须先成为实践者。当然，这并不等于说“氛围编码”或“提示工程”短期内无法产出完整而惊艳的系统——我认为这完全有可能。但若真到了那一步，技能本身可能就不复存在了，而这或许恰恰是最大的回报。

(01:55:57) Wasn’t that the whole promise of AI anyway, that it was just all natural language that even my clumsy way of formulating a question could result in a beautiful succinct answer? That actually to me is a much more appealing vision that there’s going to be these special prompt engineering wizards who know how to tickle the AI just right to produce what they want. The beauty of AI is to think that someone who doesn’t know the first thing about how AI actually works is able to formulate their idea and their aspirations for what they want, and the AI could somehow take that messy clump of ideas and produce something that someone wants.

（01:55:57）这难道不正是 AI 最初的承诺吗？即只需使用自然语言，哪怕是我这种笨拙的问题表述方式，也能得到优美而简洁的答案？对我而言，这比存在一群特殊的“提示工程巫师”——他们懂得如何恰到好处地“挠”AI 的痒处以获得想要的结果——要吸引人得多。AI 的真正魅力在于：一个对 AI 运作原理一无所知的人，也能表达自己的想法和愿望，而 AI 能将这一团混乱的想法转化为某人真正需要的东西。

(01:56:35) That’s actually what programming has always been. There’s very often been people who didn’t know how to program, who wanted programs, who then hired programmers, who gave them messy descriptions of what they wanted, and then when the programmers delivered that back said, “Oh, no, actually that’s not what I meant. I want else.” AI may be able to provide that cycle if that happens to the fullest extent of it, yeah, there’s not going to be as many programmers around, but hopefully presumably someone still, at least for the foreseeable future, have to understand whether what the AI is producing actually works or not.

（01:56:35）事实上，编程一直以来都是如此：经常有人不懂编程却想要程序，于是雇用程序员，向他们提供混乱的需求描述；而当程序员交付成果后，他们却说：“哦，不对，其实我不是这个意思，我想要的是别的。”如果 AI 能将这一循环发挥到极致，那么程序员的数量确实会减少。但至少在可预见的未来，我们仍希望有人能判断 AI 产出的内容是否真正有效。

Lex Fridman
(01:57:11) As an interesting case study, maybe a thought experiment, if I wanted to vibe code Basecamp or hey, some of the products you’ve built, what would be the bottlenecks? Where would I fail along the way?

Lex Fridman
（01:57:11）作为一个有趣的案例研究，或者思想实验：如果我想用“氛围编码”来复现 Basecamp，或者你开发过的其他产品，会遇到哪些瓶颈？我会在哪些环节失败？

DHH
(01:57:30) What I’ve seen when I’ve been trying to do this, trying to use vibe coding to build something real is you actually fail really early. The vibe coding is able to build a veneer at the current present moment of something that looks like it works, but it’s flawed in all sorts of ways. There are the obvious ways, the meme ways that it’s leaking all your API keys, it’s storing your password in plain text. I think that’s ultimately solvable. It’s going to figure that out, or at least it’s going to get better at that, but its capacity to get lost in its own Labyrinth is very great right now. You let it code something and then you want to change something and it becomes a game of Whack-A-Mole real quick.

DHH
（01:57:30）根据我的尝试经验，当你试图用“氛围编码”构建真实产品时，其实很早就失败了。“氛围编码”目前只能快速构建出一个看似能运行的表层，但它在各方面都存在缺陷。有些是显而易见的、被大家调侃的问题，比如泄露所有 API 密钥、明文存储密码等。我认为这些问题最终是可以解决的——AI 会逐渐学会避免这些错误，或者至少会变得更好。但目前它非常容易陷入自己制造的迷宫之中。你让它写一段代码，然后想稍作修改，很快就会变成一场“打地鼠”游戏——修好一处，别处又冒出新问题。

(01:58:09) Pieter Levels who’ve been doing this wonderful flight simulator was talking to that where at a certain scale the thing just keeps biting its own tail. You want to fix something and it breaks five other things, which I think is actually uniquely human because that’s how most bad programmers are at a certain level of complexity with the domain. They can’t fix one thing without breaking three other things, so in that way I’m actually in some way it’s almost a positive signal for that. The AI is going to figure this out because it’s done an extremely human trajectory right now. The kind of mistakes it’s making are the kind of mistakes that junior programmers make all the time.

（01:58:09）Pieter Levels 在开发那个出色的飞行模拟器时就提到过：当项目达到一定规模后，系统会不断自相矛盾。你想修复一个问题，结果却导致另外五个地方出错。我认为这其实非常“人性化”——因为在特定复杂度下，大多数糟糕的程序员正是如此：他们无法在不破坏其他三处功能的情况下修复一个问题。从这个角度看，这几乎是一个积极信号：AI 正在经历一条极其“人类化”的成长轨迹。它所犯的错误，正是初级程序员常犯的错误。

Rails manifesto: Principles of a great programming language

Rails 宣言：优秀编程语言的原则

Lex Fridman
(01:58:43) Yeah. Can we zoom out and look at the vision, the manifesto, the doctrine of Rails? What are some of the things that make a programming language a framework? Great, especially for web development, so we talked about happiness.

Rails 宣言：优秀编程语言的原则
Lex Fridman
（01:58:43）是的。我们能否退一步，看看 Rails 的愿景、宣言或核心理念？是什么让一种编程语言或框架变得卓越？尤其是在 Web 开发领域。我们之前谈到了“幸福感”。

DHH
(01:59:00) Yes.
Lex Fridman
(01:59:00) The underlying objective of Ruby. What else?

DHH
（01:59:00）是的。
Lex Fridman
（01:59:00）这是 Ruby 的根本目标。除此之外呢？

DHH
(01:59:04) So you’re looking at the nine points I wrote out in I think 2012 and first, before we dive into them, I want to say the reason I wrote it down is that if you want a community to endure, you have to record its values and you have to record its practices. If you don’t, eventually you’re going to get enough new people come in who have their own ideas of where this thing should go, and if we don’t have a guiding light helping us to make decisions, we’re going to start flailing. We’re going to start actually falling apart. I think this is one of the key reasons that institutions of all kinds start falling apart. We forget why Chesterton’s fence is there. We just go like, why is that fence there? Let’s yank it out. Oh, it was to keep the wolves out. Now we’re all dead.
(01:59:49) Oops. So I wanted to write these things down and if we just take them quick one by one, you talked about optimizing for programmer happiness. I put that at number one in homage of Matz, and that’s a lot about accepting that there is occasionally a trade-off between writing beautiful code and other things we want out of systems. There could be a runtime trade-off. There can be a performance trade-off, but we’re going to do it nonetheless. We’re also going to allow ambiguity in a way that many programmers by default are uncomfortable with. I give the example actually here of in the interactive Ruby Shell where you can play with the language or even interact with your domain model. You can quit it in two ways, at least that I found. You can write exit. Boom, you’re out of the program. You can write quit. Boom, you’re out of the program.

DHH
（01:59:04）你提到的是我在 2012 年左右写下的九条原则。在深入讨论之前，我想先说明我写下这些原则的原因：如果你想让一个社区长久存续，就必须记录下它的价值观和实践方式。否则，迟早会有大量新人加入，他们带着自己对项目发展方向的理解；如果我们没有一盏指路明灯来辅助决策，就会开始盲目挣扎，甚至分崩离析。我认为，这正是各类机构走向衰败的关键原因之一——我们忘记了“切斯特顿篱笆”为何存在。我们会问：“这篱笆干嘛用的？干脆拆了吧！”结果发现它是用来防狼的，现在我们都死了。
（01:59:49）哎呀。所以我才想把这些原则写下来。如果我们逐条快速过一遍：你提到了“以程序员幸福感为优化目标”。我把这一点放在首位，是为了向松本行弘（Matz）致敬。这很大程度上意味着我们接受这样一个事实：编写优美代码与其他系统目标之间有时存在权衡——可能是运行时效率的权衡，也可能是性能的权衡——但我们依然会选择前者。此外，我们还会允许一定程度的模糊性，而这种模糊性会让许多程序员本能地感到不适。举个例子，在交互式 Ruby Shell（IRB）中，你可以试验语言特性，甚至与你的领域模型互动。我发现至少有两种方式可以退出：你可以输入 exit，砰，程序退出；也可以输入 quit，砰，同样退出。
(02:00:38) They do the same thing. We just wrote both exit or the people who built that wrote both exit and quit because they knew humans were likely to pick one or the other. Python is the perfect contrast to this. In the Python interactive protocol, if you write exit, it won’t exit. It’ll give you a fucking lesson. It’ll basically tell you to read the fucking manual. It says, “Use exit() or Ctrl+D i.e. end of file to exit.” I’m like one is very human and another is very engineer, and I mean that both of them in the best possible way. Python is pedantic. Python’s the value from the start stated is that there should be preferably one and only one way to do a certain thing. Ruby is the complete opposite. No, we want the full expression that fits different human brains such that it seems like the language is guessing just what they want.
（02:00:38）它们做的是同一件事。我们之所以同时写了 exit，或者说是构建那个系统的人同时实现了 exit 和 quit，是因为他们知道人类很可能会选择其中一个或另一个。Python 与之形成了鲜明对比。在 Python 的交互式协议中，如果你输入 exit，它并不会退出，反而会给你上一堂“该死的”课——基本上就是让你去读该死的手册。它会说：“请使用 exit() 或 Ctrl+D（即文件结束符）来退出。”我觉得一种方式非常人性化，另一种则非常工程师化，而且我这么说完全是褒义的。Python 是吹毛求疵的。Python 从一开始就强调：做某件事最好只有一种、且唯一的一种方式。而 Ruby 则完全相反：不，我们希望提供充分的表达方式，以适应不同人的思维方式，让语言仿佛能猜中用户真正想要什么。

Lex Fridman
(02:01:37) And part of that is also you described the principle of the least surprise, which is a difficult thing to engineer a language because it’s a subjective thing.
Lex Fridman
（02:01:37）其中一部分也是你提到的“最少惊讶原则”，这在设计语言时很难实现，因为它是主观的。

DHH
(02:01:47) Which is why you can’t do it in one way, which is why I used the example of both exit and quit. The principle of least surprise for some people would be like, “Oh, exit. That’s how I get out of the prompt. For other people, it would be quit.” Why don’t we just do both?
DHH
（02:01:47）正因为如此，你不能只用一种方式，所以我才举了 exit 和 quit 这个例子。“最少惊讶原则”对某些人来说可能是：“哦，用 exit，这样我就能退出提示符。”而对另一些人来说，则是 quit。那我们为什么不能两者都支持呢？

Lex Fridman
(02:02:01) Okay, so what’s the convention over configuration? That’s a big one.
Lex Fridman
（02:02:01）好的，那么“约定优于配置”是什么呢？这是一个很重要的概念。

DHH
(02:02:05) That’s a big one. That’s a huge one. And it was born out of a frustration I had in the early days with especially Java frameworks where when you were setting up a web application framework for Java back in the day, it was not uncommon to literally write right hundreds if not thousands of lines of XML configuration files. Oh, I need this. I want the database to use the foreign keys as post underscore ID. No, no, no. I want it as post capital ID. Oh, no, no, no. You have to do a capital PID. There are all these ways where you can configure how foreign relation keys should work in a database and none of them matter. We just need to pick one and then that’s fine, and if pick one and we can depend on it, it becomes a convention. If it’s a convention, we don’t have to configure it if we don’t have to configure it, you can get started with you actually care about much quicker.
DHH
（02:02:05）这确实是个大概念，非常关键。它源于我早期使用 Java 框架时的挫败感。那时候，当你搭建一个 Java Web 应用框架，动辄就要写几百甚至上千行的 XML 配置文件。“哦，我需要这个。我希望数据库外键用 post_underscore_id。”“不不不，我要的是 PostCapitalID。”“哦不对不对，你得用大写的 PID。”有无数种方式可以配置数据库中外键关系的工作方式，但其实这些细节根本无关紧要。我们只需要选定一种方式，然后就用它。一旦选定了，并且可以依赖它，这就成了约定。如果有了约定，就不需要再配置；而一旦不需要配置，你就能更快地投入到真正关心的事情上。

(02:02:57) Convention of a configuration is essentially to take that idea that the system should come pre-assembled. I’m not just handing you a box of fucking Legos and asking you to build the Millennium Falcon. I’m giving you a finished toy. You can edit, you can change it. It’s still build out a Legos. You can still take some pieces off and put in some other pieces, but I’m giving you the final product and this cuts against the grain of what most programmers love. They love a box of Legos. They love to put everything together from scratch. They love to make all these detailed little decisions that just don’t matter at all, and I want to elevate that up such that, hey, I’m not trying to take the decisions away from you. I just want you to focus on decisions that actually matter that you truly care about. No one cares about whether it’s post underscore ID or post ID or PID.
（02:02:57）“约定优于配置”的核心思想就是：系统应该预先组装好。我不是扔给你一盒该死的乐高积木，然后让你自己去拼千年隼。我是直接给你一个成品玩具。你可以修改它、调整它——它仍然是用乐高搭成的，你当然也可以拆掉一些零件，换上别的，但我先给你的是一个完整的成品。这种做法恰恰违背了大多数程序员的喜好。他们喜欢一盒乐高，喜欢从零开始搭建一切，喜欢做出大量实际上毫无意义的微小决策。而我想做的是提升这一过程：嘿，我不是要剥夺你的决策权，我只是希望你把精力集中在那些真正重要、你真正在意的决策上。没人关心外键到底叫 post_underscore_id、postId 还是 PID。

Lex Fridman
(02:03:41) Yeah, great defaults.
Lex Fridman
（02:03:41）对，就是优秀的默认值。

DHH
(02:03:43) Yes.
DHH
（02:03:43）没错。

Lex Fridman
(02:03:44) It’s just a wonderful thing. You have all these aspirations, they’re going to do some kind of custom, most beautiful Legos castle that nobody’s ever built from these pieces, but in reality to be productive in most situations, you just need to build the basic thing and then on top of that is where your creativity comes.
Lex Fridman
（02:03:44）这真的很棒。你可能满怀雄心壮志，想用这些积木搭出一座前所未有的、最精美的定制城堡，但现实中，在大多数情况下，你只需要先搭出基础结构，真正的创造力是在那之上发挥的。

DHH
(02:04:03) Absolutely, and I think this is one of those, part of the doctrine that a lot of programmers who get to use Ruby on Rails begrudgingly will acknowledge it’s a nice thing. Even if they don’t really like it’s hard to beat the attraction to building with Legos from scratch out of programmers. That’s just what we like. This is why we’re programmers in the first place because we’d like to put these little pieces together, but we can direct that instinct towards a more productive end of the stack.
DHH
（02:04:03）完全同意。我认为这是 Ruby on Rails 哲学的一部分，许多使用它的程序员即使心不甘情不愿，也会承认这是一种很好的做法。尽管他们可能并不真心喜欢——毕竟程序员天生就难以抗拒从零开始用乐高搭建东西的诱惑。这就是我们的天性，也是我们成为程序员的初衷：因为我们喜欢把这些小零件拼在一起。但我们可以引导这种本能，让它服务于更高效、更高层的开发目标。

Lex Fridman
(02:04:33) Okay. What are some of the other ones?
Lex Fridman
（02:04:33）好的，还有其他一些原则吗？

DHH
(02:04:35) The menu is omakase. It actually comes out of the same principle that great defaults really matter. If you look at everything that’s wrong with the JavaScript ecosystem right now, for example, it is that no one is in charge of the menu. There are a billion different dishes and you can configure just your tailored specific configuration of it, but no one done the work to make sure it all fits together, so you have all these unique problems in the JavaScript ecosystem, for example, there’s probably 25 major ways of just doing the controller layer and then as many of how to talk to the database, so you get this permutation of N times N times N of no one is using the same thing.
DHH
（02:04:35）菜单是“主厨发办”（omakase）。这其实源于同一个原则：优秀的默认值至关重要。以当前 JavaScript 生态系统的问题为例，其症结就在于没人负责这份“菜单”。有十亿种不同的“菜肴”，你可以随意定制自己的专属组合，却没人花力气确保所有组件能和谐共存。于是 JavaScript 生态出现了各种独特的问题。比如，光是控制器层就有大约 25 种主流实现方式，数据库访问方式也同样繁多，结果就是 N×N×N 的排列组合——几乎没人用完全相同的方案。

(02:05:17) And if they are using the same thing, they’re only using the same thing for about five minutes, so we have no retained wisdom. We build up no durable skills. Rails goes the complete opposite way of saying do you know what? Rails is not just a web framework. It is a complete attempt at solving the web problem. It’s complete attempt at solving everything you need to build a great web application, and every piece of that puzzle should ideally be in the box pre-configured, pre-assembled.
（02:05:17）即便有人用了相同的方案，也往往只坚持五分钟，所以我们无法积累任何持久的经验，也无法培养可持续的技能。Rails 走的是完全相反的路线：你知道吗？Rails 不仅仅是一个 Web 框架，它是一次完整解决 Web 开发问题的尝试——它试图涵盖构建优秀 Web 应用所需的一切。理想情况下，这个拼图中的每一块都应该预先配置好、预先组装好，直接装在盒子里交给你。

(02:05:48) If you want to change some of those pieces later, that’s wonderful, but on day one you’ll get a full menu designed by a chef who really cared about every piece of the ingredient and you’re going to enjoy it, and that’s again one of those things where many programmers think like I know better and they do in some hyperlocal sense of it. Every programmer knows better. This is what Ruby is built on, that every programmer knows better in their specific situation. Maybe they can do something dangerous, maybe they think they know better and then they blow their foot off and then they truly will know better because they’ve blown their foot off once and won’t do it again. But the menu on omakase is that.
（02:05:48）如果你想以后再更换其中某些组件，那当然很好。但在第一天，你拿到的是一份由主厨精心设计的完整菜单，他对每一种食材都倾注了心思，你会享受它的。这又是那种让很多程序员觉得“我比你懂”的地方——在某些极其局部的情境下，他们确实更懂。每个程序员都认为自己更懂，这也是 Ruby 的设计哲学基础：在特定情境下，每个程序员都更了解自己的需求。也许他们会做一些危险的事，也许他们自以为更懂，结果把自己的脚炸飞了——但正因如此，他们才会真正学到教训，下次就不会再犯。而“主厨发办”的菜单正是基于这样的理念。

Lex Fridman
(02:06:28) So you in general see the value in the monolith?
Lex Fridman
（02:06:28）所以总体而言，你是认可单体架构（monolith）的价值的？

DHH
(02:06:32) Yes. The integrated system.
DHH
（02:06:32）是的，集成化的系统。

Lex Fridman
(02:06:34) Integrated-
Lex Fridman
（02:06:34）集成化的——

DHH
(02:06:35) That someone thought of the whole problem. This is one of the reasons why I’ve been on a crusade against microservices since the term was coined. Microservices was born out of essentially a good idea. What do you do at Netflix scale when you have thousands of engineers working on millions of lines of code? No one can keep that entire system in their head at one time. You have to break it down. Microservices can be a reasonable way to do that when you’re at Netflix scale. When you apply that pattern to a team of 20 programmers working on a code base of half a million lines of code, you’re an idiot. You just don’t need to turn method invocations into network calls. It is the first rule of distributed programming. Do not distribute your programming. It makes everything harder. All the failure conditions you have to consider as a programmer just becomes infinitely harder when there’s a network cable involved, so I hate the idea of premature decomposition and microservices is exactly that.
DHH
（02:06:35）就是有人通盘考虑了整个问题。这也是我自“微服务”一词出现以来就一直强烈反对它的原因之一。微服务最初源于一个好想法：当你在 Netflix 那样的规模下，有数千名工程师共同维护数百万行代码时，没有人能同时把整个系统装进脑子里，你必须把它拆分。在这种规模下，微服务是一种合理的解决方案。但如果你把这个模式套用到一个只有 20 名程序员、维护着 50 万行代码的团队身上，那你就是个傻瓜。你根本没必要把方法调用变成网络调用。这是分布式编程的第一法则：不要过早地将程序分布化。它会让一切变得更难。一旦涉及网络，作为程序员你需要考虑的所有故障情况都会变得无限复杂。因此我极度厌恶“过早分解”，而微服务恰恰就是这种做法。

(02:07:35) The monolith says let’s try to focus on building a whole system that a single human can actually understand and push that paradigm as far as possible by compressing all the concepts such that more of it will fit into memory of a single operating human, and then we can have a system where I can actually understand all of Basecamp. I can actually understand all of HEY. Both of those systems are just over a hundred thousand lines of code. I’ve seen people do this that maybe twice, maybe three times that scale and then it starts breaking down. Once you get north of certainly half a million lines of code, no individual human can do it, and that’s when you get into maybe some degree of microservices can make sense.
（02:07:35）单体架构的理念是：让我们专注于构建一个单个人类能够真正理解的完整系统，并通过压缩所有概念，尽可能地把更多内容塞进一个正常人类的大脑内存中。这样一来，我就能真正理解整个 Basecamp，也能真正理解整个 HEY。这两个系统的代码量都刚过十万行。我见过有人做到两倍甚至三倍于这个规模，但再往上系统就开始崩溃了。一旦超过 50 万行代码，就没有哪个个体还能完全掌握整个系统了，那时或许才到了微服务能派上用场的阶段。

Lex Fridman
(02:08:12) Basecamp and HEY are both a hundred thousand?
Lex Fridman
（02:08:12）Basecamp 和 HEY 都只有十万行代码？

DHH
(02:08:14) A hundred thousand lines of code.
DHH
（02:08:14）十万行代码。

Lex Fridman
(02:08:15) Wow. It’s small.
Lex Fridman
（02:08:15）哇，真小。

DHH
(02:08:16) It’s considering the fact that Basecamp I think has something like 420 screens, different ways and configurations.
DHH
（02:08:16）要知道 Basecamp 我觉得大概有 420 个界面，各种不同的方式和配置。

Lex Fridman
(02:08:23) Do you include the front end in that?
Lex Fridman
（02:08:23）这个数字包含前端代码吗？
(02:08:25) No, that’s the Ruby code. Well, it’s front end in the sense that some of that Ruby code is beneficial to the front end, but it’s not JavaScript for example. Now, the other thing we might talk about later is we write very little JavaScript actually for all of our applications. HEY, which is a Gmail competitor. Gmail ships I think 28 of uncompressed JavaScript. If you compress it, I think it’s about six megabytes, 28 megabytes. Think about how many lines of code that is.
(02:08:25) 不，那是 Ruby 代码。嗯，从某种意义上说它属于前端，因为其中一些 Ruby 代码对前端有帮助，但它并不是像 JavaScript 那样的语言。另外一点我们稍后可能会谈到：实际上，我们在所有应用程序中写的 JavaScript 非常少。HEY 是一个 Gmail 的竞品。Gmail 发送的未压缩 JavaScript 我估计有 28 兆字节；如果压缩后，大概也有 6 兆字节，不，应该是 28 兆字节。想想那得有多少行代码。

(02:08:48) When HEY launched, we shipped 40 kilobytes. It’s trying to solve the same problem. You can solve the email client problem with either 28 megabytes of uncompressed JavaScript or with 40 kilobytes if you do things differently, but that comes to the same problem essentially. This is why I have fiercely fought splitting front end and back end. Apart that in my opinion, this was one of the great crimes against web development that we are still atoning for that we separated and divided what was and should be a unified problem solving mechanism. When you are working both on front end and back end, you understand the whole system and you’re not going to get into these camps that decompose and eventually you end up with shit like GraphQL.
(02:08:48) HEY 上线时，我们只发送了 40 千字节。它试图解决的是同一个问题。你可以用 28 兆字节的未压缩 JavaScript 来解决邮件客户端的问题，也可以用 40 千字节（如果你采用不同的做法）。但本质上，这归结为同一个问题。这正是我强烈反对将前端和后端割裂开来的理由。在我看来，这种割裂是 Web 开发史上的一大罪过，我们至今仍在为此赎罪——我们把本应统一的问题解决机制人为地拆分开了。当你同时处理前端和后端时，你才能理解整个系统，不会陷入那些过度分解的阵营，最终搞出像 GraphQL 这样的烂摊子。

Lex Fridman
(02:09:36) Okay. Let’s fly through the rest of the doctrine. No one paradigm.
Lex Fridman
(02:09:36) 好的，我们快速过一下剩下的信条。“不要拘泥于单一范式”。

DHH
(02:09:44) No one paradigm goes to the fact that Ruby is a fiercely object-oriented programming language at its core, but it’s also a functional programming language. This five times I told you about, you can essentially do these anonymous function calls and you can chain them together very much in the spirit of how true functional programming languages work, Ruby has even moved closer towards the functional programming and of the scale by making strings immutable. There are ideas from all different disciplines of an all different paradigms of software development that can fit together. Smalltalk, for example, was only object-oriented and that was just it. Ruby tries to be mainly object-oriented, but borrow a little bit of functional programming, a little bit of imperative programming, be able to do all of that. Rails tries to do the same thing. We’re not just going to pick one paradigm and run it through everything.
DHH
(02:09:44) “不要拘泥于单一范式”指的是：Ruby 在核心上是一种强烈的面向对象编程语言，但它同时也具备函数式编程的特性。我之前提到过的“五次”操作，本质上就是匿名函数调用，你可以将它们链式组合起来，非常贴近真正函数式编程语言的工作方式。Ruby 甚至通过让字符串变为不可变（immutable），进一步向函数式编程靠拢。软件开发中各种不同范式、不同流派的思想是可以融合在一起的。例如，Smalltalk 就纯粹是面向对象的，仅此而已。而 Ruby 则主要以面向对象为基础，同时借鉴一点函数式编程、一点命令式编程，做到兼收并蓄。Rails 也试图做同样的事情——我们不会只选一种范式然后强行套用到所有地方。

(02:10:35) Object orientation is at the center of it, but it’s okay to invite all these other disciplines in. It’s okay to be inspired. It’s okay to remix it. I actually think one of the main benefits of Rails is that it’s a remix. I didn’t invent all these ideas. I didn’t come up with ActiveRecord. I didn’t come up with the MVC way of dividing an application. I took all the great ideas that I had learned and picked up from every different camp and I put it together. Not because there was going to be just one single overarching theory of everything, but I was going to have a cohesive unit that incorporated the best from everywhere.
(02:10:35) 面向对象是它的核心，但引入其他各种范式也是完全可以的。受启发没问题，混搭也没问题。事实上，我认为 Rails 最大的优势之一就在于它是一种“混搭”。这些想法并非我凭空发明的——ActiveRecord 不是我首创的，MVC（模型-视图-控制器）的应用程序划分方式也不是我提出的。我只是把我从各个流派中学到和收集到的所有优秀思想整合在了一起。这么做并不是因为我相信存在一个包罗万象的终极理论，而是想打造一个融汇各方精华的、协调统一的整体。

Lex Fridman
(02:11:10) Is that idea a bit at tension with the beauty of the monolith system?
Lex Fridman
(02:11:10) 这种理念是否与单体系统（monolith）之美有些矛盾？

DHH
(02:11:15) I think the monolith can be thought of as quite roomy, quite as a big tent that the monolith needs actually to borrow a little bit of functional programming for the kinds of problems that that excels, that discipline excels its solving and that paradigm excels its solving. If you also want object orientation at its core, I actually think when I’ve looked at functional programming languages, there’s a lot to love and then I see some of the crazy contortions they have to go through when part of the problem they’re solving calls for mutating something and you go like, “Holy shit, this is a great paradigm from 90% of the problem, and then you’re twisting yourself completely out of shape when you try to solve the last 10.”
DHH
(02:11:15) 我认为单体系统其实可以非常包容，就像一个大帐篷。单体系统确实需要针对某些特别适合用函数式编程解决的问题，借用一点函数式编程的方法——因为那种范式在解决这类问题上表现出色。当然，如果你仍希望以面向对象为核心，那么我的看法是：当我观察函数式编程语言时，会发现其中有很多值得喜爱之处；但我也看到，当它们遇到需要修改（mutate）状态的问题时，不得不做出一些极其扭曲的绕弯子操作。你会忍不住感叹：“天啊，这个范式对 90% 的问题都完美适用，可一到解决最后那 10% 的问题时，你就得把自己拧成麻花了。”

Lex Fridman
(02:12:00) Ooh, Exalt beautiful code is the next one.
Lex Fridman
(02:12:00) 噢，“崇尚优美代码”是下一条。

DHH
(02:12:03) We’ve talked about that at length and here’s a great example that really summarizes the main specific language quality of Ruby on Rails that you can make code actually pleasant to write and read, which is really funny to me because as we talked about when I started learning programming, it wasn’t even a consideration. I didn’t even know that that could be part of the premise, that that could be part of the solution that writing code could feel as good as writing a poem.
DHH
(02:12:03) 我们之前已经详细讨论过这一点。这里有个绝佳的例子，能很好地概括 Ruby on Rails 的核心语言特质：它能让代码写起来和读起来都令人愉悦。这对我来说特别有趣，因为正如我们之前聊到的，我刚开始学编程时，根本没考虑过这一点。我甚至不知道“写代码可以像写诗一样美好”竟然也能成为编程的前提或解决方案的一部分。

Lex Fridman
(02:12:31) Class project, application record belongs to account has many participants, class name person, validates presence of name.
Lex Fridman
(02:12:31) 比如：“class Project，ApplicationRecord belongs_to :account，has_many :participants，class_name: ‘Person’，validates :presence_of => :name。”

DHH
(02:12:41) See, you could read it out. You didn’t even change anything.
DHH
(02:12:41) 看，你直接念出来就行，根本不需要做任何改动。

Lex Fridman
(02:12:44) Like a haiku or something.
Lex Fridman
(02:12:44) 就像一首俳句什么的。

DHH
(02:12:45) Right. Isn’t that beautiful?
DHH
(02:12:45) 对吧？这难道不美吗？

Lex Fridman
(02:12:47) Yeah, it’s nice. It’s really nice. There’s an intuitive nature to it. Okay, so I have specific questions there. I mean ActiveRecord, just to take that tangent, that has to be your favorite feature.
Lex Fridman
(02:12:47) 是啊，这很棒，真的很棒。它有一种直观的美感。好吧，我有几个具体问题。就拿 ActiveRecord 来说吧——暂且岔开一下话题——这肯定是你最喜欢的功能吧？

DHH
(02:13:00) It’s the crown jewel of Rails. It really is. It’s the defining characteristic of how to work with Ruby on Rails. And it’s born in an interesting level of controversy because it actually uses a pattern that had been described by Martin Fowler in the patterns of enterprise application architecture. One of the greatest books for anyone working on business systems and if you had not read it, you must pick it up immediately. Patterns of enterprise application architecture, I think it was published in 2001. It is one of the very few programming books that I have read many times over. It’s incredible in it. Martin describes a bunch of different patterns of how to build business systems essentially. An ActiveRecord is a little bit of a footnote in there. The pattern is literally called ActiveRecord. You can look it up. It’s called ActiveRecord. I wouldn’t even creative enough to come up a name of my own, but it allows the creation, the marriage of database and object orientation in a way that a lot of programmers find a little off-putting.
DHH
(02:13:00) 它确实是 Rails 的皇冠明珠，真的。它是 Ruby on Rails 工作方式的标志性特征。而且它诞生于一场有趣的争议之中，因为它实际上采用了 Martin Fowler 在《企业应用架构模式》（Patterns of Enterprise Application Architecture）一书中描述的一种模式。这本书是所有从事业务系统开发的人必读的经典之作，如果你还没读过，请立刻去读。我记得它出版于 2001 年，是我为数不多反复阅读多次的编程书籍之一，内容非常精彩。Martin 在书中描述了多种构建业务系统的模式。而 ActiveRecord 在其中其实只是个小小的脚注——那个模式的名字就叫“ActiveRecord”，你可以去查证，真的就叫 ActiveRecord。我甚至都没创意到自己起个名字。但它以一种方式将数据库与面向对象编程结合在一起，而这种方式让很多程序员感到有点不适。

(02:14:04) They don’t actually want to pollute the beautiful object-oriented nature of that kind of programming with SQL. There was a rant by Uncle Bob the other day about how SQL is the worst thing ever. Okay, fine, whatever. I don’t care. This is practical. We are making crud applications. You’re taking things out of an HTML form and you’re sticking them into a database. It’s not more complicated than that. The more abstractions you put in between those two ends of the spectrum, the more you’re just fooling yourself. This is what we’re doing. We’re talking to SQL databases.
(02:14:04) 他们其实不想用 SQL 污染那种编程语言优美的面向对象本质。前几天 Uncle Bob（罗伯特·马丁）还发了一通牢骚，说 SQL 是史上最糟糕的东西。好吧，随便他怎么说，我不在乎。这很务实——我们做的就是 CRUD 应用（增删改查）。你从 HTML 表单里取出数据，然后存进数据库，事情就这么简单。你在两端之间加的抽象层越多，就越是在自欺欺人。这就是我们正在做的事：我们在跟 SQL 数据库打交道。

(02:14:39) By the way, quick aside, SQL was one of those things that have endured the onslaught of NoSQL databases structured list data for a better part of a decade and still reign supreme. SQL was a good thing to invest your time in learning. Every program I’m working with the web should know SQL to a fair degree, even if they’re working with an ORM, an object relational mapper as ActiveRecord, you still need to understand SQL. What ActiveRecord does is not so much try to abstract the SQL away behind a different kind of paradigm. It’s just making it less cumbersome to write, making it more amenable to build domain models on top of other domain models in a way, since you don’t have to write every SQL statement by hand.
(02:14:39) 顺便提一句，SQL 是少数经受住了 NoSQL 数据库长达近十年冲击的技术之一，至今仍占据主导地位。花时间学习 SQL 是非常值得的。每个从事 Web 开发的人都应该在一定程度上掌握 SQL，即使你使用的是像 ActiveRecord 这样的 ORM（对象关系映射器），你也仍然需要理解 SQL。ActiveRecord 的目的并不是用另一种范式把 SQL 完全抽象掉，而是让编写 SQL 不再那么繁琐，让你能更轻松地在已有领域模型之上构建新的领域模型——因为你不必手写每一条 SQL 语句。

Lex Fridman
(02:15:23) Let’s just say that ActiveRecord is an ORM, which is a layer that makes it intuitive and human interpretable to communicate with a database.
Lex Fridman
(02:15:23) 我们可以说，ActiveRecord 是一种 ORM（对象关系映射器），它是一个让数据库交互变得直观且易于人类理解的抽象层。

DHH
(02:15:33) Even simpler than that. It turns tables into classes and rows into objects. I actually think SQL is very easy to understand most of it. You can write some SQL golf too, that’s very hard to understand, but SQL at its base and much of the criticism against SQL was it was written for human consumption. It’s actually quite verbose, especially if you’re doing things like inserts over and over again. It’s quite verbose. Insert into table, parentheses, enumerate every column you want to insert, values, parentheses.
DHH
(02:15:33) 甚至比那还要简单：它把数据库表变成类，把行变成对象。其实我觉得 SQL 的大部分内容都非常容易理解。当然，你也可以写出一些“SQL 高尔夫”（极简但晦涩的 SQL），那确实很难懂。但 SQL 本身以及对它的许多批评，其实都源于它是为人类阅读而设计的。它实际上相当冗长，尤其是当你反复执行插入操作时——非常啰嗦：“INSERT INTO 表名（列1, 列2, …）VALUES（值1, 值2, …）”。
(02:16:00) In every column you want to insert values, parentheses, every value that fits with that column, it gets tedious to write SQL by hand, but it’s actually very humanly readable. ActiveRecord just takes that tediousness away, it makes it possible to combine things in a way that a humanly describable language just doesn’t. It composes things into methods and you can combine these methods and you can build structures around them. I don’t dislike SQL, I just like a lot of things in programming, I try to get rid of them. SQL wasn’t really one of them, it was just a sense of, “I don’t want to write the same thing over and over again.” It was a, “Can we be a little more succinct? Can we match it just slightly better to the object orientation without trying to hide away the fact that we’re persisting these objects into a database?”

（02:16:00）在每一列中，你都要插入值、括号，以及与该列匹配的每个值，手动编写 SQL 会变得很繁琐，但实际上 SQL 是非常易于人类阅读的。ActiveRecord 正是消除了这种繁琐性，它能以一种人类可描述语言无法做到的方式组合各种操作。它把这些操作封装成方法，你可以组合这些方法，并围绕它们构建结构。我并不讨厌 SQL；我只是像对待编程中的许多事情一样，试图摆脱重复劳动。SQL 并不是我要彻底抛弃的东西，而只是出于这样一种想法：“我不想一遍又一遍地写同样的东西。” 更确切地说，是“我们能不能更简洁一点？能不能稍微更好地契合面向对象的理念，同时又不掩盖我们正在将这些对象持久化到数据库这一事实？”

(02:16:47) That’s where I think a lot of ORMs went wrong. They tried to live in the pure world of objects, never to consider that those objects had to be consistent into a SQL database, and then they came up with convoluted way of translating back and forth. ActiveRecord says, “You know what? Just accept it.” This record, this object is not going to get saved into some no-SQL database, it’s going to be saved into SQL database, so just structure the whole thing around that. It’s going to have attributes, those attributes are going to respond to columns in the database. It’s not more complicated than that stuff making it so.

（02:16:47）我认为很多 ORM 正是在这一点上走偏了。它们试图生活在纯粹的对象世界里，从不考虑这些对象必须与 SQL 数据库保持一致，结果想出了一些复杂迂回的方式来来回回转换。而 ActiveRecord 则说：“你知道吗？干脆接受现实吧。” 这条记录、这个对象不会被存入某个 NoSQL 数据库，而是会被存入 SQL 数据库，所以整个设计就应该围绕这一点展开。它会有属性，而这些属性会对应数据库中的列。事情其实没那么复杂，就是这么简单。

Lex Fridman
(02:17:22) Yeah, but I should say, I personally love SQL, because I’m an algorithms person, so I love optimization, I love to know how the databases actually work, so I can match the SQL queries and the design of the tables such that there is optimal… Squeeze the optimal performance out of the table. Okay. Based on the actual way that that table is used. I think that pushes to the point that there is value in understanding SQL. I wonder, because I started looking at ActiveRecord and it looks really awesome. Does that make you lazy? Not you, but a person that rolls in and starts using Rails, you can probably get away with never really learning SQL, right?

Lex Fridman
（02:17:22）是的，但我得说，我个人非常喜欢 SQL，因为我是个算法方向的人，所以我热爱优化，也喜欢了解数据库底层到底是怎么工作的，这样我就能写出匹配表结构的 SQL 查询，从而榨取出最优性能。没错，这要基于这张表实际的使用方式。我认为这恰恰说明了理解 SQL 是有价值的。我有点好奇，因为我最近开始研究 ActiveRecord，它看起来真的很棒。但这会不会让人变懒？不是说你，而是指那些刚接触 Rails 的人——他们可能完全不用真正学习 SQL 就能应付过去，对吧？

DHH
(02:18:10) As long as you want to stay at the entry level of competence. This is actually my overarching mission with Rails, is to lower the barrier of entry so far down that someone can start seeing stuff on their browser without basically understanding anything. They can run Rails, new blog, run a couple of generators. They have a whole system… They don’t understand anything, but it’s an invitation to learn more. Where I get fired up, and this ties back to the AI discussion, is when that’s turned into this meme that programmers no longer have to be competent. “The AI is going to figure it out, the generators is going to figure it out. I don’t need to know SQL, ActiveRecord is going to abstract it away from me.” No, no, no. Dude, hold up. The path here is competence. I’m trying to teach you things.

DHH
（02:18:10）只要你愿意停留在入门级的能力水平，那确实可以。实际上，这正是我在 Rails 中的核心使命：把入门门槛降得足够低，以至于一个人即使几乎什么都不懂，也能立刻在浏览器里看到效果。他们可以运行 rails new blog，再跑几个生成器，就拥有了一个完整的系统……尽管他们什么也不理解，但这是一种邀请，鼓励他们进一步学习。让我特别恼火的是——这也和我们之前讨论的 AI 有关——当这种便利被扭曲成一种迷因，认为程序员不再需要具备专业能力。“AI 会搞定一切，生成器会搞定一切。我不需要懂 SQL，ActiveRecord 会替我抽象掉所有细节。” 不，不，不。伙计，等等。这条路通向的是能力，而不是逃避。我是在教你东西。

(02:18:58) I understand I can’t teach you everything in five minutes. No one who’s ever become good at anything worthwhile could be taught everything in five minutes. If you want to be a fully well-rounded application developer, that takes years, but you can actually become somewhat productive in a few days, you can have fun in a few days. For sure, you’re going to have fun in a few minutes, in a few hours, and over time, I can teach you a little more. ActiveRecord says like, “Yeah, yeah. All right, start here and then, next week, we’ll do a class on SQL.”

（02:18:58）我明白，我没法在五分钟内教会你所有东西。任何在有价值的事情上真正有所成就的人，都不可能在五分钟内学会全部。如果你想成为一名全面而成熟的应用开发者，那需要数年时间，但你其实几天之内就能做出点东西，几天之内就能获得乐趣。甚至几分钟、几小时内你就能玩得开心。随着时间推移，我可以慢慢教你更多。ActiveRecord 的态度就像是：“对对，行，先从这里开始，下周我们再上一堂 SQL 课。”

Lex Fridman
(02:19:30) Actually, you have this beautiful expression that I love. That a great programming language, like Ruby, has a soft ramp, but the ramp goes to infinity.

Lex Fridman
（02:19:30）其实，你有一句我特别喜欢的精彩表述：一门优秀的编程语言，比如 Ruby，拥有平缓的入门坡道，但这条坡道通往无限高远之处。

DHH
(02:19:39) That’s exactly right.

DHH
（02:19:39）完全正确。

Lex Fridman
(02:19:40) Yeah. It’s super accessible, super easy to get started-

Lex Fridman
（02:19:40）是的。它超级容易上手，入门极其简单——

DHH
(02:19:43) And it never stops.

DHH
（02:19:43）而且永无止境。

Lex Fridman
(02:19:45) Yeah.

Lex Fridman
（02:19:45）没错。

DHH
(02:19:45) There’s always more to learn. This is one of the reasons I’m still having fun programming, that I’m still learning new things, I can still incorporate new things. The web is deep enough as a domain, you never going to learn all of it.

DHH
（02:19:45）总有更多东西可学。这也是我至今仍享受编程的原因之一：我仍在学习新东西，仍能不断融入新知识。Web 作为一个领域足够深邃，你永远不可能学完它。

Lex Fridman
(02:19:56) Provide sharp knives.

Lex Fridman
（02:19:56）提供锋利的刀。

DHH
(02:19:58) This is a good one, because another way of saying this… The opposite way of saying this, the Java way of saying is, “Do not provide foot guns,” right?

DHH
（02:19:58）这句话说得很好，因为另一种说法——或者说它的反面，也就是 Java 式的说法是：“不要提供会伤到自己的武器”，对吧？

Lex Fridman
(02:20:06) Yeah.

Lex Fridman
（02:20:06）没错。

DHH
(02:20:06) I don’t want to give you a sharp knife. You’re a child, you can’t handle a sharp knife. Here’s a dull butter knife, cut your damn steak, right? That’s a very frustrating experience. You want a sharp knife, even though you might be able to cut yourself. I trust humans in the same way that maths trust humans. Maybe you cut off a finger. All right, you’re not going to do that again. Thankfully, if it was a virtual finger, it’s going to grow back out. Your competence is going to grow, it’s more fun to work with sharp tools.

DHH
（02:20:06）我不想给你一把锋利的刀。你还是个孩子，拿不住锋利的刀。给你这把钝钝的黄油刀，去切你的牛排吧，对吧？这种体验非常令人沮丧。你想要一把锋利的刀，哪怕可能会割伤自己。我对人类的信任，就像数学对人类的信任一样。也许你会不小心切掉一根手指，好吧，那你下次就不会再犯了。幸运的是，如果是虚拟的手指，它还能再长出来。你的能力会随之增长，用锋利的工具工作也更有乐趣。

Lex Fridman
(02:20:35) That actually contributes to the ramp that goes to infinity.

Lex Fridman
（02:20:35）这实际上正是那条通往无限高远的坡道的一部分。

DHH
(02:20:38) Yes, to the learning.

DHH
（02:20:38）是的，对学习而言。

Lex Fridman
(02:20:39) Value-integrated systems.

Lex Fridman
（02:20:39）价值集成的系统。

DHH
(02:20:42) We hit on that one. Rails is trying to solve the whole problem of the web, not just one little component. It’s not leaving you a bunch of pieces you have to put together yourself.

DHH
（02:20:42）我们已经谈到这一点了。Rails 试图解决的是整个 Web 开发的问题，而不只是一个微小的组件。它不会扔给你一堆碎片让你自己拼装。

Lex Fridman
(02:20:51) Progress over stability.

Lex Fridman
（02:20:51）进步优于稳定。

DHH
(02:20:52) You know what? If there’s one that’s dated, it’s probably that one. At this stage, Rails has been incredibly stable over many, many generations. The last major release, Rails 8, was basically a no-op upgrade for anyone running Rails 7. Rails 7 was almost a no-op upgrade for anyone running Rails 6. I used to think it required more churn to get progress, to stay on the leading edge of new stuff, and I wrote this before I experienced the indignity of the 2010s in the JavaScript community, where it seemed like stability was not just unvalued, it was actually despised. The churn in and of itself was a value we should be pursuing. If you were still working with the same framework three months later, you were an idiot, and I saw that and I actually recoiled. If I was going to write the doctrine today, I’d write that differently. I wouldn’t say, “Progress over stability.”

DHH
（02:20:52）你知道吗？如果说有一条原则已经过时了，那很可能就是这一条。现阶段，Rails 在很多很多代版本中都表现得极为稳定。上一个主要版本 Rails 8，对于运行 Rails 7 的用户来说基本上是一次无操作（no-op）升级；而 Rails 7 对于 Rails 6 用户来说也几乎是无操作升级。我曾经以为，要想取得进步、站在新技术的前沿，就必须接受频繁变动。但那是我在亲历 2010 年代 JavaScript 社区那种令人难堪的局面之前的想法——在那里，稳定不仅不被重视，甚至遭到鄙视。变动本身成了一种值得追求的价值。如果你三个月后还在用同一个框架，那你就是个傻瓜。看到这种情况，我其实是退缩了。如果今天让我重写这一信条，我会写得不一样。我不会再写“进步优于稳定”。

Lex Fridman
(02:21:50) Maybe it’d be a function of the age of the programming language also.

Lex Fridman
（02:21:50）也许这也和编程语言的成熟度有关。

DHH
(02:21:55) Maybe or a deeper understanding of the problem. I think part of what’s so fascinating about technology is that we have this perception that everything constantly moves so fast. No, it doesn’t. Everything moves at a glacial pace. There is occasionally a paradigm shift, like what’s happening with AI right now, like what happened with the introduction of the iPhone in 2007, like what happened with the internet in ’95. That’s basically the total sum of my career, three things changed. Everything else in between was incremental small improvements. You can recognize a Rails application written in 2003. I know, because the Basecamp I wrote back then is still operating, making millions of dollars in ARR, servicing customers on the initial version that was launched back then, and it looks like the Rails code, if I squint a little, that I would write today. Most things don’t change, even in computing, and that’s actually a good thing. We saw with the JavaScript ecosystem, what happens when everyone gets just mad about constant churn. Things don’t change that often.

DHH
（02:21:55）也许是这样，或者是因为对问题有了更深入的理解。我觉得技术最迷人的地方之一，就在于我们总有一种错觉，认为一切都在飞速变化。其实并非如此。大多数事物的变化速度慢如冰川。偶尔才会有范式转移，比如当下正在发生的 AI 革命，比如 2007 年 iPhone 的问世，再比如 1995 年互联网的兴起。这基本上就是我整个职业生涯中仅有的三次重大变革。其余的一切都是渐进式的微小改进。你甚至能认出 2003 年写的 Rails 应用。我知道，因为我当年写的 Basecamp 至今仍在运行，每年创造数百万美元的经常性收入（ARR），服务着客户，用的还是最初发布的那个版本。如果我眯起眼睛看，那段 Rails 代码和我今天会写的代码其实差不多。即使在计算机领域，大多数东西也并不会频繁改变，而这其实是一件好事。我们在 JavaScript 生态系统中已经看到了，当所有人都疯狂追逐持续变动时，会发生什么。事实上，事情并没有那么频繁地改变。

Lex Fridman
(02:23:00) By the way, on that small tangent, you just visibly verbally changed your mind with the you of 15 years ago?

Lex Fridman
（02:23:00）顺便提一句，在这个小插曲里，你刚刚明显地在言语上改变了十五年前自己的观点，对吧？

DHH
(02:23:09) Yes.

DHH
（02:23:09）是的。

Why managers are useless

为什么管理者毫无用处

Lex Fridman
(02:23:10) That’s interesting. Have you noticed yourself changing your mind quite a bit over the years?

Lex Fridman
（02:23:10）这很有意思。你有没有注意到，这些年来你自己其实改变了很多想法？
DHH
(02:23:17) I would say, “Oh, yes,” and then also, “Oh, no,” in the sense that there are absolutely fundamental things both about human nature, about institutions, about programming, about business that I’ve changed my mind on, and then I’ve also had experiences that are almost even more interesting, where I thought I had changed my mind and I tried it a new way, realized why I had the original opinion in the first place, and then gone back to it.
