It happens both ways. An example of the later part, for example, was managers at 37 Signals. For the longest time, I would rail against engineering managers as an unnecessary burden on a small or even medium-sized company, and at one point, I actually started doubting myself a little bit. I started thinking like, “Do you know what? Maybe all programmers do need a one-on-one therapy session every week with their engineering manager to be a whole individual.”

我会说“哦，是的”，但也会说“哦，不是的”——意思是，在人性、制度、编程和商业方面，确实有一些根本性的东西让我改变了看法；但我也经历过一些甚至更有趣的情况：我以为自己改变了主意，尝试了新方法后，却意识到当初为何持有原来的观点，于是又回到了原点。这两种情况都会发生。举个后者的例子，比如37signals公司引入经理这件事。长期以来，我一直强烈反对工程经理，认为他们对小型甚至中型公司来说是一种不必要的负担。但有一段时间，我其实开始对自己产生了一点怀疑，心想：“你知道吗？也许所有程序员每周真的都需要和他们的工程经理进行一次一对一的‘心理治疗’，才能成为一个完整的人。”

(02:24:11) We tried that for a couple of years where we hired some very good engineering managers who did engineering management the way you’re supposed to do it, the way it’s done all over the place, and after that, I thought, “No. No, I was right. This was correct, we should not have had managers.” Not every programmer needs a therapy session with an engineering manager every week, we don’t need these endlessly scheduled huddles, we don’t need all these meetings. We just need to leave people the hell alone to work on problems that they enjoy for long stretches of uninterrupted time. That is where happiness is found, that’s where productivity is found, and if you can get away with it, you absolutely should. Engineering management is a necessary evil when that breaks down.

我们曾尝试了几年，聘请了一些非常优秀的工程经理，他们按照“标准做法”、按照业界普遍的方式做工程管理。但之后我意识到：“不，不对，我原来是对的。我们本不该设经理。”并不是每个程序员每周都需要和工程经理来一场“心理治疗”，我们不需要那些没完没了的日会，也不需要那么多会议。我们真正需要的是让人们不受打扰地长时间专注于自己热爱的问题。幸福和生产力就来自这里，如果你能做到这一点，那你就应该这么做。只有当这种理想状态崩溃时，工程管理才成为一种“必要的恶”。

Lex Fridman
(02:24:54) What’s the case for managers then?

那么，支持设置经理的理由又是什么呢？

DHH
(02:24:57) The case for managers is that, if you do have a lot of people, there’s a bunch of work that just crops up. The one-on-one is one example, that programmers need someone to check in with, there’s another idealized version that someone needs to guide the career of juniors, for example, to give them redirecting feedback, and all this other stuff. It’s not that, in the abstract, I don’t agree with some of those things, but in practice, I’ve found that they often create more problems that they solve. A good example here is, can you get feedback from someone who’s not better at your job than you are? You can get some feedback, you can get feedback on how you show up at work. Are you being courteous to others? Are you being a good communicator? Okay, yes, but you can’t get feedback on your work, and that’s more important.

支持设置经理的理由是：当你有很多人时，总会冒出一堆事务性工作。比如一对一沟通——程序员需要有人定期跟进；又比如，有人需要指导初级员工的职业发展，给予纠偏反馈等等。抽象地说，我并不完全反对这些观点，但在实践中，我发现它们常常制造的问题比解决的问题还多。举个好例子：你能从一个在你本职工作上不如你的人那里获得有价值的反馈吗？你可以得到一些反馈，比如你在工作中表现如何——你是否对他人有礼貌？是否善于沟通？好吧，这些当然可以。但你无法从他们那里获得关于你实际工作的反馈，而那才是更重要的。

(02:25:44) It’s more important that you work under and with someone who’s better at your job than you are if you wish to progress in your career, and every single programmer I’ve ever worked with was far more interested in progressing in their career on that metric, getting better at their craft, than they were in picking up pointers that a middle manager could teach them. That’s not saying that there isn’t value in it, it’s not saying there isn’t value in being a better person or a better communicator. Of course, there is all those things, but if I have to choose one or the other, I value competence higher. Again, I cavit this a million times, because I know what people sometimes hear, they hear the genius asshole is just fine, and that’s great and you should excuse all sorts of malicious behavior if someone’s just really good at what they do.

如果你想在职业生涯中进步，更重要的是与比你更擅长本职工作的人共事或在其手下工作。我合作过的每一位程序员，都远比关心“中层经理能教他们哪些职场技巧”更在意如何提升自己的专业技能。这并不是说人际交往或沟通能力没有价值——当然有价值，但如果必须二选一，我更看重专业能力。我要反复强调这一点，因为我知道人们有时会误解我的意思，以为我在说“天才混蛋也没关系”，只要某人特别擅长做事，就可以为所欲为、伤害他人。

(02:26:30) I’m not saying that at all. What I am saying is that the history of competence is a history of learning from people who are better than you, and that relationship should take precedence over all else. That relationship gets put aside a bit when engineering manager’s introduced. Now, the funny thing is this conversation ties back to the earlier things we were talking about. Most engineering managers are actually former programmers. They at least know program to some extent, but what I’ve seen time and again is that they lose their touch, their feel with it very, very quickly and turn into pointy-haired bosses very, very quickly who are really good at checking for updates, “Just seeing where we are on project A here if you need anything,” or, “We’re really to deliver?” Okay, yes. Also, no. Shut up, leave me the hell alone. Let me program and then I’ll come up for air.

我完全不是这个意思。我想说的是：能力的成长史，就是向比你更强的人学习的历史，这种关系应凌驾于一切之上。而一旦引入工程经理，这种关系就会被削弱。有趣的是，这番话又回到了我们之前讨论的话题。大多数工程经理其实是前程序员，至少在一定程度上懂编程。但我一再看到的是，他们很快就会失去对编程的“手感”和直觉，迅速变成那种典型的“尖头发老板”（pointy-haired boss）——他们很擅长追问进度：“我只是想看看项目A现在进展如何，有什么需要帮忙的吗？”或者“我们真的能按时交付吗？”好吧，行。但同时，也别问了。闭嘴，离我远点，让我专心编程，等我写完自然会冒头。

(02:27:22) I’ll talk with other programmers who I can spar with, that we can learn something with, where I can turn the problems over with and we can move forward. If you look back on the history of computer industry, all the great innovation that’s happened, it’s all been done by tiny teams with no engineering managers. Just full of highly-skilled individuals. You’ve had John Carmack on here. I used to look up to its software so much, not just because I love Quake, not just because I loved what they were doing, but because he shared a bit about how the company worked. There were no managers or maybe they had one business guy doing some business stuff, but that was just to get paid. Everything else was basically just designers and programmers, and there were about eight of them and they created goddamn Quake 2. Why do you need all these people again?

我会和其他程序员交流，那些能和我切磋、共同学习、一起反复推敲问题并推动进展的人。回顾计算机行业的历史，所有伟大的创新都是由没有工程经理的小团队完成的——团队里全是高技能个体。你曾经邀请过约翰·卡马克（John Carmack）来做客。我当年非常崇拜id Software，不仅因为我热爱《雷神之锤》（Quake），不仅因为我喜欢他们做的事，更因为他分享过公司是如何运作的：他们根本没有经理，或许只有一个负责商务的人处理付款事宜，仅此而已。其他所有人基本就是设计师和程序员，大概八个人，就做出了该死的《雷神之锤2》。那你到底为什么还需要那么多其他人呢？

(02:28:09) Why do you need all these managers again? I think, again, at a certain scale, it does break down. It’s hard to just have 100,000 programmers running around wild without any product mommies or daddies telling them what to do. I understand that. Then even as I say that, I also don’t understand it, because if you look at something like Gmail for example, that was like a side project done by Buchheit at Google at the time. So much of the enduring long-term value of even all these huge companies were created by people who didn’t have a god damn manager, and that’s not an accident. That’s a direct cause and effect. I’ve turned in some way even more militant over the years against this notion of management, at least for myself and knowing who I am and how I want to work, because the other part of this is I don’t want to be a manager, and maybe this is just me projecting the fact that I’m an introvert who don’t like to talk to people on one-on-one calls every week, but it also encapsulates how I was able to progress my career.

为什么又要这么多经理呢？当然，我也承认，到了一定规模，这种模式确实会失效。让十万个程序员漫无目的地自由发挥，而没有任何“产品爸妈”告诉他们该做什么，这确实很难。我理解这一点。但即便如此，我还是无法完全接受，因为像Gmail这样的产品，当初就是布赫海特（Buchheit）在谷歌做的一个副业项目。事实上，即便是这些巨型公司，其持久且长期的价值，很多都是由那些根本没有经理的人创造的——这绝非偶然，而是直接的因果关系。多年来，我对“管理”这一概念的态度甚至变得更加激进（至少对我自己而言），因为我清楚自己是谁、想要如何工作。此外，我也不想当经理——也许这只是内向性格的投射，我不喜欢每周和人一对一通话；但这也恰恰概括了我是如何实现职业成长的。

(02:29:06) I did not really go to the next level with Ruby or otherwise until I had a door I could close and no one could bother me for six hours straight.

在拥有一个可以关上门、连续六小时无人打扰的环境之前，我在Ruby或其他方面都没能真正迈上新台阶。

Lex Fridman
(02:29:15) In companies probably one of the reasons is it’s very easy to hire managers, and managers also delegate responsibility from you, so if you just have a bunch of programmers running around, your response… It’s work, it’s intellectual work to have to deal with the first principles of every problem that’s going on.

在公司里，可能原因之一是招聘经理很容易，而且经理还能帮你分担责任。所以，如果你只有一群程序员四处奔忙，你的应对方式……那本身就是一种工作——一种需要从第一性原理出发去处理每一个问题的脑力劳动。

DHH
(02:29:39) Yep.

没错。

Lex Fridman
(02:29:39) Manager’s like, “You can relax, all will be taken care of,” but they then hire their own managers, and it just multiplies and multiplies and multiplies. I would love it if some of the great companies we have in the United States, if there was an extra side branch that we could always run… Maybe physicists can come up how to split the simulation to where it just all the managers are removed. Just in that branch, just the PR and the comms people also, and even the lawyers. Just the engineers and let’s just see, and then we merge it back.

经理会说：“你可以放松了，一切都会被安排妥当。”但他们接着又会雇自己的经理，于是层级不断叠加、膨胀。我真希望美国一些伟大的公司能开辟一条平行分支……也许物理学家能想出办法把现实模拟分裂开，在那个分支里彻底移除所有经理——连公关、传播人员甚至律师也都去掉，只剩下工程师，让我们看看会发生什么，然后再合并回来。

DHH
(02:30:16) I have a sense you run that branch at 37 singles for 20 years. I’ve experimented with forking back on the other side, I’ve experimented with having a full-time lawyer on staff, I’ve experimented with having engineering managers, and I can tell you life is much better at 50, 60 people when none of those individuals or none of those roles… It’s never about the individuals, it’s about the roles. None of those roles are in your organization full-time. Occasionally, you need a manager. Occasionally, you need a lawyer. I can play the role of manager occasionally, fine, and then I can set it back down to zero. It’s almost like a cloud surface. I want a manager service I can call on for seven hours this week and then I want to take it down to zero for the next three months.

我觉得你们在37signals已经运行这条“无经理分支”长达20年了。我自己也曾尝试过“回退”到另一条路径——试过配备全职律师，也试过设置工程经理。我可以告诉你：当团队规模在50到60人左右，且完全没有那些角色（注意，不是指具体的人，而是指那些职位）时，生活要好得多。偶尔你确实需要经理，偶尔也需要律师。我可以临时扮演一下经理的角色，没问题，但之后我会立刻把它归零。这就像是云计算一样——我希望有一种“经理即服务”，本周我调用7小时，接下来三个月就完全关闭。

Lex Fridman
(02:31:01) Yeah, I read, I don’t know if this is still the case, that Basecamp is an LLC and doesn’t have a CFO, like a full-time accountant. Is that [inaudible 02:31:10].

是的，我读到过——不知道现在是否依然如此——Basecamp是一家有限责任公司（LLC），并没有首席财务官（CFO），也没有全职会计师。是不是这样？[此处音频不清 02:31:10]
(02:31:10) These days, we do have a head of finance. We did not for the first 19 years of life, I think. We got away with basically just having an accountant do our books in the same way you would do a small ice cream shop, except we would, over time, have done hundreds of millions of dollars in revenue. The scale seemed quirky and, at some point, you can also fall in love with your own quirkiness to a degree that isn’t actually healthy, and I’ve certainly done that over time, and we should have had count the beans a little more diligently, a little earlier. This was part of a blessing of just being wildly profitable and selling software that can have infinite margins, basically, that you can get away with a bunch of stuff that you perhaps shouldn’t. What partially taught me this lesson was when we realized we had not been collecting sales tax in different US states where we had Nexus, and it took us about two years and $5 million in settlements and cleanups to get out of that mess. After that, I went like, “Okay, fine, we can hire a finance person.”

(02:31:10) 如今，我们确实有一位财务负责人。我想，在公司成立的前19年里，我们并没有这样一个人。我们基本上只是请了一名会计来记账，方式就跟一家小型冰淇淋店差不多——只不过我们在此期间累计实现了数亿美元的收入。这种做法在规模上显得古怪；而到了某个阶段，你甚至会迷恋上自己的这种古怪，迷恋到一种实际上并不健康的状态。我本人就曾长期如此。我们本该更早、更认真地“数清楚豆子”（即严格管理财务）。这在一定程度上源于我们早期的巨大盈利优势：我们销售的是边际成本近乎为零的软件，因此可以侥幸避开许多本不该被忽视的问题。让我部分学到这一教训的，是我们意识到自己并未在拥有“关联关系”（Nexus）的多个美国州收取销售税。解决这个烂摊子花了我们大约两年时间和500万美元的和解与清理费用。那之后我才说：“好吧，行吧，我们可以雇个财务人员了。”

Lex Fridman
(02:32:10) Okay.
莱克斯·弗里德曼
(02:32:10) 好的。

DHH
(02:32:11) We now have a wonderful finance person, Ron, who actually ended up replacing something else we used to have. We used to have a full-time data analytics person who would do all sorts of insight mining for, “Why are people signing up for this thing?” We ran that for 10 years and realized, “You know what? If I can have either a data analytics person or an accountant, I’m picking the accountant.”

DHH
(02:32:11) 我们现在有一位出色的财务人员，罗恩（Ron），他实际上取代了我们过去拥有的另一个角色。我们曾经有一名全职的数据分析师，专门挖掘各种洞察，比如“人们为什么注册这个产品？”我们维持了这个岗位十年，后来意识到：“你知道吗？如果只能在数据分析师和会计之间选一个，我会选会计。”

Small teams

小团队

Lex Fridman
(02:32:30) I love this so much on so many levels. Can we just linger on that advice that you’ve given, that small teams are better? I think that’s really less… Less is more. What did you say before? “Worse is better”? Okay, I’m sorry.

莱克斯·弗里德曼
(02:32:30) 这一点我在很多层面上都非常认同。我们能不能就你刚才给出的建议多聊一会儿——小团队更好？我觉得这真的是“少即是多”。你之前是不是还说过“差即是好”？啊，抱歉。

DHH
(02:32:47) Worse is better on adoption with technology a lot of times.

DHH
(02:32:47) 在技术采纳方面，“差即是好”的情况其实很常见。

Lex Fridman
(02:32:51) Yeah.

莱克斯·弗里德曼
(02:32:51) 是的。

DHH
(02:32:51) I think it actually comes out of the same thing. It comes out of the fact that many of the great breakthroughs are created by not even just tiny teams, but individuals, individuals writing something. An individual writing something on some parameter, what they do is worse. Of course, it’s worse when one person has to make something that a huge company have hundreds if not thousands of developers that they can have work on that problem, but in so many other parameters, that worstness is the value, that less is the value. In Getting Real, which we wrote back in 2006, we talk about this notion of less software. When we first got started with Basecamp back in 2004, people would ask us all the time, “Aren’t you petrified of Microsoft? They have so many more resources, they have so many more programmers. What if they take a liking to your little niche here and they show up and they just throw a thousand programmers at the problem?”

DHH
(02:32:51) 我认为这其实源自同一个理念：许多重大突破并非出自小型团队，而是由个体创造的——某个人独自写出某些东西。从某个维度来看，他们做出来的东西确实是“更差”的。当然，当一个人要做出某样东西，而大公司却能投入数百甚至数千名开发者来解决同一问题时，他的成果自然显得更差。但在许多其他维度上，正是这种“差”构成了价值，这种“少”才是真正的优势。我们在2006年写的《Getting Real》一书中就谈到了“更少的软件”这一理念。2004年我们刚开始做Basecamp时，人们总是问我们：“你们难道不害怕微软吗？他们拥有那么多资源、那么多程序员。万一他们看上了你们这个小众市场，直接派一千个程序员来对付这个问题怎么办？”

(02:33:46) My answer, perhaps partly because I was like 24 was, first of all, “No, no care in the world,” but the real answer was they’re not going to produce the same thing. You cannot produce the software that Basecamp is with a team of a 1,000 people. You will build the software that 1,000 people build, and that’s not the same thing at all. So much of the main breakthrough in both end-user systems but also in open-source systems and fundamental systems, they’re done by individuals or very small teams. Even all these classical histories of Apple has always been like, well, there’s a big organization, but then you had the team that was actually working on the breakthrough. It was four people, it was eight people, it was never 200.

(02:33:46) 我当时的回答——可能部分是因为我才24岁——首先是：“完全不在乎。”但真正的原因是：他们根本做不出同样的东西。你不可能用一千人的团队做出Basecamp这样的软件。你只会做出“一千人团队会做的那种软件”，而这完全是两回事。无论是面向终端用户的产品，还是开源系统乃至基础系统，其中绝大多数重大突破都是由个体或极小的团队完成的。就连苹果公司的经典历史也总是如此：虽然它是个庞大的组织，但真正实现突破的团队往往只有四个人、八个人，从来不是二百人。

Lex Fridman
(02:34:32) The large team seems to slow things down.

莱克斯·弗里德曼
(02:34:32) 大团队似乎会让事情变慢。

DHH
(02:34:36) Yes.

DHH
(02:34:36) 是的。

Lex Fridman
(02:34:37) It’s so fascinating, part of it’s the manager thing.

莱克斯·弗里德曼
(02:34:37) 这真的很有趣，其中一部分原因在于管理者的问题。

DHH
(02:34:40) Because humans don’t scale, communication between humans certainly don’t scale. You basically get the network-cost effect. Every time you add a new node, it goes up exponentially. This is perhaps the key thing of why I get to be so fond of having no managers at Basecamp, because our default team size is two. One programmer, one designer, one feature. When you’re operating at that level of scale, you don’t need sophistication, you don’t need advanced methodologies, you don’t need multiple layers of management, because you can just do. The magic of small teams is that they just do. They don’t have to argue, because we don’t have to set direction, we won’t have to worry about the road map. We can just sit down and make something, and then see if it’s good. When you can get away with just making things, you don’t have to plan, and if you can get out of planning, you can follow the truth that emerges from the code, from the product, from the thing you’re working on in the moment.

DHH
(02:34:40) 因为人类无法线性扩展，人与人之间的沟通尤其无法扩展。你基本上会遇到“网络成本效应”——每增加一个节点，沟通成本就呈指数级上升。这或许正是我在Basecamp如此坚持不设管理者的关键原因：我们的默认团队规模就是两人——一名程序员、一名设计师，负责一个功能。当你以这种规模运作时，你不需要复杂的流程，不需要高深的方法论，也不需要多层管理，因为你只需要动手去做。小团队的魔力就在于：他们就是去做。他们不需要争论方向，因为我们不必预先设定方向，也不必操心路线图。我们只需坐下来做出点东西，然后看看它好不好。当你能靠直接动手做出东西来推进工作时，就不需要计划；而一旦摆脱了计划，你就能追随从代码中、从产品中、从你当下所做之事中浮现出来的真相。

(02:35:43) You know far more about what the great next step is when you’re one step behind, rather than if you try 18 months in advance to map out all the steps. “How do we get from here to very far away?” You know what? That’s difficult to imagine in advance, because humans are very poor at that. Maybe AI one day will be much better than us, but humans can put one foot in front of each other. That’s not that hard, and that allows you to get away with all that sophistication. The process has become much simpler, you need far fewer people, it compounds, you need much less process, you need to waste less time in meetings. You can just spend these long glorious days and weeks of uninterrupted time solving real problems you care about and that are valuable, and you’re going to find that that’s what the market actually wants.

(02:35:43) 当你刚走完一步时，你对下一步该怎么走的理解，远比试图提前18个月规划所有步骤要清晰得多。“我们如何从这里走到很远的地方？”你知道吗？这很难提前想象，因为人类在这方面非常不擅长。也许有一天AI会比我们强得多，但人类能做到的，就是一步步往前走。这其实并不难，也因此你完全可以省去那些繁复的流程。整个过程变得简单多了：你需要的人更少，这又进一步减少了流程需求，也让你不必浪费大量时间在会议上。你可以把大段大段不受打扰的宝贵时间，投入到解决你真正关心且有价值的实际问题上——你会发现，这恰恰才是市场真正想要的。

(02:36:33) No one is buying something because there’s a huge company behind it, most of the time. They’re buying something because it’s good, and the way you get something good is you don’t sit around and have a meeting about it, you try stuff, you build stuff.

(02:36:33) 大多数时候，人们购买某样东西，并不是因为它背后有家大公司，而是因为它本身很好。而要做出好东西，靠的不是围坐开会讨论，而是不断尝试、不断构建。

Lex Fridman
(02:36:48) It really is incredible what one person, honestly one person can do in 100 hours of deep work, of focused work. Even less.

莱克斯·弗里德曼
(02:36:48) 真的令人难以置信——一个人，真的就一个人，在100小时的深度专注工作中能完成多少事。甚至更少时间也行。

DHH
(02:36:58) I’ll tell you this, I tracked exactly the number of hours I spent on the first version of Basecamp. I was doing this, because at the time, I was working on a contract basis for Jason. He was paying me… I was going to say $15 an hour, that’s what I got paid when we first got started. I think he had bumped my pay to a glorious $25, but I was billing him, and I know that the invoice for the first version of Basecamp was 400 hours. That’s what it took for one sole individual in 2004 to create an entire system that has then gone on to gross hundreds of millions of dollars and continues to do extremely well. One person, just me setting up everything. Part of that story is Ruby, part of that story’s Rails, but a lot of it is also just me plus Jason plus Ryan plus Matt.

DHH
(02:36:58) 我告诉你，我精确记录了自己开发Basecamp第一个版本所花的工时。当时我是按合同为杰森（Jason）工作的。他付我……本来想说每小时15美元，那是我们刚开始时的报酬。我想后来他慷慨地涨到了每小时25美元。我当时给他开发票，我记得Basecamp第一版的发票正好是400小时。这就是2004年一个单打独斗的人创造出一整套系统所花的时间——这套系统后来累计创造了数亿美元的收入，至今仍表现极佳。就一个人，所有事情都是我一手搭建的。这其中一部分归功于Ruby，一部分归功于Rails，但更多其实是“我 + 杰森 + 瑞安 + 马特”这个组合。

(02:37:46) That was the entire company at the time, and we could create something of sheer sustaining value with such a tiny team, because we were a tiny team. Not despite off. Small is not a stepping stone. This is the other thing that people get into their head, this is one of the big topics about a rework, that it gave entrepreneurs the permission to embrace being a small team not as a waypoint, not as, “I’m trying to become 1,000 people.” No, I actually like being a small team. Small teams are more fun. If you ask almost anyone, I’m sure Toby would say this too, even at his scale, the sheer enjoyment of building something is in the enjoyment of building it with a tiny team. Now, you can have impact at a different scale when you have a huge company, I fully recognize that and I see the appeal of it, but in the actual building of things, it’s always small teams. Always.

(02:37:46) 那就是当时整个公司全部成员。我们能用如此微小的团队创造出具有持久价值的产品，恰恰正是因为我们的团队很小——而不是“尽管”团队小。小不是通往更大的跳板。这是很多人误解的一点，也是《重做》（Rework）一书的重要主题之一：它赋予了创业者一种许可——你可以安心做一个小团队，不是作为过渡阶段，也不是抱着“我要变成千人公司”的目标。不，我就是喜欢小团队。小团队更有意思。如果你问几乎任何人，我相信托比（Toby）也会这么说，哪怕在他现在的规模下，打造产品的纯粹乐趣，正来自于和一个小团队一起创造的过程。当然，我完全承认，当你拥有一家大公司时，你可以产生不同量级的影响力，我也理解其中的吸引力。但在实际构建产品这件事上，永远都是小团队在起作用。永远都是。

Jeff Bezos

杰夫·贝索斯

Lex Fridman
(02:38:39) How do you protect the small team? Basecamp has successfully stayed small. What’s been the dragon you had to fight off? Basically, you make a lot of money, there’s a temptation to grow, so how do you not grow?

莱克斯·弗里德曼
(02:38:39) 你们是如何保护小团队的？Basecamp成功地保持了小规模。你们必须击退的“恶龙”是什么？本质上，你们赚了很多钱，就有扩张的诱惑，那你们是怎么做到不扩张的？

DHH
(02:38:55) Don’t take venture capital.

DHH
(02:38:55) 不要拿风险投资。

Lex Fridman
(02:38:56) Okay, that that’s step one.

莱克斯·弗里德曼
(02:38:56) 好的，这是第一步。

DHH
(02:38:57) That is point number one.

DHH
(02:38:57) 这是第一条。

Lex Fridman
(02:39:00) First of all-

莱克斯·弗里德曼
(02:39:00) 首先——

DHH
(02:39:00) Number two is-

DHH
(02:39:00) 第二条是——

Lex Fridman
(02:39:01) … everybody takes venture capital, so you already went.

莱克斯·弗里德曼
(02:39:01) ……大家都拿风险投资，所以你已经领先了。
(02:39:05) That’s been the answer for the longest time, because the problem isn’t just venture capital, it’s other people’s money. Once you take other people’s money, completely understandably, they want a return, and they would prefer to have the largest return possible, because it’s not them sitting in the code, it’s not them getting the daily satisfaction out of building something, chiseling beautiful code poems out of the editor, right? They don’t get that satisfaction. They get the satisfaction maybe of seeing something nice put into the world, that’s fair, but they certainly also get a satisfaction of a higher return. There is this sense, certainly in venture capital, stated in venture capital, that the whole point of you taking the money is to get to $1 billion or more.
（02:39:05）长期以来，这一直是答案所在，因为问题不仅在于风险投资，而在于“别人的钱”。一旦你拿了别人的钱——完全可以理解——他们就希望获得回报，而且越高的回报越好，因为他们不是坐在代码前的人，也不是每天从构建产品、在编辑器中雕琢出优美的“代码诗篇”中获得满足感的人，对吧？他们得不到那种满足。他们或许会因看到美好的事物被带到世界上而感到欣慰，这很公平，但他们肯定也会因获得更高的回报而感到满足。在风险投资领域，尤其明确地存在这样一种观念：你拿这笔钱的全部目的，就是要做到十亿美元甚至更高的估值。

(02:39:44) Now, the path to that usually does go through running established playbooks, and then when it comes to software, the enterprise sales playbook is that playbook. If you’re doing B2B, software SaaS, you will try to find product market fit, and the second you have it, you will abandon your small and medium-sized accounts to chase the big whales with a huge sales force and, by then, you’re 1,000 people and life sucks.
（02:39:44）通常，通往这一目标的道路就是照搬既定的“剧本”；而在软件行业，这个剧本就是企业级销售模式。如果你做的是面向企业的SaaS软件，你会努力寻找产品市场契合点，而一旦找到，你就会立刻放弃中小客户，转而用庞大的销售团队去追逐那些“大鲸鱼”客户。到那时，公司规模已经膨胀到一千人，生活也就变得糟糕透顶。

Lex Fridman
Lex Fridman

(02:40:10) That said, people are just curious about this. Have gotten a chance to get to know Jeff Bezos. He invested in Basecamp, not controlling…
（02:40:10）话虽如此，人们对此还是很好奇。我有机会认识了杰夫·贝索斯。他投资了Basecamp，但并非控股……

DHH
DHH

(02:40:22) He bought secondaries. This was the funny thing, is that when… Investing have these two dual meanings. Normally, when people think about investing, they think you’re putting in growth capital, because you want the business to hire more people, to do more R&D, so they can grow bigger. Bezos didn’t do that, actually. He bought an ownership stake directly from Jason and I, and 100% of the proceeds of that purchase went into my and Jason’s bank account. Personal bank accounts. Not a single cent went into the account of the company, because we didn’t need the money to grow. What we needed or what we certainly enjoyed was, to some extent, maybe the vote of confidence, but more so the security of taking a little bit off the tables is that we dared turn down the big bucks from venture capitals.
（02:40:22）他买的是二手股份（secondary shares）。有趣的是……“投资”这个词有双重含义。通常人们想到投资时，会认为这是注入增长资本，目的是让公司雇佣更多人、投入更多研发，从而实现更大规模的增长。但贝索斯实际上并没有这么做。他是直接从我和杰森手中购买了部分股权，这笔交易的全部收益100%进入了我和杰森的个人银行账户——一分一厘都没有进入公司账户，因为我们并不需要这笔钱来扩张业务。我们真正需要（或者说更享受）的，某种程度上或许是这种信任投票，但更重要的是，从桌上拿走一小部分收益所带来的安全感，让我们有底气拒绝来自风投的大笔资金。

(02:41:14) It was essentially a vaccine against wanting to take a larger check from people who then wanted to take the company to something enormous that we didn’t want to go with it. Jeff gave Jason and I just enough money that we were comfortable turning all these people down in a way where, if it had turned belly up six months later, we wouldn’t have been kicking ourselves and gone, “We had something here that was worth millions, and now we have nothing and I have to worry about rent and groceries again.”
（02:41:14）这本质上是一种“疫苗”，防止我们被那些想把公司推向我们并不想要的巨大规模的人开出的大额支票所诱惑。杰夫给我们的钱刚刚好，足以让我们安心地拒绝所有其他投资人——即使六个月后事情彻底失败，我们也不会捶胸顿足地后悔：“我们曾经拥有价值数百万的东西，现在却一无所有，又得为房租和买菜发愁了。”

Lex Fridman
Lex Fridman

(02:41:44) It is a vote of confidence. I’d love to hear Jeff’s side of this story of why, because he doesn’t need the money. I think it probably is just believing in people and wanting to have cool stuff be created in the world and make money off of it, but not like-
（02:41:44）这确实是一种信任投票。我很想知道杰夫自己对这件事的看法——为什么他会这么做？因为他根本不需要这笔钱。我觉得可能只是因为他相信某些人，希望世界上能诞生一些酷炫的东西，并从中赚点钱，但并不是那种……

DHH
DHH

(02:42:05) 100% the motivation for Jeff wasn’t a return, because he actually has a team, his private office, that runs these investments, who did the calculus on the investment pitch we gave him, which was so ridiculous that Jason and I were laughing our asses off when we were writing down our metrics. I was like, “No one’s going to pay this. No one is going to give us this multiple of this amount of revenue, and that’s fine.” I mean, we took the call essentially out of an awe that Jeff Bezos even wanted to look at us. “Do you know what? We don’t want venture capital, we don’t need other people’s money, but let’s just give him a bullshit number that no sane person would actually say yes to, and then we can each go our own way.”
（02:42:05）杰夫的动机100%不是为了财务回报。事实上，他有一个私人办公室团队专门负责这类投资，他们对我们提出的投资方案做了财务测算——那个方案荒谬到我和杰森在写下那些指标时笑得前仰后合。我当时就说：“没人会付这个价钱的。没人会给我们这么高的估值倍数，相对于我们的收入来说，但这没关系。” 我们之所以接这个电话，纯粹是因为惊讶于杰夫·贝索斯居然愿意关注我们。“你知道吗？我们不想要风投资金，也不需要别人的钱，那我们就随便报个离谱的数字吧，反正正常人绝不会答应，然后大家各走各路。”

(02:42:48) His investment team said like, “Jeff, no way. This makes no economic sense at all, they’re asking for way too much money with way too little revenue,” and Jeff just went like, “I don’t care, I want to invest in this guy,” because to him, at the time, it was chump change. Jason and I each got a few million dollars, whatever the currency swing between the yen and the dollar that day probably moved 10X for his net worth than our investment did. Jeff seemed genuinely interested in being around interesting people, interesting companies, helping someone go to distance. I actually look back on that relationship with some degree of regret, because I took that vote of confidence for granted in ways that I’m a little bit ashamed of. Over the years, I’ve been more critical about some of the things that Amazon had done that I feel now is justified.
（02:42:48）他的投资团队说：“杰夫，绝对不行。这完全没经济逻辑，他们收入那么少却要那么多钱。”但杰夫只是说：“我不在乎，我就想投资这个人。”因为对他来说，那点钱不过是小数目。我和杰森各自拿到了几百万美元——而那天日元兑美元的汇率波动，可能给他净资产带来的变动都比我们这笔投资高出十倍。杰夫似乎真心喜欢和有趣的人、有趣的公司待在一起，也乐于帮助别人走得更远。回想这段关系，我其实有些遗憾，因为我曾以一种令自己略感羞愧的方式，把那份信任视为理所当然。这些年来，我对亚马逊的一些做法提出了更多批评，现在我觉得那些批评是合理的。

(02:43:41) That’s just part of that processing of it, but on the economic sense, he gave us that confidence. He gave us the economic confidence, but then he also gave us the confidence of a CEO running, perhaps at the time the most important internet business in the US, showing up to our calls, which we would have with him once a year, and basically, just going like, “Yeah, you guys are doing awesome stuff. You should just keep doing awesome stuff. I read your book, it’s awesome. You launched this thing, it’s awesome. You should just do more of that. I don’t actually know how to run your business, you guys know.”
（02:43:41）这只是我处理那段经历的一部分。但从经济角度看，他确实给了我们信心——不仅是经济上的安全感，更是一位当时或许执掌着美国最重要互联网企业的CEO，每年都会抽出时间跟我们通话，基本上就是说：“你们做得太棒了，继续做下去就好。我读了你们的书，很棒；你们发布了这个产品，也很棒。你们就该多做这样的事。我其实不知道该怎么运营你们的公司，你们自己才最清楚。”

Lex Fridman
Lex Fridman

(02:44:13) The book was out. From a fan perspective, I’m curious about how Jeff Bezos is able to see… Because to me, you and Jason are special humans in the space of tech, and the fact that Jeff was able to see that, right? How hard is it to see that?
（02:44:13）那本书已经出版了。作为一个粉丝，我很好奇杰夫·贝索斯是怎么看出来的……因为在我的眼里，你和杰森在科技圈里是非常特别的人，而杰夫居然能看出来这一点，对吧？要看出这一点有多难？

DHH
DHH

(02:44:29) He certainly saw it very early, and I think this is something that Jeff does better than almost anyone else. He spots that opportunity so far in advance of anyone else even opened their eyes to it, or certainly is willing to bet on it far early and far harder than anyone else is, and he’s just right time and again. We were not the only investment that he made and, certainly, Amazon had an extremely long-term vision, far longer than I have ever had the gumption to keep… I think of myself as a long-term thinker, I’m playing a child’s game compared to the game that Jeff is playing. When I looked at Amazon’s economics around the dot-com boom and bust, they looked ridiculous. They were losing so much money, they were so hated by the market. No one believed that it was going to turn into what it is, but Jeff did in a way that, that level of conviction, I really aspire to.
（02:44:29）他确实很早就看出来了，我认为这是杰夫比几乎任何人都强的地方。他发现机会的时间远远早于其他人甚至还没意识到它的存在，而且他愿意比任何人都更早、更坚定地下注，并且一次又一次地证明自己是对的。我们并不是他唯一的投资对象。而且毫无疑问，亚马逊拥有极其长远的愿景——远比我所能坚持的还要长远得多……我一直自认为是个长期思考者，但跟杰夫玩的游戏相比，我简直是在玩儿童游戏。回想互联网泡沫繁荣与破灭时期，亚马逊的经济模型看起来荒谬至极：巨额亏损，被市场极度厌恶。没人相信它会变成今天的样子，但杰夫做到了——他对信念的坚持程度，正是我所向往的。

(02:45:23) I think that’s one of the main things I’ve taken away from that relationship is that you can just believe in yourself. To that degree against those odds? That’s ridiculous. He did that so many times at our level that it’s pathetic if I’m doubting myself.
（02:45:23）我想，从那段关系中我学到的最重要的一点就是：你可以如此坚定地相信自己。在那样不利的条件下还能做到这种程度？简直不可思议。他在我们这个层面上已经无数次展现出这种信念，如果我还对自己心存怀疑，那就太可悲了。

Lex Fridman
Lex Fridman

(02:45:42) Yeah. I think Amazon is one of those companies. It’s come under a bunch of criticism over the years. This is something about humans that I don’t appreciate so much, that we take for granted the positive that a thing brings real quick, and then we just start criticizing the thing. It’s the Wi-Fi and the airplanes.
（02:45:42）是啊。我觉得亚马逊就是这样的公司之一。多年来它受到了大量批评。这是人性中我不太欣赏的一点：我们很快就把某件事带来的积极影响视为理所当然，然后就开始批评它。就像Wi-Fi和飞机一样。

DHH
DHH

(02:46:02) That’s exactly it.
（02:46:02）正是如此。

Lex Fridman
Lex Fridman

(02:46:04) I think Amazon, there could be a case made that Amazon is one of the greatest companies in the last 100 years.
（02:46:04）我觉得完全可以提出一个观点：亚马逊是过去一百年中最伟大的公司之一。

DHH
DHH

(02:46:15) For sure, I think it’s an easy case to make. What I also think is that the price you pay to be one of the greatest companies in the last 100 years is a lot of detractors, a lot of pushback, a lot of criticism. That this is actually order restored in the universe. One of my favorite teachers in all the time I’ve been on the internet is Kathy Sierra. I don’t know if you know her work, but she was active for only a few short years before the cruel internet ran her off, but she wrote a blog called Creating Passionate Users, and she carved into my brain this notion of balance in the universe. If you’re creating something of value that a lot of people love, you must create an equal and opposite force of haters. You cannot have people who love what you do without also having people who hate what you do.
（02:46:15）当然，我觉得这个观点很容易成立。但我也认为，成为过去百年最伟大公司的代价，就是会招致大量反对者、阻力和批评。这其实是宇宙秩序的恢复。我在互联网上这些年，最喜欢的老师之一是凯西·西埃拉（Kathy Sierra）。不知道你是否了解她的作品，但她只活跃了短短几年，就被残酷的网络环境逼退了。她曾写过一个名为《Creating Passionate Users》（创造热情用户）的博客，她在我脑海中刻下了一个关于宇宙平衡的概念：如果你创造出某种很多人喜爱的有价值的东西，就必然会催生一股同等强度的仇恨力量。你不可能只有爱你作品的人，却没有恨你作品的人。

(02:47:05) The only escape from that is mediocrity. If you are so boring and so uninteresting that no one gives a damn whether you exist or not, yeah, you don’t get the haters, but you also don’t get the impact of people who really enjoy your work. I think Amazon is that just at the massive scale, right? They’ve brought so much value and change to technology, to commerce that they must simply have a black hole size of haters. Otherwise, the universe is simply going to tip over.
（02:47:05）唯一能逃避这种情况的方式就是平庸。如果你无聊到没人关心你是否存在，那确实不会有黑粉，但同样也不会有真正欣赏你作品的人所带来的影响力。我认为亚马逊就是这样一个极端大规模的例子，对吧？他们为技术和商业带来了如此巨大的价值和变革，必然也会吸引一个“黑洞级别”的仇恨群体。否则，宇宙的天平就会倾覆。
(02:47:34) Let me ask you about small teams. You mentioned Jason a bunch of times, Jason Fried. You have been partners for a long, long time. Perhaps it’s fair to say he’s more on the the design, business side and you’re the tech, the engineering wizard. How have you guys over all these years, creating so many amazing products, not murder each other? It’s a great story of partnership. What can you say about collaboration? What can you say about Jason that you love, that you’ve learned from? Why does this work?

(02:47:34) 我想问问你关于小团队的事。你多次提到杰森（Jason），也就是杰森·弗里德（Jason Fried）。你们已经合作了非常非常久。或许可以公平地说，他更偏向设计和商业方面，而你是技术、工程方面的奇才。这么多年来，你们一起创造了这么多出色的产品，却始终没有把对方干掉？这真是一段绝佳的合伙故事。你能谈谈协作吗？你能说说你欣赏杰森的哪些地方、从他身上学到了什么？为什么这种合作关系能行得通？

DHH
(02:48:07) First, I’ll say we have tried to murder each other several times over the years, but far less, I think in the last decade. In the early days, our product discussions were so fierce that, when we were having them in the office and there were other employees around, some of them were legitimately worried that the company was about to fall apart, because the volume coming out of the room would be so high and sound so acrimonious that they were legitimately worried the whole thing was going to fall apart. You know what’s funny? Is that it never felt like that in the moment. It always felt like just a peak vigorous search for something better, and that we were able to stomach that level of adversity on the merits of an idea, because it was about the idea. It wasn’t about the person and it never really got personal. Not even never, really, it didn’t get personal. It wasn’t like, “Jason, you’re an asshole.” It was like, “Jason, you’re an idiot, and you’re an idiot because you’re looking at this problem the wrong way, and let me tell you the right way to do it.”

DHH
(02:48:07) 首先，我得说，这些年来我们确实好几次都想把对方干掉，不过我想在过去十年里这种情况少多了。早期的时候，我们对产品的讨论激烈到什么程度呢？当我们在办公室里争论，周围还有其他员工时，有些人真的担心公司马上就要散伙了——因为房间里传出的声音太大、听起来太剑拔弩张，以至于他们真心觉得整个公司要完蛋了。但有趣的是，当时我们自己完全没那种感觉。对我们来说，那始终只是一场为了找到更好方案而展开的激烈探索。我们之所以能承受那种程度的对抗，是因为争论的焦点始终是观点本身，而不是人。它从未真正变成人身攻击。甚至可以说，它根本就没变成过人身攻击。我们不会说：“杰森，你是个混蛋。”而是说：“杰森，你是个傻瓜，因为你看待这个问题的方式错了，让我来告诉你正确的方法。”

Lex Fridman
(02:49:21) As a small tangent, let me say that some people have said, we’ll probably return to this, that you sometimes can have flights of temper on the internet and so on. I never take it that way, because it is the same kind of ilk. Maybe I haven’t seen the right traces of temper, but usually, it’s about the idea, and it’s just excited, passionate human.

Lex Fridman
(02:49:21) 顺便岔开一下话题，有人说——我们可能之后还会回到这一点——你有时在网上会发脾气之类。但我从来不是那样理解的，因为这其实属于同一种性质。也许我没看到你真正发火的样子，但通常情况下，你的激烈反应都是围绕观点展开的，只是出于一个充满激情、情绪高涨的人的自然表现。

DHH
(02:49:46) That’s exactly what I like to think of it as. It doesn’t always come across as that and I can see why spectators in particular sometimes would see something that looks like I’m going after the man rather than the ball. I do think I’ve tried to get better at that, but in my relationship with-

DHH
(02:49:46) 这正是我希望别人这样理解我的方式。但我知道，这种态度并不总能被准确传达出来，我也明白为什么旁观者有时会觉得我是在针对人，而不是针对事。我确实一直在努力改进这一点，但在与杰森的关系中——

DHH
(02:50:00) I do think I’ve tried to get better at that, but in my relationship with Jason, I think it’s worked so well because we have our own distinct areas of competence, where we fully trust each other. Jason trusts me to make the correct technical decisions. I trust him to make the correct design and product direction decisions, and then we can overlap and share on the business, on marketing, on writing, on other aspects of it. So that’s one thing, is that if you’re starting a business with someone where you do exactly the same as they do, and you’re constantly contesting who’s the more competent person, I think that’s far more difficult and far more volatile. So if you’re starting a business and you’re both programmers and you both work on the same kind of programming, good luck. I think that’s hard.

DHH
(02:50:00) 我确实一直在努力改进，但我和杰森的合作之所以如此成功，是因为我们各自拥有明确且不同的专长领域，并且彼此完全信任。杰森相信我能做出正确的技术决策，我相信他能做出正确的设计和产品方向决策。至于商业、营销、写作等其他方面，我们可以交叉协作、共同参与。所以有一点很重要：如果你和一个跟你做完全相同事情的人一起创业，总是不断争执谁更胜任，那会困难得多，也更容易产生冲突。比如，如果你和另一个人都是程序员，而且都做同类型的编程工作，那祝你好运吧——我觉得那真的很难。

(02:50:49) I tried to pick an easier path, working with a designer, where I knew that at least half of the time I could just delegate to his experience and competence and say like, do you know what? I may have an opinion. I have an opinion all the time on design, but I don’t have to win the argument because I trust you. Now, occasionally we would have overlaps on business or direction where we’d both feel like we had a strong stake in the game and we both had a claim to competence in that area, but then for whatever reason, we also both had a long-term vision, where I would go, do you know what? I think we’re wrong here, but as I learned from Jeff Bezos, by the way, I’m going to disagree and commit. That was one of those early lessons he gave us, that was absolutely crucial and perhaps even instrumental in ensuring that Jason and I have been working together for a quarter of a century. Disagree and commit is one of the all time Jeff Bezos’ greats.

(02:50:49) 我选择了一条更轻松的路——和一位设计师合作。我知道至少有一半时间，我可以直接把事情交给他，依靠他的经验和能力。我会对自己说：“你知道吗？我对设计当然有自己的看法，我一直都有意见，但我不必非赢不可，因为我信任你。”当然，偶尔在商业或方向问题上也会出现重叠，那时我们都会觉得自己在这个领域有很强的发言权，也都自认具备相应的能力。但不知为何，我们同时又都拥有长远的愿景。这时我就会想：“你知道吗？我觉得我们在这点上可能是错的，但正如我从杰夫·贝索斯那里学到的那样——我要‘不同意但承诺支持’（disagree and commit）。”这是他早年教给我们的一课，至关重要，甚至可以说是确保我和杰森能合作整整二十五年的关键因素之一。“不同意但承诺支持”绝对是杰夫·贝索斯最伟大的理念之一。

Lex Fridman
(02:51:42) I’m just surprised that Yoko Ono hasn’t come along. You know what I mean? There’s so many Yokos in this world.

Lex Fridman
(02:51:42) 我只是惊讶居然没有“小野洋子”（Yoko Ono）出现。你懂我意思吧？这世界上有太多“小野洋子”了。

DHH
(02:51:51) It might’ve happened if not in part because we don’t sit on each other’s lap all the time. Most of our careers, we haven’t even lived in the same city. I lived in Chicago for a couple of years while we were getting going after I’d moved to the US in 2005, but then I moved to Malibu and then I lived in Spain and then I lived in Copenhagen. And Jason and I, from the foundation of our relationship learned how to work together in a remarkably efficient way where we didn’t have to actually talk that much. On any given week, I’d be surprised if Jason and I spent more than two hours of direct exchange and communication.

DHH
(02:51:51) 如果不是因为我们从不整天黏在一起，这种情况可能早就发生了。在我们合作的大部分时间里，我们甚至不住在同一个城市。2005年我搬到美国后，最初几年住在芝加哥；后来搬去了马里布，之后又住过西班牙，再后来是哥本哈根。从我们关系建立之初，杰森和我就学会了一种异常高效的合作方式——我们根本不需要频繁交流。随便挑一周，如果我和杰森直接沟通的时间超过两个小时，我都会感到惊讶。

Lex Fridman
(02:52:33) Yeah. Sometimes it’s the basic human frictions that just accumulate all time.

Lex Fridman
(02:52:33) 是啊。有时候就是那些日常的人际摩擦不断累积起来。

DHH
(02:52:37) Yes. I think if you rub up against another person, that person damn well better be your spouse, if it’s too much for too long.

DHH
(02:52:37) 没错。我觉得，如果你长时间、高强度地和另一个人密切接触，那这个人最好是你配偶，否则真的很难熬。

Lex Fridman
(02:52:43) Yeah. But even there, COVID has really tested the relationship. It’s fascinating to watch.

Lex Fridman
(02:52:43) 是啊。但即使是夫妻关系，新冠疫情也对其造成了严峻考验。这现象很有意思。

DHH
(02:52:48) It has, and I do think that having some separation, which is kind of counterintuitive because I think a lot of people think the more collaboration you can have, the better. The more ideas that can bounce back and forth, the better. And both Jason and I, for whatever reason came to the conclusion early on in careers, absolutely not. That’s complete baloney. This is why we were huge proponents of remote work. This is why I enjoy working in my home office where I can close the door and not see another human for six hours at the time. I don’t want to bounce ideas off you all the time. I want to bounce ideas off you occasionally and then I want to go off and implement those ideas.

DHH
(02:52:48) 确实如此。我认为保持一定的距离非常重要——虽然这有点反直觉，因为很多人觉得协作越多越好，想法来回碰撞得越频繁越好。但不知为何，杰森和我在职业生涯早期就得出结论：绝对不是这样，那完全是胡扯。这也是为什么我们一直是远程办公的坚定倡导者。这也是为什么我喜欢在自己的家庭办公室工作——我可以关上门，连续六个小时不见任何人。我不想一直和你反复讨论想法，我只想偶尔和你碰撞一下想法，然后自己去实现它们。

(02:53:24) There’s way too much bouncing going on and not enough scoring, not enough dunking, and I think this is one of the great traps of executive rule. Once a founder elevates themselves all the way up to an executive, where what they’re doing is just telling other people what to do, that’s the realm they live in 24/7. They just live in the idea realm. Oh, I can just tell more people, more things what to do and we can just see it happen. If you actually have to be part of implementing that, you slow your horse. Do you know what? I had a good idea last week. I’m going to save the rest of my good ideas until next month.

(02:53:24) 现在有太多“来回讨论”，却太少“得分”，太少“扣篮”。我认为这是高管统治的一大陷阱。一旦创始人把自己提升到纯粹高管的位置——整天只告诉别人该做什么——他们就24/7活在“想法世界”里。他们会想：“哦，我可以告诉更多人做更多事，然后看着事情发生。”但如果你真的必须亲自参与执行，你就会放慢节奏。你会意识到：“你知道吗？我上周刚有个好点子。我要把剩下的好点子留到下个月再用。”

Why meetings are toxic

为什么会议有毒

Lex Fridman
(02:53:58) There is a temptation for the managers and for the people in the executive layer to do something, which that’s something usually means a meeting. And so that’s why you say-

Lex Fridman
(02:53:58) 管理层和高管层总有一种“要做点什么”的冲动，而这种“做点什么”通常就意味着开会。所以你才会说——

DHH
(02:54:11) Yes. Their job is telling other people what to do.

DHH
(02:54:11) 没错。他们的工作就是告诉别人该做什么。

Lex Fridman
(02:54:13) Yeah. And the meeting, so this is one of the big things you’re against is meeting-

Lex Fridman
(02:54:13) 是的。所以会议——这正是你强烈反对的事情之一，就是开会——

DHH
(02:54:17) Meetings are toxic. And this really I think ties into this with Jason and I. If I had to count out the total number of meetings we’ve had in 24 years of collaborations, where we in person sat in front of each other and discussed a topic, probably it’d be less than whatever three months at a fan company. We just haven’t done that that much. We haven’t worn it out. One of this funny metaphors that Trump came up with at one point was, a human has a limited number of steps in their life. That’s the longevity argument here. You can do so much activity and then you run out.

DHH
(02:54:17) 会议是有毒的。这一点其实和我和杰森的合作方式密切相关。如果要统计我们在24年合作中面对面坐下来讨论某个议题的会议总数，恐怕还比不上某些普通公司在三个月内的会议数量。我们真的很少那样做，所以我们从未把彼此“用坏”。特朗普曾提出过一个有趣的比喻：人一生中能走的步数是有限的——这就是长寿论的核心。你能做的活动是有限的，用完了就没了。

(02:54:53) There’s some kernel in that idea that can be applied to relationship. There’s some amount of exchange we can have. There’s some amount of time we can spend together, where you can wear it out. Jason and I were diligent about not wearing each other out, and I think that is absolutely key to the longevity of the relationship combined with that level of trust and then just combining with the level that we really like the work itself. We don’t just like the brainstorming the [inaudible 02:55:21] where we just come up with good ideas. Now we like to do the ideas, and we like to be part of that process directly ourselves. I like to program, he likes to do design. We could go off and do our little things for long stretches of time. In case you come together and go like, hey, let’s launch a great product.

(02:54:53) 这个观点中有一个核心思想可以应用到人际关系上：我们之间的交流是有限度的，共处的时间也是有限度的——用多了就会“磨损”。杰森和我一直很注意不去过度消耗彼此。我认为，正是这一点，加上我们之间的高度信任，以及我们对工作本身的热爱，共同构成了这段长久合作关系的关键。我们不只是喜欢头脑风暴（[此处音频不清 02:55:21]）——仅仅提出好点子；我们更喜欢去真正实现这些点子，并且亲自参与整个过程。我喜欢编程，他喜欢做设计。我们可以长时间各自专注自己的事情，等到时机成熟再聚在一起，说一句：“嘿，咱们发布一个了不起的产品吧。”
(02:55:35) This might sound like I’m asking you to do therapy, but I find myself to sometimes want or long for a meeting because I’m lonely. Remote work is just sitting by yourself, I don’t know, it can get really lonely for long stretches of time.
（02:55:35）这听起来可能像是在让你做心理治疗，但我发现自己有时会因为孤独而渴望或想要开个会。远程工作就是一个人独自坐着，我不知道，长时间下来真的会非常孤独。

DHH
(02:55:56) Let me give you a tip. Get a wife.
DHH
（02:55:56）我给你个建议：找个妻子。

Lex Fridman
(02:56:00) Yes. God, damn it.
Lex Fridman
（02:56:00）是啊，该死的。

DHH
(02:56:03) Get a couple kids.
DHH
（02:56:03）再生几个孩子。

Lex Fridman
(02:56:05) All right.
Lex Fridman
（02:56:05）好吧。

DHH
(02:56:05) Family really is the great antidote to loneliness, and I mean that as sincerely as I can possibly say it. I certainly had exactly that feeling you described early in my career when I was working remotely, and I was just like me living in an apartment, a total stereotype, where for the longest time when I first moved to Chicago, all I had on the floor was a mattress. And then I bought this big TV and I didn’t even mount it, and then I had a stack of DVDs. And I was basically, I was working a lot of time and then I would just go home and I’d do that, and it wasn’t great. It really wasn’t. I do think that humans need humans. And if you can’t get them at work, and I actually sort of kind of don’t want them at work, at least I don’t want them for 40 hours a week. That’s not what I prefer.
DHH
（02:56:05）家庭确实是孤独的最佳解药，我说这话是发自内心最真诚的。我职业生涯早期远程工作时，完全体会过你描述的那种感受——我一个人住在公寓里，完全是刻板印象中的样子：刚搬到芝加哥时很长一段时间里，地板上只有一张床垫；后来买了台大电视，甚至都没挂起来，还有一堆DVD。基本上就是长时间工作，回家后就做这些事，那感觉并不好，真的不好。我认为人确实需要他人。如果你在工作中得不到这种联结——而且我其实也不太希望在工作中得到，至少我不希望每周40小时都靠工作来满足这种需求——那不是我想要的。

(02:56:51) You need something else. You need other relationships in your life, and there is no greater depth of relationship if you can find someone that you actually just want to spend a lot of time with. That’s key to it and I think it’s key for both Jason and I that we’ve had families for quite a long time, and it grounds you to in a way where the sprint of a startup can get traded in for the marathon of an enduring company, and you get settled in a way. We talked briefly about sometimes I get fired up. I mean, a lot of times, maybe even most of the times I get fired up about topics, but I don’t get fired up in the same way now as I used to when I was 24. I’m still extremely passionate about ideas and trying to find the right things, but having a family, meeting my wife, building a life around that has just mellowed everything out in a completely cliche way, but I think it’s actually key.
（02:56:51）你需要别的东西。你需要生活中有其他关系，而如果你能找到一个你真心愿意花大量时间相处的人，那种关系的深度是无与伦比的。这一点至关重要。我认为对我和Jason来说，关键在于我们很早就有了家庭，这让我们的根基更加稳固——创业初期那种短跑冲刺可以转化为打造一家持久企业的马拉松，你会以一种更安定的方式生活。我们之前简单提到过，我有时会情绪激动。事实上，很多时候，甚至大多数时候，我对某些话题依然会热血沸腾，但现在的激动方式已不像24岁时那样了。我对想法依然充满热情，也仍在努力寻找正确的事物，但拥有家庭、遇见我的妻子、围绕她构建生活，这一切以一种极其老套却真实有效的方式让我变得平和，我认为这其实非常关键。

(02:57:51) I think if we could get more even younger people not to wait until they were in their god-damn 30s or early 40s to hitch up with someone, we’d be better off and we’d have more stable business relationships as well, because folks would get that nurturing human relation somewhere else. Now, when I say all of that, I also accept that there are plenty of great businesses that’s been built over the years that have not been built remote, that have been built by a gang of hooligans sitting in an office for immense hours at time.
（02:57:51）我认为，如果能让更多年轻人不要等到该死的三十多岁甚至四十出头才找个人安定下来，我们会过得更好，商业合作关系也会更稳定，因为人们能在别处获得滋养性的人际关系。当然，我也承认，过去几十年里确实有很多伟大的企业并非远程建立的，而是由一群“混混”在办公室里连续工作超长时间打造出来的。

(02:58:23) I mean, both John Carmack and Tim Sweeney talked about that in the ’90s with their careers that that was just basically work, sleep, hang out with the guys at the office, right? Totally fair. That never appealed to me. Both Jason and I saw eye to eye on the idea that 40 hours a week dedicated to work was enough that if we were going to go to distance for not just the five to seven years it takes to build a VC case up to an exit, but for potentially 10 years, 20 years or further, we needed to become whole humans, because only that whole human-ness was going to go to distance, which included building up friendships outside of work, having hobbies, finding a mate and having a family. And that entire existence, those legs of the stool that work is not the only thing in life is completely related to the fact that we’ve been around for 25 years. There’s way too much, especially in America of false trade-offs. Oh, you want to build a successful business? Well, you can either have money enjoyment or family or health, pick one.
（02:58:23）比如John Carmack和Tim Sweeney在90年代谈到他们的职业生涯时就说，基本就是工作、睡觉、和办公室里的哥们儿混在一起，对吧？这完全合理。但这种方式从来吸引不了我。我和Jason对此看法一致：每周投入40小时工作就足够了。如果我们打算坚持的不只是风投模式下常见的五到七年直至退出，而是可能十年、二十年甚至更久，我们就必须成为完整的人——只有这种完整性才能支撑长期发展，其中包括建立工作之外的友谊、培养爱好、找到伴侣并组建家庭。这种整体的生活方式——即工作并非生活的全部——正是我们公司能持续25年的原因所在。在美国尤其如此，太多虚假的取舍被强加于人：“你想打造成功企业？那你只能在金钱、快乐、家庭或健康中选一个。”

(02:59:40) What? Why do we have to give up all of this? Now, again, I’m not saying, and there are moments, prayers, life where you can sprint, but I am saying if that sprint turns into a decade, you’re going to pay for it. And you’re going to pay for it in ways I’ve seen time and again, seemed like a very bad trade, that even if it works. And by the way most of the time it does not. Most of the time startups go bust. Most of the times people spend five, seven years or something that does not pan out, and they don’t get the payout. And then they just sit with regret of like, what the fuck happened to my 20s? Early on, Jason and I basically made the pact that working together was not going to lead to that kind of regret, that we were going to allow ourselves and each other to build a whole life outside of work. And the fact that that worked is something I feel is almost like forbidden knowledge.
（02:59:40）什么？为什么我们必须放弃所有这些？当然，我不是说人生不能有冲刺阶段——确实存在某些时刻、某些阶段你可以全力冲刺——但我要说的是，如果这种冲刺变成十年之久，你终将为此付出代价。我反复目睹过这种代价，即使最终“成功”了，看起来也是极不划算的交易。况且，大多数时候根本不会成功。大多数初创公司都会倒闭。大多数人花了五到七年时间做一件事，结果却一无所获，拿不到任何回报，最后只能懊悔地想：“我的二十多岁到底怎么了？” 很早以前，Jason和我就达成了一项约定：我们的合作绝不能导致这种遗憾；我们要允许自己、也允许对方在工作之外构建完整的人生。而这一做法竟然奏效了——我觉得这几乎像是一种“禁忌知识”。

(03:00:38) Certainly in technology circles in US, it’s something that we’ve tried to champion for 20 years and we still get slacked for. Just two days ago, I had another Twitter beef with someone saying like, “Oh, well, okay, maybe it worked, but you didn’t turn into Atlassian, so you’re a failure. Basecamp isn’t Jira, so why are you even bothering?” And it’s such a fascinating winner-takes- all mentality that unless you dominate everyone else in all the ways, you’ve lost. When so much of life is far more open to multiple winners, where we can end up with a business that have made hundreds of millions of dollars over the years and we’ve kept much of that to do whatever we want and that that’s enough. That’s good. That’s great. That’s actually something worth aspiring to. Certainly, it should be a path for someone to consider choosing rather than the VC unicorn of bust mentality that dominates everything.
（03:00:38）尤其是在美国科技圈，我们为此倡导了20年，至今仍被人嘲讽。就在两天前，我又在推特上跟人吵了一架，对方说：“哦，行吧，也许你们成功了，但你们没变成Atlassian，所以你们就是失败者。Basecamp又不是Jira，你们瞎折腾什么？” 这种“赢家通吃”的心态实在令人着迷——除非你在所有方面都碾压别人，否则你就输了。但现实是，生活中本可以容纳多个赢家：我们可以拥有一家多年来赚了几亿美元的企业，并保留大部分利润去做自己想做的事——这就够了。这很好，这很棒，这其实是值得追求的目标。这绝对应该成为人们可以考虑选择的一条路径，而不是被那种“要么成为独角兽、要么彻底失败”的风投思维所主导。

Case against retirement

反对退休

Lex Fridman
(03:01:39) Yeah. I’d love to ask you about this exchange so you can explain to me the whole saga, but so just a link on that a little bit is, I think there’s a notion that success for tech founder is like work for a few years all out and then exit, sell your company for, I don’t know, hundreds of millions of dollars. That’s success. When it seems in reality, when you look at who the people like you, like really smart, creative humans, who they actually are and what happiness entails, it actually entails working your whole life a little bit. Because you actually love the programming, you love the building, you love the designer and you don’t want to exit, and that’s something you’ve talked about really, really eloquently about. So you actually want to create a life, where you’re always doing the building and doing it in a way that’s not completely taken over your life.
Lex Fridman
（03:01:39）是的。我很想请你谈谈这次争论，让你给我讲讲整个故事。简单说一下，我觉得现在有种观念认为，科技创始人的成功就是拼命干几年，然后退出，把公司卖个几亿美元——这才叫成功。但实际上，当你观察像你这样真正聪明、富有创造力的人，看看他们究竟是谁、幸福到底意味着什么，你会发现幸福其实意味着一辈子都适度地工作。因为你真心热爱编程、热爱创造、热爱设计，你根本不想退出——这一点你曾非常非常雄辩地阐述过。所以你真正想创造的是一种生活：始终在建造，但又不至于让工作完全吞噬你的生活。

DHH
(03:02:40) Mojito Island is a mirage. It always was. There is no retirement for ambitious people. There is no just sitting back on the beach and sipping a mojito for what, for two weeks before you go damn crazy and want to get back into the action. That’s exactly what happens to most people who have the capacity to build those kinds of exits. I’ve never seen, I shouldn’t say never. I’ve almost never seen anyone be able to pull that off, yet so many think that that’s why they’re doing it. That’s why they’re sacrificing everything because once I get to the finish line, I’m golden, I’ve won, I can retire, I can sit back, I can just relax. And you find out that that kind of relaxation is actually hell. It’s hell for creative people to squander their God-given creative juices and capacities. And I was really lucky to read the book Flow by Mihaly Csikszentmihalyi early on [inaudible 03:03:39].
DHH
（03:02:40）“莫吉托岛”是个海市蜃楼，一直都是。有抱负的人根本不存在退休这回事。你不可能只是坐在海滩上喝莫吉托鸡尾酒——顶多两周，你就会发疯似的想重新投入行动。这正是大多数有能力实现那种“退出”的人的真实遭遇。我从没见过——好吧，不该说“从没”，我几乎从未见过有人真能成功做到这一点，可还是有那么多人以为这就是他们奋斗的目标。他们之所以牺牲一切，是因为相信一旦冲过终点线，自己就功成名就了，就可以退休、放松、享受人生。但你很快会发现，那种放松其实是地狱。对富有创造力的人来说，浪费上天赋予的创造力和能力，就是一种折磨。我很幸运，很早就读到了米哈里·契克森米哈赖（Mihaly Csikszentmihalyi）的《心流》这本书[此处音频不清，03:03:39]。

Lex Fridman
(03:03:38) Nice, the pronunciations.
Lex Fridman
（03:03:38）不错啊，发音挺准的。

DHH
(03:03:40) Do you know what? I had to practice that with AI over the last few days because I knew I was going to cite him and I butchered his name several times. So AI taught me how to pronounce that at least somewhat correctly. But his main work over his career was essentially the concept of flow that came out of a search for understanding happiness. Why are some people happy? When are they happy? And what he learned was quite illuminating. He learned that people aren’t happy when they sit on Mojito Island. They’re not happy when they’re free of all obligations and responsibilities. No. They’re happy in these moments where they’re reaching and stretching their capacities just beyond what they can currently do. In those moments of flow, they can forget time and space. They can sit in front of the keyboard, program a hard problem, think 20 minutes have passed and suddenly it’s been three hours.
DHH
（03:03:40）你知道吗？过去几天我特意用AI练习了这个名字的发音，因为我知道会谈及他，而我之前好几次都念错了。所以AI教会了我至少勉强正确的发音。但他整个职业生涯的核心研究成果，其实就是“心流”这个概念——它源于对幸福本质的探索：为什么有些人会感到幸福？他们在什么时候感到幸福？他的发现极具启发性：人们坐在“莫吉托岛”上并不会幸福；当他们摆脱所有义务和责任时也不会幸福。不，真正的幸福出现在他们挑战自我、将能力刚好延伸到当前极限之外的那些时刻。在心流状态中，人们会忘记时间和空间——比如坐在键盘前攻克一个难题，以为只过了20分钟，抬头一看却发现已经过去了三个小时。
(03:04:36) They look back upon those moments with the greatest amount of joy, and that is what peak happiness is. If you take away the pursuit of those kinds of problems, if you eliminate all the problems from your plate, you’re going to get depressed. You’re not going to have a good time. Now, there are people who can do that, but they’re not the same kind of people who built these kinds of companies. So you have to accept the kind of individual you are. If you are on this path, don’t bullshit yourself. Don’t bullshit yourself into thinking, I’m just going to sacrifice everything, my health, my family, my hobbies, my friends, but in 10 years I’m going to make it all up, because in 10 years I can do it.
(03:04:36) 他们回望那些时刻时，感受到最大的快乐，而这正是巅峰幸福的体现。如果你剥夺了自己去追求这类问题的动力，如果你把所有问题都从你的生活中剔除，你就会陷入抑郁。你不会过得开心。当然，确实有人能做到彻底“无忧无虑”，但他们并不是那些创建这类公司的人。所以你必须接受自己是怎样的人。如果你走的是这条路，就别自欺欺人。不要骗自己说：“我现在牺牲一切——健康、家庭、爱好、朋友——但十年后我就能全部弥补回来，因为到那时我就能做到了。”

(03:05:15) It never works out like that. It doesn’t work out on both ends of it. It does not work out if you’re successful and you sell your company, because you’ll get bored out of your mind after two weeks on retirement. It doesn’t work out if the company is a failure and you regret the last 10 years spent for nothing. It doesn’t work out if it all works and you stay in the business because it never gets any easier. So you’re going to fail on all metrics if you just go, there’s only work and nothing else. And I didn’t want that. I wanted the happiness of flow. I understood that insight was true, but I wanted to do it in a way where I could sustain the journey for 40 or 50 years.
(03:05:15) 这种想法从来不会如愿以偿。无论结果如何，它都不会成功。即使你成功了、卖掉公司，也会在退休两周后感到极度无聊；如果公司失败了，你会懊悔过去十年白白浪费；即便一切顺利、你继续留在企业里，事情也永远不会变得更容易。因此，如果你只专注于工作而忽视其他一切，那么在所有衡量标准上你都会失败。我不想要那样的生活。我渴望的是心流状态中的幸福。我明白这个洞见是真实的，但我想找到一种方式，让我能持续这段旅程四十年甚至五十年。

Lex Fridman
(03:05:53) And there’s other interesting caveat that I’ve heard you say is that if you do exit and you sell your company, and you want to stay in, you want to do another company, that’s going to usually not be as fulfilling because really your first baby like…
Lex Fridman
(03:05:53) 我还听过你提到另一个有趣的提醒：如果你退出并卖掉了公司，却还想继续创业、再开一家新公司，通常不会那么有满足感，因为你的第一个公司就像你的第一个孩子一样……

DHH
(03:06:09) You can’t do it again or most people can’t do it again. A, because their second idea is not going to be as good as the first one. It is so rare to capture lightning in the bottle like we have, for example with Basecamp. I know this from experience because if you’re trying to build a lot of other businesses since, and some of them have been moderate successes, even good successes, none of them have been Basecamp. It’s really difficult to do that twice. But founders are arrogant pricks, including myself, and we like to think that, do you know what we succeeded in large part because we’re just awesome. We’re just so much better than everyone else. And in some ways that’s true some of the time, but you can also be really good at something that matters for a hot moment. That door is open, the door closes. Now you’re still good at the thing, but it doesn’t matter. No one cares.
DHH
(03:06:09) 你很难再复制一次，或者说大多数人根本做不到。首先，因为他们的第二个创意通常不如第一个好。像我们当初做 Basecamp 那样“抓住闪电”的机会极其罕见。我对此深有体会，因为我后来尝试创办了很多其他公司，其中有些取得了中等甚至不错的成功，但没有一个能比得上 Basecamp。要两次做到这种程度真的非常困难。但创始人（包括我自己）往往都是自负的混蛋，总以为：“你知道吗？我们之所以成功，主要是因为我们太牛了，比其他人都强得多。”有时候这在某种程度上确实成立，但你也可能只是恰好擅长某件在特定时刻很重要的事。那扇门打开了，但随后又关上了。现在你虽然仍然擅长那件事，但它已经不再重要了，没人关心。

(03:06:54) There’s that part of it. And then there’s the part of it that going back to experience things for the first time only happens the first time. You can’t do it again. I don’t know if I have it in me to go through the bullshit of the early days again. And I say bullshit in the sense of the most endearing sense. It’s all great to do it. I know too much. This is one of the reasons why whenever I’m asked the questions, if you could tell your younger self something that would really, what would you say to your younger self? I would fucking not say a thing. I would not rob my younger self of all the life experiences that I’ve been blessed with due to the ignorance of how the world works. Building up the wisdom about how the world works is a joy, and you got to build it one break at a time.
(03:06:54) 这是一方面。另一方面在于，第一次体验某些事情只能发生一次。你无法重来。我不知道自己是否还有精力再去经历创业初期那些“狗屁”阶段了——我说“狗屁”其实是带着最亲切的感情而言的，那段经历其实很棒。但我现在知道得太多了。这也是为什么每当有人问我：“如果你能告诉年轻时的自己一件事，你会说什么？”我的回答会是：我他妈什么都不会说。我不想剥夺年轻时那个我对世界一无所知所带来的宝贵人生体验。积累对世界运行方式的理解本身就是一种喜悦，而这份智慧只能靠一次次碰壁慢慢建立起来。

(03:07:40) If you just handed all the results, it’s like, oh, should we watch your movie? Here’s how it ends. I don’t want to watch the movie now. You spoiled it. I don’t want you to spoil my business experience. I don’t want to spoil any of my ignorance. The greatest blessing half the time when you’re starting something new is A, you don’t know how hard it’s going to be. B, you don’t know what you don’t know. The adventure is to pay off. The responsibility is to pay off. This is something Jordan Peterson has really taught me to articulate. This notion that responsibility is actually key to meaning.
(03:07:40) 如果你直接把所有答案都给我，那就像是：“我们要不要看你的电影？结局是这样的。”那我就不想看了——你剧透了。我不想让你剧透我的创业经历，也不想让任何人破坏我的无知状态。当你开始做一件新事情时，一半的幸运恰恰在于：第一，你不知道这件事会有多难；第二，你不知道自己有哪些不知道的东西。这场冒险终将有所回报，而承担责任本身也会带来回报。这是乔丹·彼得森真正教会我去清晰表达的一点：责任实际上是意义的关键所在。

(03:08:16) Man’s Search for Meaning, Viktor Frankl talks about this as well, that we can endure any hardship if there’s a reason why. Now, he talked about it in truly life altering concentration camp ways, but you can also apply at a smaller scale with less criticality of even just your daily life that all that hardship in building the original business that is responsibility you take upon yourself. The appeal, the reason you take that on you is in part because you don’t know fully what it entails. If you had known upfront, if I had known upfront how hard it would be, how much frustration there’d be along the way, if you just told me that in a narrative before I got started, I would’ve been like, eh, maybe I should just go get a job.
(03:08:16) 维克多·弗兰克尔在《活出生命的意义》中也谈到了这一点：只要有一个理由，我们就能忍受任何苦难。他是在集中营那种彻底改变人生的极端情境下讨论这个问题的，但这个道理也可以应用到日常生活的较小尺度上——比如你在创立最初那家公司时所承受的所有艰辛，本质上就是你主动承担的责任。你之所以愿意承担，部分原因恰恰是你并不完全清楚其中的代价。如果一开始我就知道这条路会有多艰难、途中会有多少挫败，如果你在我起步前就把这些写成一个故事告诉我，我可能会想：“嗯，也许我还是去找份工作算了。”

Hard work

努力

Lex Fridman
(03:09:00) You said so many smart things there. Just to pick one, it’s funny that sometimes the advice givers, the wisdom givers have gone through all the bullshit, and so there is a degree to which you want to make the mistake. So I think I would still give the advice of you want to have a stretch of your life, where you work too hard, including anything that fails. I don’t think you can learn the lessons why that’s a bad idea in any other way except by doing it. There is a degree, but of course you don’t…
Lex Fridman
(03:09:00) 你刚才说了太多睿智的话。只挑一点来说，有趣的是，那些给出建议和智慧的人，往往自己已经历过所有那些“狗屁”过程，所以某种程度上，你其实需要犯那些错误。因此我认为，我仍然会建议：你应该有一段人生时期，拼命工作，哪怕最终失败也没关系。除非亲身经历，否则你无法真正理解为什么“过度牺牲一切”是个糟糕的主意。当然，这有个度，但你显然不是……

DHH
(03:09:37) I think you should stretch. Should you have to stretch for a decade? I’m not so sure.
DHH
(03:09:37) 我认为你应该挑战自我。但是否非得持续整整十年？我不太确定。

Lex Fridman
(03:09:40) Yeah. The decade thing is 20s is a special time.
Lex Fridman
(03:09:40) 是啊，十年这个说法，二十多岁确实是个特殊的时期。

DHH
(03:09:43) It’s a lot to trade. You don’t get your 20s back, you don’t get your 30s back, you don’t get your 40s back. I would’ve regret it personally if I hadn’t done the other things I did in my 20s. If I hadn’t had the fun I had, if I hadn’t had the friends I had, if I hadn’t built up the hobbies that I did, if I hadn’t started driving race cars at an early enough age to actually get really good at it, if I had just gone all in on business because I would’ve got the same out in the end. This is something Derek Sivers really taught me, is he has this great essay about how when he went for a bike ride, he could go really hard all out and he could do the ride, I think, in whatever 19 minutes, or he could enjoy the ride, go 5% slower, do the ride in 21 minutes and realize there’s only two minutes apart.
DHH
(03:09:43) 这代价太大了。你的二十多岁回不来，三十多岁、四十多岁也都一去不返。如果我在二十多岁时没有去做那些其他事情——如果没有享受那些乐趣，没有结交那些朋友，没有培养那些爱好，没有在足够早的年纪开始开赛车以至于真正变得很厉害——而只是全身心扑在事业上，我最后得到的结果其实并不会更好。德里克·西弗斯（Derek Sivers）曾让我深刻领悟到这一点。他写过一篇精彩的文章，讲他骑自行车的经历：他可以拼尽全力，用比如19分钟完成骑行；也可以放松享受，慢5%，用21分钟完成——结果只差两分钟。

(03:10:32) Either I go all in all the time, there’s nothing else, I’m completely exhausted at the [inaudible 03:10:37] or I traveled the same distance and I arrived maybe two minutes later, but I got to enjoy the scenery, listen to the birds, smell the flowers. That journey is also valuable. Now, I say that while accepting and celebrating that if you want to be the best at one thing in the world, no, you have to sacrifice everything. You have to be obsessed with just that thing. There is no instant of someone who’s the best in the world at something who’s not completely obsessed. I didn’t need to be best at anything. This was a rare blessing of humility I had early on is like, do you know what? I am not that smart. I’m not that good. I’m not that talented. I can do interesting things by combining different aspects and elements that I know, but I’m not going to be the best at anything.
(03:10:32) 要么我始终全力以赴，不顾一切，最后精疲力竭；要么我走同样的路，只是晚到两分钟，却能欣赏风景、听鸟鸣、闻花香。这段旅程同样珍贵。当然，我也承认并尊重这样一个事实：如果你想成为世界上某个领域最顶尖的人，就必须牺牲一切，必须对此事极度痴迷。世上没有任何一个顶尖高手不是完全痴迷于其领域的。但我不需要成为任何领域的“最好”。我很早就拥有一种难得的谦逊之福：我意识到，“你知道吗？我其实没那么聪明，没那么优秀，也没那么有天赋。我能做的，只是把我知道的不同方面和元素结合起来，做一些有趣的事，但我永远不会成为某个领域的最强者。”

(03:11:27) And that released me from this singular obsession with just going, I’m going to be the best programmer in the world. I know I’m not. I fucking failed at it twice before I even got how conditional it’s worked. I’m not smart enough to be the best at anything. I’m not dedicated enough to do that. That’s a bit of a blessing. And I think as a society, we have to straddle both celebrating peak excellence, which we do all the time, and celebrating the peak intensity of mission it takes to become that. And then also going like, do you know what? We don’t all need to be Michael Jordan. There’s only going to be one of those.
(03:11:27) 这让我摆脱了那种单一执念——“我要成为全世界最好的程序员”。我知道自己做不到。在我真正理解编程成功的条件之前，我已经失败过两次了。我没有聪明到能在任何领域做到最好，也没有足够的专注力去实现那种极致。这反而是一种福气。我认为，作为社会，我们既要持续颂扬巅峰卓越（我们一直在这么做），也要颂扬达成这种卓越所需的极致使命感；同时也要认识到：我们并不都需要成为迈克尔·乔丹。那样的人，全世界只有一个。

Lex Fridman
(03:12:04) Well, we should say that there’s certain pursuits where a singular obsession is required. Basketball is one of them. By the way, probably racing. If you want to be the best at F-1 in the world-
Lex Fridman
(03:12:04) 当然，我们也得承认，有些追求确实需要单一的痴迷。篮球就是其中之一。顺便说一句，赛车可能也是。如果你想成为世界上最好的F1车手——

DHH
(03:12:17) If you want to be Senna, you got to be a maniac.
DHH
(03:12:17) 如果你想成为塞纳（Senna），你就得是个疯子。
(03:12:20) But I would argue that there’s most disciplines like programming allows if you want to be, quote, unquote, “the best,” whatever that means. I think that’s judged at the end of your life. And usually if you look at that path, it’s going to be a nonlinear one. You’re not going to look like the life of an Olympic athlete who’s singular focused. There’s going to be some acid there in the 20s or there’s going to be several detours, which should the true greats, there’s going to be detours, and sometimes they’re not going to be Steve Jobs’ asset type of situation. There’ll be just different companies you’ve worked for different careers or different efforts you allocated your life to, but it’s going to be nonlinear. It’s not going to be a singular focus.
（03:12:20）但我想说的是，在大多数领域——比如编程——如果你想成为所谓的“最好”（无论这到底意味着什么），你的人生路径通常是非线性的。我认为一个人是否“最好”，要到生命尽头才能评判。如果你回看那些真正杰出之人的道路，它绝不会像奥运选手那样只专注于一件事。你的二十多岁可能会经历一些“迷幻”阶段，或者走上几条弯路；真正的伟大人物往往都有过弯路，而且这些弯路并不总是像史蒂夫·乔布斯那种“资产型”的传奇转折。更常见的是，你可能在不同公司工作过，尝试过不同的职业，或把人生精力分配给了各种不同的追求——但这条路注定是非线性的，绝非单一聚焦。

DHH
DHH

(03:13:09) The way I think about this sometimes is I want a good bargain on learning. I can become in the top 5% of whatever I defined as good at something, much, much easier. Perhaps it’s 20 times easier, a hundred times easier to get into the top 5% than it is to get into the top 0.1%. That’s almost impossibly hard to get into that. But if I’m content just being at the top 5%, I could be at the top 5% on five things at once. I can get really good at writing. I can get decent at driving a race car. I can become pretty good at programming, I can run a company, I can have a family.
（03:13:09）我有时这样思考：我希望在学习上获得高性价比。我可以相对轻松地进入某项技能的前5%——也许比进入前0.1%容易20倍，甚至100倍。而要达到前0.1%，几乎难如登天。但如果我对停留在前5%就感到满足，那我就可以同时在五件事上都达到前5%：我可以写得非常好，赛车开得不错，编程相当熟练，能经营一家公司，还能拥有一个家庭。

(03:13:48) I can do a lot of things at the same time that gives me sort of that variety that almost was idealized. Karl Marx has this idea, oh, I’m going to fish in the morning and hammer in the evening and paint on the weekends, right? That there’s a sense for me at least, where his diagnosis of alienation was true, that just that tunnel vision, there’s just this one thing I’m just going to focus on that gives me a sense of alienation. I can’t stomach.
（03:13:48）我可以同时做很多事情，这种多样性几乎是一种理想状态。卡尔·马克思就曾提出这样的想法：早上钓鱼，晚上打铁，周末画画，对吧？至少对我来说，他对“异化”的诊断是准确的——那种只盯着一件事、极度专注的状态会让我产生强烈的疏离感，我实在无法忍受。

(03:14:15) When I’m really deep on programming. And sometimes I go deep for weeks, maybe even in a few cases months, I have to come up for air and I have to go do something else like, all right, that was programming for this year. I’ve done my part, and I’m going to go off riding or annoy people on the internet or drive some race cars to do something else, and then I can do the programming thing with full intensity again next year.
（03:14:15）当我深度投入编程时——有时会连续沉浸几周，甚至在少数情况下长达数月——我就必须“浮出水面换口气”，去做点别的事。比如：“好了，今年的编程任务完成了，我的份已经尽了。”然后我会去骑车、在网上惹人烦，或者开赛车，做些完全不同的事情。这样，到了明年，我才能再次全身心地投入编程。

Why we left the cloud

我们为何离开云服务

Lex Fridman
Lex Fridman

(03:14:38) Speaking of annoying people on the internet, you got to explain to me this drama. Okay, so what is this guy that said, “Imagine losing to Jira, but boasting they have a couple million dollars per year.” So this had to do with this almost now a meme decision to leave the cloud. DHH left the cloud. I think that’s literally a meme, but it’s also a fascinating decision. Can you talk through the full saga of DHH leaves the cloud, leaving AWS, saving money, and I guess the case this person is making now?
（03:14:38）说到在网上惹人烦，你得给我解释一下这场风波。有个家伙说：“想象一下输给Jira，却还吹嘘自己年入几百万美元。”这似乎和你那个如今几乎成了网络梗的决定有关——DHH离开了云服务。我觉得这真的已经变成一个meme（网络迷因）了，但同时也是个引人深思的决定。你能完整讲讲“DHH离开云服务”这段故事吗？包括离开AWS、节省开支，以及那个人现在提出的观点？

DHH
DHH

(03:15:14) Is that we wasted our time optimizing a business that could have been a hundred times bigger if we’d just gone for the moon.
（03:15:14）他的意思是，我们浪费了时间去优化一项本可以百倍壮大的业务，如果我们当初敢放手一搏的话。

Lex Fridman
Lex Fridman

(03:15:20) And for the moon includes?
（03:15:20）而“放手一搏”具体指什么？

DHH
DHH

(03:15:22) Venture Capital includes other things, not caring about cost.
（03:15:22）包括风险投资、以及其他不计较成本的做法。

Lex Fridman
Lex Fridman

(03:15:26) But also because AGI is around the corner, you should have been investing into AI, right? Is this just part of-
（03:15:26）但也是因为通用人工智能（AGI）即将到来，你们本该大力投资AI，对吧？这是不是也属于——

DHH
DHH

(03:15:32) Sort of [inaudible 03:15:33]. I think it’s a bit of a muddy argument, but if we just take it at its peak ideal, which I actually think is a reasonable point, is that you can get myopically focused on counting pennies when you should be focused on getting pounds that I’ve optimized our spend on infrastructure by getting out of the cloud, and that took some time and I could have taken that time and spend it on making more features that would attract more customers or spend even more time with AI or done other things. Opportunity cost is real. I’m not denying that. I’m pushing back on the idea that for a company of our size saving $2 million a year on our infrastructure bill, which is about somewhere between 1/2 to 2/3 goes directly to the bottom line, which means its return to Jason or I as owners and our employees part of our profit sharing plan is totally worth doing.
（03:15:32）有点儿……[听不清 03:15:33]。我觉得这个论点有些模糊，但如果取其最理想化的版本——我认为这确实有一定道理——那就是：你可能会短视地只顾着省小钱，而忽略了赚大钱的机会。我通过退出云服务优化了基础设施支出，但这花了一些时间；而这些时间本可以用来开发更多吸引客户的功能，或者投入更多精力到AI上，或者其他事情。机会成本是真实存在的，我并不否认这一点。但我反对的是这样一种观点：对我们这种规模的公司来说，每年在基础设施账单上节省200万美元（其中大约一半到三分之二直接转化为净利润，也就是作为股东的Jason和我，以及参与利润分享计划的员工们实际获得的回报）是不值得的。

(03:16:34) This idea that cost don’t matter is a very Silicon Valley way of thinking that I again understand at the scale of something maybe, but I also actually think it’s aesthetically unpleasing. I find an inefficient business as I find an inefficient program full of line noise to just be a splinter in my brain. I hate looking at an expense report and just seeing disproportionate waste. And when I was looking at our spend at 37signals a while back, a few years back, I saw bills that did not pass my smell test. I remembered how much we used to spend on infrastructure before the cloud, and I saw numbers I could not recognize in proportion to what we needed. The fact that computers had gotten so much faster over time, shouldn’t things be getting cheaper? Why are we spending more and more money servicing more customers? Yes, but with much faster computers. Moore’s law should be lowering the costs, and the opposite is happening. Why is that happening? And that started a journey of unwinding why the cloud isn’t as great as the deal as people like to think [inaudible 03:17:48].
（03:16:34）“成本无关紧要”这种想法是非常硅谷式的思维。我或许能理解它在某些规模下的合理性，但我也真心觉得这种做法在审美上令人不适。在我看来，一家低效的企业，就像一段充满冗余代码的低效程序一样，让我如鲠在喉。我讨厌看到费用报表上充斥着不成比例的浪费。几年前，当我回顾37signals的支出时，发现了一些完全通不过我“直觉检验”的账单。我记得在上云之前，我们在基础设施上的花费是多少；而现在看到的数字，与我们实际需求完全不成比例。计算机性能这些年大幅提升，按理说成本应该下降才对。为什么我们在服务更多客户的同时，支出却越来越高？没错，我们用的是更快的计算机，摩尔定律本应让成本不断降低，现实却恰恰相反。为什么会这样？正是这个问题，开启了我深入探究“云服务其实并不像人们吹嘘的那么划算”的旅程。[听不清 03:17:48]

AWS

AWS

Lex Fridman
Lex Fridman

(03:17:48) Yeah. Can we look at the specifics just for people who don’t know the story and then generalize to what it means about the role of the cloud in the tech business? So the specifics is you were using AWS S3.
（03:17:48）好的。我们能不能先讲讲具体细节，方便不了解这个故事的人理解，然后再推广到云服务在整个科技行业中的角色？具体来说，你们当时用的是AWS S3。

DHH
DHH

(03:18:03) We were using AWS for everything. Hey.com launches an entirely cloud app. It was completely on AWS for compute, for databases, for all of it. We were using all the systems as they’re best prescribed that we should. Our total cloud bill for Basecamp, our total spend with AWS was I think 3.2 million or 3.4 million at its peak. That’s kind of a lot of money, 3.4 million. I mean we have a ton of users and customers, but still that just struck me as unreasonable. And the reason why it was so unreasonable was because I had the pitch for the cloud ringing in my ears, hey, this is going to be faster. This is going to be easier. This is going to be cheaper. Why are you trying to produce your own power? Do you have your own power plant? Why would you do that? Leave the computers to the hyperscalers. They’re much better at it anyway.
（03:18:03）我们当时什么都用AWS。Hey.com上线时是一个完全基于云的应用——计算、数据库，所有组件都在AWS上。我们严格按照官方推荐的最佳实践使用了所有系统。Basecamp在AWS上的总支出峰值时达到了约320万到340万美元。这笔钱可不少——340万美元。虽然我们确实有大量用户和客户，但我仍然觉得这太不合理了。之所以觉得不合理，是因为我耳边一直回响着云服务商的宣传口号：“这会更快、更容易、更便宜。你自己发电干嘛？你有自己的发电厂吗？何必呢？把计算交给超大规模云厂商吧，他们在这方面强得多。”

(03:18:58) I actually thought that was a compelling pitch. I bought in on that pitch for several years and thought, do you know what? I’m done ever owning a server again. We are just going to rent our capacity, and Amazon is going to be able to offer us services much cheaper than we could buy them themselves because they’re going to have these economies of scale. And I was thinking Jeff’s word ringing, “My competitor’s margin is my opportunity.” That was something he used to drive amazon.com with, that if he could just make 2% when the other guy was trying to make 4%, he would end up with all the money and on volume he would still win.
（03:18:58）我当时真觉得这个说法很有说服力。我信了好几年，心想：“从今以后再也不用自己买服务器了。我们就租用算力，亚马逊凭借规模效应，肯定能以远低于我们自建的成本提供服务。”我还记得杰夫·贝索斯常说的一句话：“竞争对手的利润空间就是我的机会。”他当年就是靠这个理念推动amazon.com发展的——如果别人想赚4%的利润，而我只赚2%，最终我会靠规模赢下整个市场。

(03:19:34) So I thought that was the operating ethos for AWS. It turns out that’s not true at all. AWS, by the way, operates at almost 40% margin. So just in that, there’s a clue that competitors are not able to do the competitive thing we like about capitalism, which is to lower costs and so forth. So the cloud pitch in my optics, it’s fundamentally false. It did not get easier, first of all. I don’t know if you’ve used AWS recently. It is hella complicated. If you think Linux is hard, you’ve never tried to set up IAM rules or access parameters or whatever for AWS.
（03:19:34）所以我以为这也是AWS的运营哲学。结果发现完全不是这么回事。顺便说一句，AWS的利润率接近40%。光这一点就说明，竞争对手并没有做到资本主义所推崇的那种竞争行为——比如降低成本等等。因此，在我看来，云服务的宣传从根本上就是虚假的。首先，它并没有变得更简单。我不知道你最近有没有用过AWS，但它真的非常复杂。如果你觉得Linux很难，那你还没试过配置AWS的IAM规则、访问权限参数之类的东西。

Lex Fridman
Lex Fridman

(03:20:13) AWS was always difficult. It was always [inaudible 03:20:15].
（03:20:13）AWS一直都很难用，一直都是[听不清 03:20:15]。

DHH
DHH

(03:20:14) Well, I think it’s gotten even more difficult, but yes, now some of that is, it’s difficult because it’s very capable and you have a bunch of capacity on tap, and there are reasons I don’t think they’re good enough to justify how complicated the whole jing-a-ma-jing has become. But what’s certainly true is that it’s no longer easier, it’s not easier to use AWS than it is to run your own machines, which we learned when we pulled out the cloud and didn’t hire a single extra person. Even though we operate all our own hardware, the team stayed exactly the same. So you have this three-way pitch, right? It’s going to be easier, it’s going to be cheaper. Certainly wasn’t cheaper. We’ve just proved that by cutting our spend on infrastructure by 1/2 to 2/3 and it’s going to be faster. The last bit was true, but way too many people overestimated the value of that speed.
（03:20:14）我觉得它现在变得更难了。当然，部分原因是它功能强大，而且随时可用的资源很多。但我不认为这些理由足以证明整个系统变得如此复杂是合理的。但有一点是确定的：它不再更简单了。使用AWS并不比自己运维服务器更容易——这一点我们在退出云服务后得到了验证：我们没有为此多雇一个人，尽管现在所有硬件都由我们自己运维，团队规模却完全没变。所以当初云服务商承诺的三点——更简单、更便宜、更快——“更便宜”显然不成立，我们刚刚通过将基础设施支出削减一半到三分之二证明了这一点；“更快”或许是真的，但太多人严重高估了这种速度的价值。
(03:21:05) If you need a thousand computers online in the next 15 minutes, nothing beats the cloud. How would you even procure that? If we just need another 20 servers, it’s going to take a week or two to get boxes shipped on pallets, delivered to a data center and unwrapped and racked and all that stuff. But how often do we need to do that? And how often do we need to do that if buying those servers is way, way cheaper so we get vastly more compute for the same amount of money? Could we just buy more servers and not even care about the fact that we’re not hyper-optimized on the compute utility, that we don’t have to use things like automatic scaling to figure things out because we have to reduce costs? Yes, we can. So we went through this journey over a realization in early 2023, when I had finally had enough with our bills.

(03:21:05) 如果你需要在接下来的15分钟内让一千台计算机上线，那么没有什么能比得上云服务。但你真的会需要这样做吗？如果我们只需要再增加20台服务器，那可能要花一两周时间才能把设备装在托盘上运过来、送到数据中心、拆箱、上架，完成所有这些流程。但我们到底多久才真正需要这么做一次？而且，如果直接购买这些服务器要便宜得多——用同样的钱能获得多得多的计算能力——我们又有多频繁地需要临时扩容呢？我们能不能干脆多买些服务器，根本不用在意计算资源是否被“极致优化”，也不必为了降低成本而依赖自动扩缩容之类的技术？答案是：完全可以。于是，在2023年初，我终于对我们高昂的账单忍无可忍，由此开启了一段新的旅程。

(03:21:57) I wanted to get rid of them. I wanted to spend less money. I wanted to keep more of the money ourselves. And in just over six months, we moved seven major applications out of the cloud in terms of compute, caching, databases to works onto our own servers. A glorious, beautiful new fleet bought from the king of servers, Michael Dell, who really, by the way, is another icon of mine. I saw he just celebrated 41 years in business. 41 years, this man has been selling awesome servers that we’ve been using for our entire existence. But anyway, these pallets arrive in a couple of weeks and we rack them up and get everything going, and we were out, at least with the compute part. We then had a long multi-year commitment to S3, because the only way to get decent pricing in the cloud, by the way, is not to buy on a day-to-day basis, not to rent on a day-to-day basis, but to bind yourself up to multi-year contracts. With compute, it’s often a year. That was in our case.

(03:21:57) 我想摆脱这些账单，想花更少的钱，想把更多的钱留在自己口袋里。在短短六个月多一点的时间里，我们就把七个主要应用——包括计算、缓存和数据库——全部从云端迁移到了我们自己的服务器上。这是一支辉煌而漂亮的全新服务器舰队，购自“服务器之王”迈克尔·戴尔（Michael Dell），顺便说一句，他也是我心中的另一位偶像。我刚看到他庆祝了创业41周年。41年来，这个人一直在销售出色的服务器，而我们公司自始至终都在使用它们。无论如何，几周后这些托盘就到了，我们把服务器上架、配置好，一切顺利运行起来，至少在计算部分，我们彻底脱离了云。不过，我们在S3存储方面仍有一份长期的多年合约，因为顺便提一句，在云上获得合理价格的唯一方式，并不是按天计费或按天租用，而是把自己绑定到多年期合同上。对计算资源来说，通常是签一年——我们当时就是如此。
