Lex Fridman
(05:25:06) Yeah. Because at this point, I’m very object-oriented-brained.

Lex Fridman
（05:25:06）是啊。因为到现在为止，我的思维方式已经非常面向对象了。

DHH
(05:25:11) Yes.

DHH
（05:25:11）是的。

Lex Fridman
(05:25:11) Yeah.
（05:25:11）是的。

DHH
(05:25:12) And that’s my problem too. I don’t care as much about these low-level problems in computer science. I care about the high-level. I care about writing software. I care about the abstraction layer that really floats well with web applications and business logic.
DHH
（05:25:12）这也是我的问题。我对计算机科学中的那些底层问题并不那么在意。我关心的是高层级的东西，我关心的是编写软件，关心的是那种与 Web 应用和业务逻辑真正契合的抽象层。

(05:25:29) And I’ve come to accept that about myself, even though, as we talked about, when I was a kid, I really wanted to become a games programmer. And then I saw what it took to write a collision-detection engine, and I go like, “Yeah, that’s not me at all.” I’m never going to be into vector matrix manipulation or any of that stuff. It’s way too much math. And I’m more of a writing person than of a math person.
（05:25:29）我已经接受了自己的这一点，尽管正如我们之前谈到的，小时候我真的很想成为一名游戏程序员。但后来我看到要写一个碰撞检测引擎需要做些什么，我就想：“嗯，那完全不是我。” 我永远不会对向量矩阵运算或类似的东西感兴趣——数学成分太多了。我更像是个文字型的人，而不是数学型的人。

Lex Fridman
(05:25:54) I mean, just in the way you were speaking today, you have a poetic, literary approach to programming.
Lex Fridman
（05:25:54）我的意思是，就你今天说话的方式来看，你对编程有一种诗意的、文学性的方法。

DHH
(05:26:03) Yes.
DHH
（05:26:03）是的。

Lex Fridman
(05:26:04) Yeah. It’s interesting.
Lex Fridman
（05:26:04）是啊，这很有意思。

DHH
(05:26:04) That’s actually exactly right. So I did actually a keynote at RailsConf 10 years ago, where I called myself a software writer. I mean, I’m not the first person to say that. “Software writer” has been in the vernacular for a long time.
DHH
（05:26:04）这其实说得非常准确。十年前我在 RailsConf 上做过一次主题演讲，当时我称自己为“软件写作者”。当然，我不是第一个这么说的人，“软件写作者”这个说法早就存在于日常用语中了。

(05:26:16) But the modern identity that most programmers adopt when they’re trying to be serious is software engineer, and I reject that label. I’m not an engineer. Occasionally, I dabble in some engineering, but the vast majority of the time, I’m a software writer. I write software for human consumption and for my own delight.
（05：26：16）但如今大多数程序员在试图显得严肃时采用的身份是“软件工程师”，而我拒绝这个标签。我不是工程师。偶尔我会涉足一些工程工作，但绝大多数时候，我是个软件写作者。我写的软件是为了给人使用，也为了自己的愉悦。

(05:26:40) I can get away with that because I’m working in a high-level language like Ruby, working on collaboration software and to-do lists and all the other stuff. Again, if I was trying to apply my talent to writing 3D game engines, no, that’s not the right mindset. That’s not the right identity.
（05：26：40）我能这么做，是因为我使用的是像 Ruby 这样的高级语言，开发的是协作软件、待办事项列表之类的东西。再说一遍，如果我要把自己的才能用在编写 3D 游戏引擎上，那就不对了——那不是适合我的思维模式，也不是适合我的身份。

(05:26:58) But I find that the software engineering identity flattens things a little bit. I’d like to think that we have software writers and software mathematicians, for example, and then those are actually richer ways of describing the abstraction level that you’re working at than “engineer.”
（05：26：58）但我发现“软件工程师”这个身份有点把事情扁平化了。我更愿意认为，比如我们有“软件写作者”和“软件数学家”，这些其实是比“工程师”更能丰富描述你所处抽象层级的方式。

Lex Fridman
(05:27:16) Yeah. And I think if AI becomes more and more successful, I think we’ll need the software writer skill more and more because it feels like that’s the realm of which… Because it’s not writer. You’re going to have to do the software, you’re going to have to be a computer person, but there’s a more… I don’t know. I just don’t want to romanticize it, but it’s more poetic, it’s more literary. It more feels like writing a good blog post than-
Lex Fridman
（05：27：16）是啊。而且我觉得，如果 AI 越来越成功，我们会越来越需要“软件写作者”这种技能，因为感觉那才是……它不仅仅是写作。你仍然得做软件，得是个懂计算机的人，但其中还有更多……我不知道该怎么说。我不想把它浪漫化，但它更具诗意，更具文学性，更像是在写一篇好博客文章，而不是——

DHH
(05:27:48) I actually wish that AI had a bit higher standards for writing. I find the fact that it accepts my slobby, incomplete sentences a little offensive. I wish there was a strict mode for AI where it would snap my fingers if I was just feeding it keywords and like, “Speak proper. Do pronunciation, do punctuation.” Because I love that. I love crafting a just-right sentence that hasn’t been boiled down, that has no meat on it, has no character in it. It’s succinct, it’s not overly flowery. It’s just right.
DHH
（05：27：48）我其实希望 AI 对写作能有更高的标准。我发现它居然接受我那些潦草、不完整的句子，这让我有点反感。我希望 AI 有个“严格模式”，当我只喂给它关键词时，它会打个响指说：“好好说话！注意发音，注意标点。” 因为我很喜欢这样。我喜欢精心打磨出恰到好处的句子——那种没有被简化掉、没有多余赘肉、也没有空洞字符的句子。它简洁，不过分华丽，刚刚好。

(05:28:26) That writing phase to me is just addictive. And I find that when programming is the best, it’s almost equivalent exactly to that. You also have to solve a problem. You’re not just communicating a solution. You have to actually figure out what are you trying to say. But even writing has that.
（05：28：26）对我来说，那个写作阶段简直令人上瘾。我发现，当编程处于最佳状态时，几乎就等同于那种体验。你也必须解决一个问题，不只是传达一个解决方案；你得真正弄清楚自己到底想表达什么。但写作本身也有这样的过程。

(05:28:45) Half the time when I start writing a blog post, I don’t know exactly which arguments I’m going to use; they develop as part of the writing process. And that’s how writing software happens too. You know roughly the kind of problem you’re trying to solve. You don’t know exactly how you’re going to solve it. And as you start typing, the solution emerges.
（05：28：45）我写博客文章时，有一半时间一开始并不清楚自己会用哪些论点；它们是在写作过程中逐渐形成的。写软件也是如此。你大致知道要解决哪类问题，但并不确切知道该如何解决。当你开始敲代码时，解决方案才会浮现出来。

Lex Fridman
(05:29:05) And actually, as far as I understand, you and Jason are working on a new book. It’s in the early days of that kind of topic. I think he said… he tweeted that it’s going to be titled something like, “We don’t know what we’re doing upfront” or something like that. That kind of topic. And you figure it out along the way.
Lex Fridman
（05：29：05）实际上，据我所知，你和 Jason 正在合著一本新书。这个主题还处于早期阶段。我记得他说过……他在推特上提到，书名大概是《我们一开始根本不知道自己在做什么》之类的。就是这类主题——你们是在过程中逐步弄明白的。

DHH
(05:29:22) That’s a big part of it; trying to give more people the permission to trust their own instincts and their own gut and realizing that developing that supercomputer in your stomach is actually the work of a career and that you should not discard those feelings in preference to over… or not even complicated; to analytics, to intellectualism.
DHH
（05：29：22）这确实是其中很重要的一部分：试着让更多人获得信任自己直觉和本能的许可，并意识到培养你肚子里那台“超级计算机”其实是一辈子的修行。你不该为了过度……甚至都不算复杂的东西，比如数据分析或理智主义，而抛弃那些感受。

(05:29:50) Very often when we look at the big decisions we’ve had to make, they’ve come from the gut, where you cannot fully articulate why do I think this is the right thing. Well, because I’ve been in this business for 20 years and I’ve seen a bunch of things and I’ve talked to a bunch of people, and that is percolating into this being the right answer.
（05：29：50）很多时候，当我们回顾自己做过的重大决定，它们其实都来自直觉——你无法完全说清楚为什么觉得这是对的。原因在于，我在这个行业已经干了 20 年，见过很多事情，也和很多人聊过，所有这些经验正在酝酿出这个正确的答案。

(05:30:08) A lot of people are very skeptical about that in business or unable to trust it because it feels like they can’t rationalize. Why are we doing something? Well, because I feel like it, damn it. That’s a great privilege of being a bootstrapped, independent founder who don’t owe their business to someone else and doesn’t have to produce a return because I feel like a lot of the bullshit really creeps in when you’re trying to rationalize to other people why you do the things you do and why you take the decisions that you do.
（05：30：08）很多人在商业中对此持怀疑态度，或者无法信任这种直觉，因为他们觉得这无法理性解释。“我们为什么要做这件事？”“因为我就是这么觉得，该死的！” 这正是自筹资金、独立创始人的巨大特权——你的企业不欠别人，也不必为了回报率而妥协。我觉得，当你试图向别人解释自己为何做某事、为何做某个决定时，大量胡扯就会悄悄混进来。

(05:30:34) If you don’t have anyone to answer to, you are free to follow your gut, and that’s hell of an enjoyable way to work, and it’s also and very often the correct way to work. Your gut knows a lot. You can’t articulate it, but it’s spot-on more times than not.
（05：30：34）如果你不需要向任何人交代，你就可以自由地跟随自己的直觉。这是一种极其愉快的工作方式，而且往往也是正确的方式。你的直觉知道很多东西——虽然你说不清楚，但它多数时候都是对的。

Lex Fridman
(05:30:54) Yeah. Having to make a plan can be a paralyzing thing. I suppose there’s different kinds of brains. And first of all, I can’t wait to read that book if it materializes.
Lex Fridman
（05：30：54）是啊。制定计划有时反而会让人瘫痪。我想，人的大脑类型各不相同。首先，如果这本书真的出版了，我迫不及待想读。

(05:31:06) I often feel like in the more interesting things I do in my life, I really don’t know what I’m doing upfront. And I think there’s a lot of people around me that care for me that really want me to know what I’m doing. They’re like, “What’s the plan? Why are you doing this crazy thing?”
（05：31：06）我常常觉得，在我生活中做的那些更有趣的事情里，我一开始根本不知道自己在干什么。而我身边有很多关心我的人，他们真心希望我知道自己在做什么。他们会问：“你的计划是什么？你为什么要干这种疯狂的事？”

(05:31:24) And if I had to wait until I have a plan, I’m not going to do it. They have different brains on this kind of stuff. Some people really are planners and it maybe energizes them, but I think most creative pursuits, most really interesting, most novel pursuits are like, you kind of have to just take the leap and then just figure out as you go.
（05：31：24）如果非要等到我有了计划才行动，那我根本就不会去做。他们在这种事情上的思维方式不同。有些人确实是计划型人格，计划或许能让他们充满干劲。但我认为，大多数创造性追求、大多数真正有趣或新颖的探索，都是你必须先跳下去，然后边走边摸索。

DHH
(05:31:45) My favorite essay in Rework is the last one, and it’s entitled, “Inspiration is perishable.” And I think that captures a lot of it, that if you take the time to do a detailed plan, you may very well have lost the inspiration by the time you’re done.
DHH
（05：31：45）《重来》（Rework）里我最喜欢的一篇文章是最后一篇，标题叫《灵感易逝》。我觉得这句话道出了很多真相：如果你花时间去做一份详尽的计划，很可能等你做完时，灵感早已消散。

(05:32:02) If you follow the inspiration in that moment and trust your gut, trust your own competence that you will figure it out, you’re going to get so much more back. You’re going to go on the adventure you otherwise wouldn’t have, whether that’s just the business decisions or life decision. You have to seize that inspiration.
（05：32：02）如果你在那一刻追随灵感，相信自己的直觉，相信自己有能力搞定一切，你会收获多得多。你会踏上原本不会有的冒险旅程，无论是商业决策还是人生选择。你必须抓住那份灵感。

(05:32:21) There’s a great set of children’s books written by this Japanese author about chasing an idea and trying to get a hold of it, and it’s beautifully illustrated as an idea is something that’s floating around, as something you have to catch and latch onto, that I really feel captures this notion that inspiration is perishable; it’ll disappear. If you just put it back on the shelf and say, “Well, I got to be diligent about this, I got to line up a plan,” you may run out, and then there’s no steam to keep going.
（05：32：21）有一位日本作家写了一套很棒的儿童绘本，讲的是追逐一个想法并试图抓住它的故事。书中把“想法”描绘成漂浮在空中的东西，是你必须去捕捉并牢牢抓住的。我特别认同这种观点：灵感是易逝的，它会消失。如果你只是把它放回架子上，说：“好吧，我得认真一点，得先列个计划”，那你可能会耗尽动力，再也没力气继续前进了。

Open source

开源

Lex Fridman
(05:32:54) I have to ask you about open source. What does it take to run a successful open source project? You’ve spoken about that it’s a misconception that open source is democratic. It’s actually meritocratic. That’s a beautiful way to put it. So there often is a benevolent dictator at the top often. So can you just speak to that, having run successful open source projects yourself and being a benevolent dictator yourself?

Lex Fridman
（05：32：54）我得问问你关于开源的问题。要运营一个成功的开源项目需要什么？你曾说过，人们误以为开源是民主的，其实它是精英治理的（meritocratic）。这种说法非常精妙。通常，项目顶端往往有一位“仁慈的独裁者”。你自己就成功运营过开源项目，也是这样一位“仁慈的独裁者”，能否就此谈谈？

DHH
(05:33:26) Which is going to be a bit of a biased piece of evidence here, but-
DHH
（05：33：26）这可能会有点偏颇，但——

Lex Fridman
(05:33:31) Why monarchy is best.
Lex Fridman
（05：33：31）为什么君主制才是最好的。
(05:33:33) It’s great. We should definitely have dictators and they should control everything, especially when the dictator is me. Now, well, I think I learned very early on that a quick way to burn out in open source is to treat it as a business, as though your users are customers, as though they have claims of legitimacy on your time and your attention and your direction.
（05:33:33）这很棒。我们确实应该有独裁者，而且他们应该掌控一切，尤其是当那个独裁者是我自己的时候。不过，我很早就意识到，在开源领域迅速耗尽心力的一个捷径，就是把它当成一门生意——仿佛你的用户就是顾客，仿佛他们有权对你的精力、注意力和方向提出正当要求。

(05:33:56) Because I faced this almost immediately with Ruby on Rails. As soon as it was released, there were a million people who had all sorts of opinions about where I ought to take it. And not just opinions, but actually demands. “Unless you implement an Oracle database adapter, this is always going to be a toy.” It was actually more or less that exact demand that prompted me to have a slide at one of the early Rails conferences that just said, “Fuck you.”
（05:33:56）因为我在 Ruby on Rails 上几乎立刻就遇到了这种情况。它刚一发布，就有成千上万的人对我该如何发展它发表各种意见——还不只是意见，而是实实在在的要求：“除非你实现一个 Oracle 数据库适配器，否则它永远只是个玩具。”差不多就是这种要求，促使我在早期一场 Rails 大会上放了一张幻灯片，上面只写了三个字：“去你妈的。”

Lex Fridman
(05:34:25) Yeah, I saw that.
莱克斯·弗里德曼
（05:34:25）是的，我看到过那张幻灯片。

DHH
(05:34:27) I’m not going to do what you tell me to. I’m here as a bringer of gift. I am sharing code that I wrote on my own time, on my own volition. And you don’t have to say thank you. I mean, it’d be nice if you did. You can take the code and do whatever you want with it, you can contribute back if you want, but you can’t tell me what to do or where to go or how to act.
DHH
（05:34:27）我才不会照你说的去做。我是作为一个礼物的给予者来到这里的。我分享的是我自己在空闲时间、出于自愿编写的代码。你不必说谢谢——当然，如果你说了我会很高兴。你可以拿走这些代码，随意使用，也可以选择回馈贡献，但你不能命令我该做什么、往哪走、如何行事。

(05:34:51) I’m not a vendor. This is a fundamental misconception that users of open source occasionally step into because they’re used to buying software from companies who really care about their business. I care about people using my software, I think it’s great, but we don’t have a transactional relationship. I don’t get something back when you tell me what to do, except grief, and I don’t want it, so you can keep it.
（05:34:51）我不是供应商。这是开源用户偶尔会陷入的一个根本性误解——因为他们习惯了从那些真正关心自己业务的公司购买软件。我当然在乎有人使用我的软件，我觉得这很棒，但我们之间并没有交易关系。当你告诉我该做什么时，我得不到任何回报，除了烦恼，而我不想要它，所以你可以留着。

(05:35:18) So my open source philosophy from the start has been I got to do this primarily for me. I love when other people find use in my open source. It’s not my primary motivation. I’m not primarily doing it for other people. I’m primarily doing it for me and my own objectives.
（05:35:18）因此，我从一开始就秉持这样的开源哲学：我做这件事首先是为了我自己。我很高兴别人能从我的开源项目中获益，但这并非我的主要动机。我并不是主要为了他人而做，而是为了我自己和我自己的目标。

(05:35:35) Because as Adam Smith said, it’s not for the benevolence of the butcher that we expect our daily meat. It’s for his self-interest. And I actually find that to be a beautiful thought that our commons increase in value when we all pursue our self-interest, certainly in the realm of open source.
（05:35:35）正如亚当·斯密所说，我们指望每天吃到肉，并不是因为屠夫仁慈，而是因为他追求自身利益。我认为这是一种美妙的想法：当我们各自追求自身利益时，公共领域的价值反而会提升——在开源领域尤其如此。

(05:35:57) This is also why I reject this notion that open source is in some sort of crisis, that there’s a funding crisis, that we have to spend more. No, we don’t. Open source has never been doing better. Open source has never controlled more domains in software than it has right now. There is no crisis.
（05:35:57）这也是为什么我反对那种认为开源正处于某种危机之中的说法——比如所谓“资金危机”，或者我们必须投入更多。不，我们不需要。开源从未像今天这样繁荣，也从未在软件领域掌控过比现在更多的领域。根本不存在什么危机。

(05:36:14) There’s a misconception from some people making open source and from a lot of people using open source that open source is primarily like commercial software; something you buy and something where you can then make demands as a customer and that the customer is always right. The customer is not always right, not even in business, but certainly not in open source.
（05:36:14）一些开源开发者和许多使用者都存在一种误解，认为开源本质上就像商业软件——是你买来的东西，因此你可以作为顾客提出要求，而且“顾客永远是对的”。但事实是，顾客并不总是对的，即使在商业领域也是如此，更不用说在开源领域了。

(05:36:35) In open source, the customer as it is, is a receiver of gifts. We are having a gift exchange. I show up and give you my code. If you like it, you can use it. And if you have some code that fits in with where I’m going with this, I would love to get those gifts back. And we can keep trading like that.
（05:36:35）在开源中，所谓的“顾客”其实只是礼物的接收者。我们进行的是一种礼物交换。我出现并把我的代码送给你；如果你喜欢，就可以使用它。如果你有一些代码恰好契合我项目的发展方向，我也很乐意收到你回赠的礼物。我们可以一直这样互相交换下去。

(05:36:54) I give you more gifts. You give me some of your gifts. Together, we pool all the gifts such that someone showing up brand new just get a mountain of gifts. This is the magic thing of open source is it increases the total sum value of what’s in the commons when we all pursue our own self-interest.
（05:36:54）我继续给你更多礼物，你也给我一些你的礼物。我们一起把这些礼物汇聚起来，让每一个新加入的人都能直接获得一座礼物之山。这就是开源的魔力所在：当我们各自追求自身利益时，公共池塘里的总价值反而不断增长。

(05:37:10) So I’m building things for Rails that I need. And you know what? You want me to do that. You do not want me to build things that I don’t need on behalf of other people because I’ll do a crap job. I build much better software when I can evaluate the quality of that software by my own use.
（05:37:10）所以我为 Rails 构建的是我自己需要的东西。你知道吗？这正是你希望我做的。你并不希望我为了别人而去构建我自己不需要的功能，因为那样我只会做出糟糕的东西。只有当我能通过自己的使用来评估软件质量时，我才能做出更好的软件。

(05:37:28) I need this feature. I’m going to build a good version of that feature, and I’m going to build just enough just for me. So I’m not going to bloat it. I’m not trying to attract the customer here. I’m not trying to see some angle. I’m just building what I need. And if you go into open source with that mentality that you’re building for you and everything else is a bonus, I think you have all the ingredients to go the distance.
（05:37:28）我需要这个功能，就会做出一个高质量的版本，而且只做够我用的部分。所以我不会让它变得臃肿。我在这里不是为了吸引“顾客”，也不是为了算计什么策略。我只是在构建我需要的东西。如果你带着这种心态进入开源——即你为自己而构建，其他一切都是额外收获——那么我认为你就具备了长期坚持下去的所有要素。

(05:37:53) I think the people who burn out in open source is when they go in thinking, “I’m making all these gifts. I don’t really need them myself, but I’m hoping someone else does and maybe they’ll also give me some money.” That’s a losing proposition. It never basically works.
（05:37:53）我认为那些在开源中耗尽心力的人，往往是抱着这样的想法开始的：“我在制作所有这些礼物。我自己其实并不需要它们，但我希望别人需要，也许他们还会给我一些钱。”这是一种注定失败的策略，基本上从来行不通。

(05:38:08) If you want money for your software, you should just sell it. We have a perfectly fine model of commercial software that people can make that kind and then they can sell it. But I find a lot of confusion, let’s just call it that politely, in open source contributors who want to have their cake and eat it too.
（05:38:08）如果你希望靠软件赚钱，那就直接卖它好了。我们已经有非常成熟的商业软件模式：人们可以开发这类软件，然后出售它。但我发现很多开源贡献者存在大量困惑——我们就礼貌地称之为“困惑”吧——他们既想保留蛋糕，又想吃掉它。

(05:38:26) They like the mode of working with open source, they maybe even like the status that comes from open source, but they also would like to earn a living for making that open source. And therefore, they occasionally end up with the kind of grievances that someone who feels underappreciated at work will develop when others aren’t doing enough to recognize their great gifts.
（05:38:26）他们喜欢开源的工作方式，甚至可能享受开源带来的声望，但同时又希望能靠做开源谋生。因此，他们偶尔会产生类似职场中感到不被赏识的人那样的怨气——当别人没有充分认可他们所付出的巨大“礼物”时，这种情绪就会浮现。

Lex Fridman
(05:38:47) And then they might walk away. I wish I had more insight into their mind state of the individual people that are running these projects, if they’re feeling sad or they need more money. It’s just such a dark box.
莱克斯·弗里德曼
（05:38:47）然后他们可能会直接退出。我真希望能更了解这些项目维护者内心的真实状态——他们是否感到悲伤，或者是否需要更多金钱支持。这简直就是一个黑箱。

DHH
(05:39:04) It can be.
DHH
（05:39:04）确实如此。

Lex Fridman
(05:39:05) I mean, of course, there’s some communication, but I just sadly see too often they just walk away.
莱克斯·弗里德曼
（05:39:05）我的意思是，当然会有一些沟通，但我悲哀地发现，太多人最终只是默默离开。

DHH
(05:39:11) Right. And I think that’s actually part of the beauty of open source.
DHH
（05:39:11）没错。而且我认为这恰恰是开源之美的一部分。

Lex Fridman
(05:39:16) Is walking away.
莱克斯·弗里德曼
（05:39:16）就是能够随时离开。

DHH
(05:39:16) You are not obligated to do this code forever. You’re obligated to do this for as long as you want to do it. That’s basically your own obligation.
DHH
（05:39:16）你并没有义务永远维护这段代码。你唯一的义务，就是只要你还想做，就继续做下去。这本质上是你对自己的承诺。

Lex Fridman
(05:39:26) Okay, so you might criticize this and push back. You did write a blog post on forever, ” Until the end of the internet” with [inaudible 05:39:32]. There is a beautiful aspect, and you found a good balance there. But I don’t know, you’re bringing so much joy to people with this thing you created. It’s not an obligation, but there’s a real beauty to taking care of this thing you’ve created.
莱克斯·弗里德曼
（05:39:26）好吧，你可能会批评或反驳这一点。你确实写过一篇关于“永远”的博客文章，标题是《直到互联网终结》（[此处音频不清 05:39:32]）。其中有一种美好的成分，而且你找到了很好的平衡。但我不知道——你创造的东西给无数人带来了快乐。虽然这不是义务，但悉心照料自己创造之物，本身就有真实的美。

DHH
(05:39:27) There is.
DHH
（05:39:27）确实如此。

Lex Fridman
(05:39:49) And not forgetting… I think what the open source creator is not seeing enough, how many lives you’re making better. There’s certain pieces of software that I just-
莱克斯·弗里德曼
（05:39:49）而且不要忘记……我认为很多开源创作者并没有充分意识到，他们的工作究竟改善了多少人的生活。有些软件，我就是——

Lex Fridman
(05:40:00) … lives you’re making better. There’s certain pieces of software that I just quietly use a lot and they bring my life joy and I wish I could communicate that well. There’s ways to donate, but it’s inefficient. It’s usually hard to donate.
莱克斯·弗里德曼
（05:40:00）……让多少人的生活变得更好。有些软件，我默默地频繁使用，它们给我的生活带来快乐，而我却很难很好地表达这份感激。虽然有捐赠渠道，但效率很低，通常很难成功捐出去。

DHH
(05:40:16) It is. There’s some ways for some people that made it easier. GitHub donations is one way of doing it. I donate to a few people even though I don’t love the paradigm. I also accept that we can have multiple paradigms. I accept that I can do open source for one set of motivations and other people can do open source for other motivations. We don’t all have to do it the same way, but I do want to counter the misconception that open source is somehow in a crisis unless we all start paying for open source. That model already exists. It’s commercial software. It works very well and plenty of great companies have been built off the back of it and the expectations are very clear. I pay you this amount and I get this software.
DHH
（05:40:16）确实如此。有些人已经找到了更简便的方式，比如 GitHub 的捐赠功能就是其中之一。我自己也会向一些人捐款，尽管我并不完全认同这种模式。我也接受我们可以拥有多种不同的模式。我接受我可以出于一套动机做开源，而其他人可以出于另一套动机做开源。我们不必都用同一种方式。但我确实想反驳那种误解——认为除非我们都开始为开源付费，否则开源就处于某种危机之中。这种模式早已存在，那就是商业软件。它运作得非常好，许多伟大的公司都是在此基础上建立起来的，而且各方的期望非常明确：我付你这笔钱，你给我这个软件。
(05:40:55) Open source, once you start mixing money into, it gets real muddy real fast, and a lot of it’s just from those misaligned expectations that if you feel like you’re starving artists as an open source developer and you are owed X amount of money because your software is popular, you’re delusional and you need to knock that off. Just get back on track where you realize that you’re putting gifts into the world and if you get something back in terms of monetary compensation, okay, that’s a bonus. But if you need that money back in terms of monetary compensation, just charge for software or go work for a software company that will employ you to do open source. There’s tons of that. That is probably actually the primary mode that open source software is being developed in the world today. Commercial companies making open source that they need themselves and then contributing it back.

(05:40:55) 开源一旦掺杂了金钱，事情很快就会变得非常混乱，而其中很多问题都源于期望错位——如果你作为一名开源开发者觉得自己像个“饥饿的艺术家”，并认为因为自己的软件很受欢迎，就理应获得某笔金额的报酬，那你就是自欺欺人，应该立刻停止这种想法。你应该回到正轨：意识到自己是在向世界馈赠礼物；如果因此获得了金钱回报，那很好，算是额外奖励。但如果你确实需要靠这笔钱维生，那就直接对软件收费，或者去一家愿意雇佣你从事开源工作的软件公司上班。这样的机会多的是。事实上，如今全球开源软件的主要开发模式很可能正是如此：商业公司开发自己所需的开源软件，然后再回馈给社区。

WordPress drama

WordPress 争议

Lex Fridman
(05:41:46) So I’m glad you drew some hard lines. Here is a good moment to bring up what I think is maybe one of the greatest open source projects ever, WordPress. And you spoke up in October 24 about some of the stuff that’s been going on with WordPress’s founder, Matt Mullenweg, in a blog post, “Open source royalty and mad kings,” is a really good blog post on just the idea of Benevolent Dictators For Life, this model for open source projects. And then the basic implication was that Matt, as the BDFL of WordPress has lost his way a bit with this battle with WP Engine. So I should also say that I really love WordPress. It brings me joy. I think it’s a beacon of what open source could be. I think it’s made the internet better, a lot of people to create wonderful websites. And I also think, now you might disagree with this, but from everything I’ve seen, WP Engine just gives me bad vibes.

Lex Fridman
(05:41:46) 我很高兴你划清了一些界限。现在正好可以提一下我认为可能是有史以来最伟大的开源项目之一：WordPress。你在10月24日发表了一篇博客文章，谈到了 WordPress 创始人 Matt Mullenweg 最近的一些事，那篇题为《开源皇室与疯狂君王》的文章非常好，深入探讨了“终身仁慈独裁者”（Benevolent Dictator For Life, BDFL）这一开源项目治理模式。文章的基本观点是，作为 WordPress 的 BDFL，Matt 在与 WP Engine 的这场争斗中有些迷失方向了。我也得说，我真的很喜欢 WordPress，它让我感到快乐。我认为它是开源精神的灯塔，让互联网变得更好，帮助无数人创建了精彩的网站。而且我觉得——你可能会不同意——但从我所看到的一切来看，WP Engine 给我的感觉很不好。

(05:43:03) I think they’re not the good guy in this. I don’t like it. I understand the frustration, I understand all of it, but I don’t think that excuses the behavior. There is a bit of… See this kind of counter to a little bit what you said, which is when you have an open source project of that size, there is a bit of a… When you’re the king of a project of a kingdom that large, there’s a bit of responsibility. Anyway, could you speak maybe, to your empathy of Matt and to your criticism? And maybe paint a path of how he and WordPress can be winning again.

(05:43:03) 我觉得他们在这场纷争中不是好人。我不喜欢他们的做法。我能理解那种挫败感，也理解整件事的来龙去脉，但我不认为这能成为不当行为的借口。这里其实有点……和你刚才说的观点形成一点反差：当你拥有一个如此规模的开源项目时，某种程度上……当你是一个如此庞大“王国”的“国王”时，你就承担着一定的责任。无论如何，你能否谈谈你对 Matt 的共情之处以及你的批评？也许还能描绘一条路径，让他和 WordPress 能重新赢得人心？

DHH
(05:43:52) First, I echo what you said about what a wonderful thing it is that WordPress success, there are not many projects in the open source world or in the world at large that has had as big of an impact on the internet as WordPress has. He deserves a ton of accolades for that work. So that was my engagement, essentially my premise. Do you know what? I had tremendous respect for what Matt has built with WordPress, what that entire ecosystem has built around itself. It’s a true marvel, but there’s some principles that are larger than my personal sympathies to the characters involved. I agree. The Silver Lake private equity company that’s involved with WP Engine is not my natural ally. I’m not the natural ally of private equity doing some game with VP Engine. That’s not my interest in the case. My interest is essentially a set of principles and the principles are if you release something as an open source, people are free to use it as they see fit and they’re free to donate code, or resources, or money back to the community as they see fit.

DHH
(05:43:52) 首先，我完全赞同你对 WordPress 成功的评价——在开源世界乃至整个世界范围内，很少有项目像 WordPress 这样对互联网产生如此巨大的影响。Matt 因此理应获得大量赞誉。这实际上就是我介入此事的前提。你知道吗？我对 Matt 用 WordPress 所建立的一切，以及围绕它形成的整个生态系统，怀有极大的敬意。这确实是个奇迹。但有些原则比我对相关人物的个人同情更重要。我同意，参与 WP Engine 的 Silver Lake 私募股权公司并不是我的天然盟友，我也不是那些与 WP Engine 玩资本游戏的私募股权公司的天然盟友。但这并非我关注的重点。我关心的是一套基本原则：如果你将某样东西以开源形式发布，人们就有权按自己的意愿使用它，并有权按自己的意愿向社区捐赠代码、资源或资金。

(05:45:10) You may disagree about whether they’ve done enough, whether they should do more, but you can’t show up after you’ve given the gift of free software to the world and then say, “Now that you’ve used that gift, you actually owe me a huge slice of your business because you got too successful using the thing I gave you for free.” You don’t get to take a gift back. That’s why we have open source licenses. They stipulate exactly what the obligations are on both sides of the equation. The users of open source don’t get to demand what the makers of open source do and how they act and the makers of open source don’t get to suddenly show up with a ransom note to the users and say, “Actually you owe me for all sorts of use.” I’m 100% allergic to that kind of interaction. And I think Matt unfortunately for whatever reason, got so wrapped up in what he was owed that he failed to realize what he was destroying. WordPress and Automatic already makes a ton of money.

(05:45:10) 你或许可以争论他们是否做得足够、是否应该做更多，但你不能在把免费软件作为礼物送给世界之后，又跑出来说：“既然你用了这份礼物并因此大获成功，那你就欠我一大块你的生意。”你不能收回一份礼物。这正是我们制定开源许可证的原因——它们明确规定了双方各自的责任边界。开源用户无权要求开源作者必须做什么、如何行事；同样，开源作者也不能突然向用户发出勒索信，声称“你其实欠我各种使用费”。我对这种互动方式百分之百反感。我认为 Matt 不幸地陷入了“自己应得什么”的执念之中，以至于没能意识到自己正在破坏什么。WordPress 和 Automattic 早已赚取了巨额利润。

(05:46:19) This is part of the wonder of WordPress. This is a project that generates hundreds of millions of dollars and Matt didn’t feel like he was getting enough of that. That’s not a good argument, bro. You can’t just violate the spirit and the letter of these open source licenses and just start showing up with demand letters even to characters that are not particularly sympathetic. This goes to the root of my interpretation of open source in general. The GPL is a particular license that actually demands code from people who use it under certain circumstances. I’ve never liked the GPL. I don’t want your shitty code. If you don’t want to give it to me, what am I going to do with that? Some code dump that you’ve… I’m not on board with that part of Stallman’s vision at all. I love the MIT license. To me that is the perfect license because it is mercilessly short.

(05:46:19) 这正是 WordPress 奇迹的一部分——这个项目每年创造数亿美元收入，而 Matt 却觉得自己的份额不够。兄弟，这不是个好理由。你不能违背开源许可证的精神和字面规定，甚至对那些并不特别值得同情的对象也发出索款函。这触及了我对开源本质理解的核心。GPL 是一种特殊的许可证，它在某些情况下会强制使用者交出代码。我从来都不喜欢 GPL。我才不想要你那些烂代码。如果你不愿意给我，我拿那些代码垃圾又能干什么？我对斯托曼（Stallman）愿景中的这一部分完全不认同。我钟爱 MIT 许可证，在我看来它才是完美的许可证，因为它极其简短。

(05:47:17) I think it’s two paragraphs, three paragraphs, really short and it basically says, “Here’s some software. It comes with no warranty. You can’t sue me. You can’t demand anything, but you can do whatever the hell you want with it. Have a nice life.” That’s a perfect open source interaction in my opinion, and that license needs to be upheld. These licenses in general, even the GPL, even if I don’t like it, we have to abide by them because if we just set aside those licenses, when we in a moment’s notice feel like something’s slightly unfair, we’ve lost everything. We’ve lost the entire framework that allowed open source to prosper and allowed open source to become such an integral part of commerce too. I mean, back when open source was initially finding its feet, it was at war with commercial software. Stallman is at war with commercial software and always has been.

(05:47:17) 我觉得它就两到三段话，非常简短，基本上意思是：“这是些软件，不提供任何担保。你不能起诉我，也不能对我提任何要求，但你可以随心所欲地使用它。祝你生活愉快。”在我看来，这才是理想的开源互动方式，而这种许可证必须得到维护。总的来说，这些许可证——即便是我不喜欢的 GPL——我们都必须遵守。因为一旦我们仅因一时觉得某事略有不公就随意抛弃这些许可证，我们就失去了一切。我们将失去整个让开源得以繁荣、并使其成为商业世界不可或缺一部分的框架。要知道，早期开源刚起步时，是与商业软件处于战争状态的。斯托曼一直与商业软件为敌，过去如此，现在依然如此。

(05:48:11) Bill Gates was in return at war with open source for the longest time. The open source licenses and the clarity that they provide allowed us to end that war. Today, commercial software and open source software can peacefully coexist. I make commercial software, I sell Basecamp, I sell HEY, and then I also make a bunch of open source software that I give away for free gifts. That can’t happen if we start violating these contracts. No commercial company is going to go, “Let me base my next project off this piece of open source if I’m also running the liability that some Matt maker is going to show up seven years in and demand I give them $50 million.” That’s not an environment conducive to commerce collaboration or anything else and it’s just basically wrong. I think there’s one analysis that’s all about the practical outcomes of this, which I think are bad.

(05:48:11) 比尔·盖茨反过来也长期与开源为敌。正是开源许可证及其提供的清晰规则，让我们结束了这场战争。如今，商业软件和开源软件可以和平共存。我开发商业软件，销售 Basecamp 和 HEY，同时也开发大量开源软件并免费赠予他人。但如果开始违反这些契约，这一切就不可能实现。没有哪家商业公司会愿意说：“让我基于这段开源代码开发下一个项目吧，哪怕七年后某个‘Matt 式’的作者突然出现，要求我支付五千万美元。”这不是一个有利于商业合作或其他任何协作的环境，从根本上讲也是错误的。我认为有一种分析聚焦于这种做法的实际后果，而我认为这些后果非常糟糕。

(05:49:05) There’s also an argument that’s simply about ethics. This is not right. You can’t just show up afterwards and demand something. This is not too dissimilar in my opinion, to the whole Apple thing we talked about earlier, Apple just showing up and feeling like they’re entitled to 30% of everyone’s business. No, that’s not right. That’s not fair. So I think Matt unfortunately steered himself blind on the indignity he thought was being perpetrated against him because there was all this money being made by WP Engine making a good product and not giving quite enough back in Matt’s opinion, tough cookie.

(05:49:05) 此外还有一个纯粹关乎伦理的论点：这样做是不对的。你不能事后突然出现并提出索取要求。在我看来，这和我们之前谈到的苹果事件非常相似——苹果单方面出现，觉得自己理应拿走别人业务收入的30%。不，这不对，也不公平。因此我认为，Matt 不幸地被自己感受到的“不公”蒙蔽了双眼——WP Engine 凭借一款优秀产品赚了大钱，却在 Matt 看来回馈不足。但现实就是如此，他得接受这一点。

Lex Fridman
(05:49:49) I think there, maybe I’m reading too much into it, but there might be some personal stuff too which weren’t only not giving enough but probably implicitly promising that they will give and then taking advantage of him in that way in his mind. Just like interpersonal interaction and then you get interpersonally frustrated.

Lex Fridman
(05:49:49) 我觉得，也许是我过度解读了，但其中可能也掺杂了一些个人因素——不仅仅是回馈不足，可能还曾含蓄地承诺过会给予支持，结果在他看来却被利用了。这就像是人际关系中的互动，最终导致了人际层面的挫败感。

DHH
(05:50:10) I get that.

DHH
(05:50:10) 我理解。

Lex Fridman
(05:50:11) You forget the bigger picture ethics of it. It’s like when a guy keeps promising he’ll do something and then you realize you wake up one day a year or two later, “Wait a minute, I was being lied to this whole time,” and that I don’t even know if it’s about money.

Lex Fridman
(05:50:11) 你会因此忘记更宏观的伦理问题。就像一个人不断承诺会做某事，结果一两年后你突然醒悟：“等等，我一直都被骗了。”而且我甚至不确定这是否真的关乎金钱。
(05:50:29) I’d get mad too. It’s totally fine to get mad when people disappoint you. That’s not justification for upending decades of open source licensees and the essential de facto case law we’ve established around it. This is why I chose to even weigh in on this because I like WordPress. I don’t use WordPress. I’m not a part of that community. I don’t actually have a dog in this fight. I’m biased if anything towards Matt just as a fellow BDFL. I would like to see him do well with this, but I also think there’s some principles that stake here that ring much louder. I don’t want Rails to suddenly be tainted by the fact that it’s open source and whether companies can rely on it and build businesses on it because wait, maybe one day I’m going to turn Matt and I’m going to turn Matt King and I’m going to show up with a demand ransom letter. Now screw that. We have way more to protect here. There’s way more at stake than your personal beef with someone or your perceived grievance over what you’re owed.
(05:50:29) 我也会生气。当别人让你失望时，生气是完全可以理解的。但这并不能成为颠覆几十年来开源许可证体系以及我们围绕它所建立的事实判例的理由。我之所以选择对此发声，是因为我喜欢 WordPress。我不使用 WordPress，也不属于那个社区，实际上我在这件事上并没有切身利益。如果说我有偏见，那也只是因为我和 Matt 一样，都是各自项目的“仁慈独裁者”（BDFL），所以我倾向于支持他。我希望他能在这次事件中取得好的结果，但我也认为，这里涉及的一些原则远比个人情绪更为重要。我不希望 Rails 因其开源属性而突然蒙上污点，让企业开始怀疑是否还能放心地基于它构建业务——万一哪天我也像 Matt 那样，摇身一变成为“Matt King”，然后寄出一封勒索信呢？别开玩笑了。我们在这里要保护的东西远比你对某人的私人恩怨或你自认为应得的补偿重要得多。

Lex Fridman
(05:51:31) What would you recommend? What do you think he should do, can do to walk it back to heal?
Lex Fridman
(05:51:31) 你会建议他怎么做？你觉得他应该、又能做些什么来挽回局面、修复关系？

DHH
(05:51:40) Decide. This is the curious thing. He could decide to give this up. That’s very, very difficult for driven ambitious people to do, to accept that they’re wrong and to give up and lay down their sword. So I had a hope earlier on in this that was possible. I haven’t seen any evidence that Matt is interested in that and I find that deeply regretful, but that’s his prerogative. I continue to speak out when he’s violating the spirit and ethics of open source, but I wish he would just accept that this was a really bad idea. He made a bad bet and I think he thought he’d just get away with it, that they’d just pay up and that he could put pressure.
DHH
(05:51:40) 做个决定吧。这很奇怪：他其实可以选择放弃。但对于那些有强烈驱动力和雄心的人来说，承认自己错了、放下武器、就此作罢，是非常非常困难的。我一开始还抱有一丝希望，觉得这是可能的。但我至今没看到任何迹象表明 Matt 有这个意愿，这让我深感遗憾，不过这终究是他的权利。只要他违背了开源的精神与伦理，我就会继续发声。但我真心希望他能承认，这确实是个糟糕的主意——他下了一个错误的赌注，可能还以为自己能侥幸成功，以为对方会乖乖付钱，自己施加一点压力就行。

(05:52:24) I mean, I know that temptation. When you sit as the head of a very important project, you know that comes with a great degree of power and you really need a great degree of discipline to rein that in and not exercise that power at every step where you feel aggrieved. I’ve felt aggrieved a million times over in the 20 plus years of Ruby on Rails. I’ve really tried very hard not to let those, sometimes petty, sometimes substantial grievances over time seep in to the foundation of the ecosystem and risk ruining everything.
(05:52:24) 我完全理解这种诱惑。当你身处一个极其重要项目的核心位置时，你知道自己拥有巨大的权力，而你也必须具备极强的自律，才能克制住不在每次感到委屈时就动用这份权力。在 Ruby on Rails 超过二十年的发展历程中，我曾无数次感到委屈。但我一直努力不让这些委屈——有时微不足道，有时确实严重——逐渐渗透进整个生态系统的根基，从而危及一切。

Money and happiness

金钱与幸福

Lex Fridman
(05:53:03) As the king of the Rails kingdom. Has the power gotten to your head over the years?
Lex Fridman
(05:53:03) 作为 Rails 王国的国王，这些年来权力有没有冲昏你的头脑？

DHH
(05:53:07) I’m sure it has. I mean, who wouldn’t?
DHH
(05:53:07) 我相信肯定有。毕竟，谁不会呢？

Lex Fridman
(05:53:10) Do you pace around in your chamber? [inaudible 05:53:12]-
Lex Fridman
(05:53:10) 你会不会在房间里踱步？[05:53:12 处声音不清]—

DHH
(05:53:11) I do, occasionally, and I do marvel at both what’s been built, what’s been possible. Over a million applications have been made with Ruby on Rails by one estimate that I’ve seen. Businesses like Shopify and GitHub and a million others have been built on top of something that I started. That’s very gratifying. But you really have to be careful not to smell your own exhaust too much and you have to be just as careful not to listen too much to the haters and not to listen too much to the super fans either that you assess the value and the principles of what you’re working towards on its own merits, on your own scoreboard. I try to block that out and then just go, “Well, I’m working on Rails because I love to write Ruby. I love to use Ruby to make web applications. That’s my North Star and I’ll continue to do that and I’ll continue to share all of the open source gifts that I uncover along the ways,” and that’s it. That’s enough too.
DHH
(05:53:11) 的确偶尔会。我也会惊叹于已经建成的一切和曾经实现的可能。据我所见的一项估算，已有超过一百万个应用程序是用 Ruby on Rails 构建的。像 Shopify、GitHub 以及无数其他公司，都是建立在我最初启动的这个项目之上的。这令人非常满足。但你必须格外小心，不要过度陶醉于自己的成就，也不要过分在意批评者或狂热粉丝的声音。你需要根据事情本身的内在价值和你自己的标准来评估你所追求的目标。我努力屏蔽这些外界噪音，只专注于一点：“我之所以做 Rails，是因为我热爱写 Ruby，热爱用 Ruby 构建 Web 应用。这就是我的北极星。我会继续这么做，并继续分享我在路上发现的所有开源成果。”仅此而已，也已足够。

(05:54:23) I don’t have to get all of it out of it. This is sometimes just as with the guy who thought I’d given up on being Jira or something, instead of doing Basecamp, there are people over the years who’ve asked like, “Why didn’t you charge for Rails? Don’t you know how much money had been made off Rails?” If we just look at something like Shopify, it’s worth billions of dollars. I’m not a billionaire and so freaking what? I got more than enough. I got plenty of my share.
(05:54:23) 我并不需要从中获取一切。就像有些人以为我放弃了成为“Jira”之类的产品，转而做了 Basecamp 一样，多年来总有人问我：“你为什么不对 Rails 收费？难道你不知道别人靠 Rails 赚了多少钱吗？”光看 Shopify 这一家公司，市值就达数十亿美元。我不是亿万富翁，那又怎样？我已经得到了远超所需的回报，分到的份额已经绰绰有余。

(05:54:51) I will say though, I’m also introspective enough to realize that if it hadn’t panned out as well as it did for me on my own business, maybe I would’ve been more tempted. Maybe if you see other people build huge successful companies off the back of your work and you really don’t have a pot to piss in, you might be tempted to get a little upset about that. I’ve seen that in the Rails world as well, where there are people who contributed substantial bodies of work and then got really miffed when they didn’t feel like they got enough back. I was fortunate enough that the business that Jason and I built with Ruby on Rails was as successful as it was and I made the money I needed to make that I didn’t need to chase the rest of it.
(05:54:51) 不过我也足够自省，意识到如果我自己创办的公司没有取得如此成功，我或许也会更容易受到诱惑。如果你看到别人基于你的工作建立起庞大的成功企业，而你自己却一贫如洗，难免会有些愤懑。我在 Rails 社区也见过类似情况：一些人贡献了大量代码，却因觉得自己没得到应有的回报而非常不满。我很幸运，Jason 和我用 Ruby on Rails 打造的事业取得了巨大成功，我赚到了所需的钱，因此无需再去追逐剩下的那些。

Lex Fridman
(05:55:36) But we should also just make explicit that many people in your position chase the money. It’s not that difficult to chase. Basically you turned away money, you made a lot of decisions that just turned away money.
Lex Fridman
(05:55:36) 但我们也要明确一点：很多处在你位置上的人会去追逐金钱。追逐金钱其实并不难。而你基本上是主动拒绝了金钱，做了许多放弃赚钱机会的决定。

DHH
(05:55:53) Maybe. I also think of this example with Matt. He probably thought there was easy money for the taking and it wasn’t so easy, was it? It looked like low-hanging dollar bills and they turned out to be some really sour grapes. It turned out he probably destroyed vast sums of money by undermining the whole WordPress trust and the ecosystem and putting question marks in the heads of folks who would choose to use WordPress or something else going forward. So I often think when people think like, “Oh, you left money on the table.” First of all, so what? I don’t have to have all the money, but second of all, maybe the money wasn’t on the table at all.
DHH
(05:55:53) 也许吧。我也想到 Matt 的例子。他可能以为有轻松的钱可赚，但事实并非如此，对吧？那些看似唾手可得的美元钞票，结果却成了酸葡萄。他很可能因为破坏了整个 WordPress 的信任基础和生态系统，让未来考虑使用 WordPress 或其他工具的人心生疑虑，反而毁掉了巨额财富。所以我常常想，当人们说“哦，你把钱留在桌上了”时，首先，那又怎样？我不需要拥有所有的钱；其次，也许那笔钱根本就不存在。

Lex Fridman
(05:56:33) And maybe the cost, even if you got the money, maybe the cost in other ways like we’ve talked about, would outweigh all the money that you could have possibly gotten. I think you said that the thing that makes you happy is flow and tranquility. Those two things. Really beautifully put. And gaining money might assign to your responsibility of running a larger thing that takes away the flow that you gain from being… Fundamentally for you what flow means is programming and then tranquility is like… I think you also have a beautiful post of like, “Nirvana is an empty schedule.”
Lex Fridman
(05:56:33) 而且即便你拿到了钱，其所带来的代价——正如我们之前谈到的——可能远远超过你所能获得的全部金钱收益。我记得你说过，让你感到幸福的是“心流”和“宁静”这两样东西，说得真美。而获取金钱可能会带来管理更大规模事务的责任，从而剥夺你从……中获得的心流。对你而言，“心流”的本质就是编程，而“宁静”则像是……我记得你还写过一篇很美的文章，说“涅槃就是一张空白的日程表”。

DHH
(05:57:17) When I look at a upcoming week and I see that I have no scheduled meetings at all, which is quite common, or maybe I just have one thing for one hour on one day, I think to myself, “Do you know what? This could very easily have been very different. We could have been running a company of 100s of people or 1000s of people and my entire calendar would’ve been packed solid with little Tetris blocks of other people’s demands on my attention and time and I would’ve been miserable as fuck. And I look at that and go, “What more can I ask for?” Which is a really nice state of being, I’d actually say. I didn’t have this always. I did have, early on in my career, some sense of I need a little more, a little more security. And I remember this really interesting study where a bunch of researchers asked people who had made certain amounts of money, “How much money would it take for you to feel secure?”
DHH
(05:57:17) 每当我看到接下来的一周完全没有安排会议——这其实很常见，或者最多只有一天安排了一个小时的事情——我就会对自己说：“你知道吗？这一切本可以完全不同。我们本可以运营一家几百人甚至上千人的公司，我的日历会被密密麻麻的小方块填满，全是别人对我时间和注意力的要求，那我会痛苦得要死。”看着现在这样，我会问自己：“我还能奢求什么？”说实话，这是一种非常美好的状态。我并非一直如此。职业生涯早期，我也曾觉得还需要更多一点、再多一点安全感。我记得有一项特别有意思的研究：研究人员问那些已经拥有一定财富的人，“你需要多少钱才会感到安全？”

(05:58:14) They’d ask people who had a million dollars net worth, “How much money do you need?” “Probably need $2 million. $2 million, then I’d be good.” Then they asked people with a net worth of $5 million, how much do you need?” “10. I need 10.” Ask people with $10 million, “What do you need?” “20.” Every single time people would need double of what they did. I did that for a couple of doublings until I realized, “You know what? This is silly. I’m already where I wished I would be and a million times over, so what less is there to pursue?” Now that doesn’t mean that if more money is coming my way, I’m going to say no to it. Of course not. But, it does mean that I’m free to set other things higher. And I also do think you realize, as Jim Carrey would say, “I wish everyone would get all the money that they wished for and they’d realize it wasn’t the answer.”
(05:58:14) 他们问净资产100万美元的人：“你需要多少钱？”回答是：“大概200万吧，有200万我就安心了。”接着问净资产500万美元的人，需要多少？“1000万，我需要1000万。”再问拥有1000万美元的人，需要多少？“2000万。”每次人们都觉得需要翻倍才够。我自己也经历了几次这样的翻倍，直到某天突然意识到：“你知道吗？这太荒谬了。我早已达到了自己曾经梦想的位置，而且远超千百倍，还有什么值得再去追逐的呢？”当然，这并不意味着如果有更多钱送上门我会拒绝——当然不会。但这意味着我可以自由地把其他东西看得比金钱更重要。我也确实相信，正如金·凯瑞（Jim Carrey）所说：“我希望每个人都能得到他们渴望的所有金钱，然后意识到那并不是答案。”

(05:59:01) That money solves a whole host of problems and anxieties and then it creates a bunch of new ones and then it also doesn’t touch a huge swath of the human experience at all. The world is full of miserable, anxious, hurt, rich people. It’s also full of miserable, anxious, poor people and I’d rather be a miserable, anxious, rich person than a poor person. But it isn’t this magic wand that make everything go away, and that’s again one of those insights, just like having children, that you cannot communicate in words. I’ve never been able to persuade a person who’s not wealthy that wealth wasn’t going to solve all their problems.

（05:59:01）金钱能解决一大堆问题和焦虑，但同时又会制造出许多新问题，而且对人类经验中很大一部分根本毫无触及。世界上充满了痛苦、焦虑、受伤的富人，也充满了痛苦、焦虑的穷人。我宁愿做一个痛苦、焦虑的富人，也不愿做穷人。但金钱并不是一根能让一切烟消云散的魔法棒——这又是一种无法用语言传达的领悟，就像养育孩子一样。我从未成功说服过一个不富裕的人：财富并不能解决他们所有的问题。

Lex Fridman
(05:59:42) One quote you’ve returned to often that I enjoy a lot is the Coco Chanel quote of, “The best things in life are free and the second-best things are very, very expensive.” And I guess the task is to focus on surrounding yourself with the best things in life like family and all of this and not caring about the other stuff.

Lex Fridman
（05:59:42）你经常引用、我也非常喜欢的一句可可·香奈儿的话是：“生活中最好的东西是免费的，次好的东西则非常、非常昂贵。”我想，我们的任务就是专注于围绕在自己身边的那些生命中最美好的事物，比如家人等等，而不要过分在意其他东西。

DHH
(06:00:07) I would easily say you can care about the other stuff. Just know the order of priority. If you are blessed with a partner that you love, some children that you adore, you’ve already won the greatest prize that most humans are able to achieve. Most humans in this world, if they are of marital age and they have children, if you ask them what’s the most important thing they would all say that, they would all say that, no matter whether they’re rich or poor. It’s easy to lose sight of that when you’re chasing the second-best things because do you know what? They’re also very nice.

DHH
（06:00:07）我很容易就能说，你当然可以关心那些“其他东西”，但要清楚优先级的顺序。如果你有幸拥有一个你深爱的伴侣、一些你疼爱的孩子，那你已经赢得了大多数人所能获得的最伟大的奖赏。这个世界上大多数处于适婚年龄且已有子女的人，无论贫富，如果你问他们什么最重要，他们都会给出同样的答案。当你追逐那些“次好的东西”时，很容易忽视这一点——但你知道吗？那些东西确实也很美好。

(06:00:45) I really like that Pagani Sonda. It was a very expensive car and I would’ve had no chance of acquiring it if I hadn’t become rather successful in business. So I don’t want to dismiss it either. It’s great fun to have money. It’s just not as fun for quite as long or as deep as you think it is. And these other things, having an occupation and a pursuit that you enjoy, being able to carry burdens with a stiff upper lip and with again, a sense of meaning, is incredible. To have family, to have friends, to have hobbies, to have all these things that are actually available to most people around the world, that’s winning. And it doesn’t mean you have to discount your ambitions. It doesn’t mean you can’t reach for more, but it does mean it’s pretty dumb if you don’t realize that it’s not going to complete you in some hocus-pocus woo sense to make more. It really isn’t.

（06:00:45）我真的很喜欢那辆帕加尼Zonda。那是一辆非常昂贵的车，如果我在事业上没有取得相当的成功，根本不可能拥有它。所以我也不想完全否定它。有钱确实很有趣，只是这种乐趣持续的时间和深度并不如你想象的那么久、那么深。而其他那些事情——拥有一份你喜欢的职业和追求，能够咬紧牙关、带着意义感去承担重负，才是真正的了不起。拥有家人、朋友、爱好，拥有这些全世界大多数人都能获得的东西，这才是真正的赢。这并不意味着你要放弃雄心，也不意味着你不能追求更多，但这确实意味着：如果你天真地以为赚更多钱就能以某种玄妙的方式让你变得完整，那就太愚蠢了。事实真的不是那样。

Hope

希望

Lex Fridman
(06:01:56) What gives you hope about the future of this whole thing we have going on here, human civilization?

Lex Fridman
（06:01:56）是什么让你对人类文明的未来抱有希望？

DHH
(06:02:04) I find it easier to be optimistic than pessimistic because I don’t know either way. So if I get to choose, why not just choose to believe it’s going to pan out? “We suffer more in our imagination than we do in reality,” that’s one of the quotes out of Stoicism. And I also think we have a tendency, a lot of humans have a tendency to be pessimistic in advance for things they don’t know how it’s going to pan out. Climate change, for example, is making a lot of people very anxious and very pessimistic about the future. You know nothing. 40 years ago, we thought the problem was that the planet was going to be too cool. I happen to believe that it’s probably correct that the planet is getting too hot and that CO2 has something to do with it. Whether we have the right measures to fix it in time, if that’s even possible or not, is completely up in the air and we don’t know.

DHH
（06:02:04）我发现乐观比悲观更容易，因为无论哪种我都无法确定。既然我能选择，为什么不干脆选择相信事情会好起来呢？“我们在想象中所受的苦，远多于现实中所受的苦”——这是斯多葛学派的一句名言。我也认为，很多人有一种倾向：在事情结果未知之前就提前悲观。比如气候变化，让很多人对未来感到极度焦虑和悲观。但其实你一无所知。40年前，我们还以为地球会变得太冷。我个人倾向于相信，现在地球确实在变暖，而且二氧化碳与此有关。但我们是否有正确的措施及时应对——甚至这是否可能——完全悬而未决，我们并不知道。

(06:03:03) If you convince yourself with such certainty that the world is going to turn to shit. It is, right up here in your head, today. Climate change might wipe out this entire species in 200 years. It’s not next year. It’s not 10 years from now. Life might become more unpleasant and there might be more negative effects and so on. Yes, okay, but then deal with that hardship when it arrives. Don’t take that in advance. How are you helping earth by just walking around being depressed?

（06:03:03）如果你如此确信世界将走向毁灭，那么对你来说，今天此刻它就已经在你的脑子里毁灭了。气候变化或许会在200年后灭绝整个人类，但那不是明年，也不是十年后。生活可能会变得更不愉快，可能出现更多负面影响，没错，可以接受。但等困难真正到来时再去应对它，不要提前预支痛苦。你整天垂头丧气地四处走动，对地球又有什么帮助呢？

Lex Fridman
(06:03:36) I think our whole conversation today is also an indication, it’s just two humans talking. There’s billions of us and there is something about us that wants to solve problems and build cool stuff and so we’re going to build our way out of whatever shit we get ourselves into. This is what humans do. We create problems for ourselves and figure out how to build rocket ships to get out of those problems. And sometimes, the rocket ships create other problems like nuclear warheads and then we’ll, I hope, figure out ways how to avoid those problems. And then, there’ll be nanobots and then the aliens will come and it’ll be a massive war between the nanobots and the aliens and that will bring all of us humans together.

Lex Fridman
（06:03:36）我觉得我们今天的整个对话本身就是一个例证——这只是两个普通人在交谈。世界上有数十亿人，而我们身上有种特质：渴望解决问题、创造酷炫的东西。因此，无论我们把自己陷入什么样的烂摊子，我们终将靠建造来摆脱困境。这就是人类做的事：我们给自己制造问题，然后想办法造出火箭飞船逃离这些问题。有时候，这些火箭飞船又会带来新问题，比如核弹头；但我希望，我们最终能找到办法避免那些问题。接着会出现纳米机器人，外星人也会到来，然后纳米机器人和外星人之间将爆发一场大规模战争——而这反而会让全人类团结起来。

DHH
(06:04:24) The funny thing, just to pick up one of the points you mentioned, the atom bomb, for example. When that was first invented, a lot of people thought we have essentially ended life on earth or maybe we prevented World War III from happening in the past 80 years because assured, neutral annihilation kept the superpowers from attacking each other at least head-on and kept their fighting to proxy wars. You know what? Proxy wars are not great, but they’re probably better than World War III with nuclear weapons. So it’s quite difficult in the moment to tell what’s actually benefit and what’s not, and I think we should be a bit more humble. I’ve certainly become more humble over time of thinking I know which way it’s going to turn. I think the pandemic was a huge moment for a lot of people where there was so much certainty about whether this intervention worked or that intervention didn’t work and most people were wrong.

DHH
（06:04:24）有趣的是，就拿你提到的一个例子来说吧，比如原子弹。当初它刚被发明时，很多人认为人类基本上已经终结了地球上的生命；但或许恰恰相反，在过去80年里，正是“确保相互毁灭”的威慑阻止了第三次世界大战的爆发——超级大国至少没有直接正面开战，而是把冲突限制在代理人战争层面。你知道吗？代理人战争当然不好，但可能总比使用核武器的第三次世界大战要好。因此，在当下很难判断某件事究竟是利是弊，我认为我们应该更谦逊一些。随着时间推移，我确实变得更加谦逊，不再自以为知道事情会如何发展。我认为疫情对很多人来说都是一个重大时刻：当时人们对自己认定的某些干预措施是否有效表现出极强的确定性，但大多数人其实是错的。

(06:05:25) Certainly a lot of very smart people, very qualified people got that just utterly and catastrophyingly wrong. So just a little intellectual humility, I think back upon that and go like, “You know what? I’m not a PhD in virology,” and I don’t claim that I somehow saw how it always going to play out, but the people who were really experts in it, they’ve got a bunch of it wrong. Nobody knows anything. I keep reminding myself of that every day. No one knows anything. We can’t predict the economy a month out. We can’t predict world affairs a month… The world is just too complicated.

（06:05:25）的确，许多非常聪明、非常有资历的人在这件事上犯了彻底而灾难性的错误。所以，保持一点智识上的谦逊吧。我回想起那段时光就会想：“你知道吗？我又不是病毒学博士”，我从不声称自己能预知事情会如何发展；但就连那些真正的专家，也搞错了很多事。没人真正知道任何事。我每天都提醒自己这一点：没人知道任何事。我们连一个月后的经济都无法预测，更别说一个月后的国际局势……这个世界实在太复杂了。

Lex Fridman
(06:06:03) When I watched the Netflix documentary, Chimp Empire, and how there’s a hierarchy of chimps, all of that looks eerily similar to us humans. We’re recent descendants. So these experts, some of the chimps got a PhD, others don’t. Others are really muscular. Others are beta male kind. They’re sucking up to the alpha. There’s a lot of interesting dynamics going on that really maps cleanly to the geopolitics of the day. They don’t have nuclear weapons, but the nature of their behavior is similar to ours. So I think we barely know what’s going on, but do think there’s a basic will to cooperate as a basic compassion that underlies just the human spirit that’s there. And maybe that is just me being optimistic, but if that is indeed there, then we’re going to be okay.

Lex Fridman
（06:06:03）当我看了Netflix的纪录片《黑猩猩帝国》，看到黑猩猩社会中的等级制度时，所有这些都与我们人类惊人地相似。毕竟我们是近亲。那些“专家”里，有些黑猩猩像是拿了博士学位，有些没有；有些肌肉发达；有些则是典型的“贝塔雄性”，拼命讨好“阿尔法”。其中有许多有趣的动态，简直完美映射到当今的地缘政治。它们虽然没有核武器，但行为本质与我们相似。所以我认为，我们几乎不清楚到底发生了什么，但我确实相信，人类精神深处有一种基本的合作意愿和基本的同理心。也许这只是我的乐观主义，但如果这种东西真的存在，那我们就终将安然无恙。

DHH
(06:07:03) The capacity is certainly there. Whether we choose that capacity or not, who knows and in what situation. I think accepting that we all have the capacity for both ways, for both incredible generosity and kindness and also cruelty. I think, Young, with this whole theory of the shadow was really spot-on that we all have that capacity in us and accepting that it’s our job to attempt to cultivate the better parts of our human nature is weighed against our propensity to some time be the worst of ourselves.

DHH
（06:07:03）这种能力无疑是存在的。但我们是否会运用它？在何种情境下运用？谁也不知道。我认为，我们必须承认：每个人都有两种潜能——既能展现出非凡的慷慨与善意，也能表现出残忍。荣格关于“阴影”的整套理论说得非常到位：我们内心都具备这两种潜能。而我们的责任，就是在与自身堕落倾向的对抗中，努力培育人性中更美好的部分。

Lex Fridman
(06:07:41) I’m excited to find out what’s going to happen. It’s so awesome to be human. I don’t want to die. I want to be alive for a while to see all the cool shit we do. And one of the cool things I want to see is all the software you create and all the things you tweet, all the trouble you get yourself into on Twitter. David, I’m a huge fan. Like I said, thank you for everything you’ve done for the world, for the millions of developers you’ve inspired and one of whom is me, and thank you for this awesome conversation, brother.

Lex Fridman
（06:07:41）我很期待看看接下来会发生什么。做人真是太棒了。我不想死，我想多活一阵子，亲眼见证我们人类创造出的所有酷炫玩意儿。而我想看的酷事之一，就是你开发的所有软件、你发的每一条推文，还有你在推特上惹出的各种麻烦。大卫，我是你的超级粉丝。正如我之前所说，感谢你为这个世界所做的一切，感谢你激励了数百万开发者——我就是其中之一。也谢谢你带来这场精彩的对话，兄弟。

DHH
(06:08:11) Thanks so much for having me.

DHH
（06:08:11）非常感谢邀请我。

(06:08:14) Thanks for listening to this conversation with DHH. To support this podcast, please check out our sponsors in the description and consider subscribing to this channel. And now, let me leave you with some words from Rework by DHH and Jason Fried, “What you do is what matters, not what you think, or say, or plan.” Thank you for listening and hope to see you next time.

(06:08:14) 感谢您收听本期与 DHH 的对话。为了支持本播客，请查看描述中的赞助商信息，并考虑订阅本频道。最后，让我用 DHH 与 Jason Fried 在《重来》（Rework）一书中的一句话作为结尾：“重要的是你做了什么，而不是你想了什么、说了什么或计划了什么。”感谢您的收听，期待下次再见。
